    "tid": 26318025,
    "publishdate": "2015-04-02",
    "title": "Md. Ruknuddin vs The Hon'Ble High Court At Calcutta & Ors on 2 April, 2015",
    "doc": "<h2 class=\"doc_title\">Md. Ruknuddin vs The Hon&#x27;Ble High Court At Calcutta &amp; Ors on 2 April, 2015</h2>\n\n<h3 class=\"doc_author\">Author: <a href=\"/search/?formInput=authorid:n-mhatre\">Nishita Mhatre</a></h3>\n\n<h3 class=\"doc_bench\">Bench: <a href=\"/search/?formInput=benchid:n-mhatre\">Nishita Mhatre</a></h3>\n\n<pre id=\"pre_1\">              IN THE HIGH COURT AT CALCUTTA\n               CIVIL APPELLATE JURISDICTION\n                      APPELLATE SIDE\n\nPRESENT:\n\nThe Hon&#x27;ble Justice Nishita Mhatre\n         And\nThe Hon&#x27;ble Justice R. K. Bag\n\n                       M.A.T. No. 2097 of 2014\n                                With\n                        C.A.N. 11742 of 2014\n\n                            Md. Ruknuddin\n                                                ... Appellant\n                                   vs.\n              The Hon&#x27;ble High Court at Calcutta &amp; Ors.\n                                              ... Respondents\n\nFor the Appellant             : Mr. M. M. Verma\n                                Mr. Abhisekh Verma\n\nFor the Respondent\nNos. 1 &amp; 2                    : Mr. Saptangshu Basu\n                                Ms. Ananya Das\n\nFor the Respondent No.5       : Mr. Tarique Quasimuddin\n                                Ms. Zainab Tahur\n\nHeard on                      : 26.03.2015\n\nJudgment on                   : 02.04.2015\n\n\n\nNishita Mhatre, J.:\n</pre>\n<p data-structure=\"Facts\" id=\"p_1\">1.    The present appeal is directed against the order of the learned\n\nSingle Judge in WP No. 29139(W) of 2014 dated 13th November, 2014.\n\nThe appellant is a Civil Judge (Junior Division) and at the relevant time\n\nwas posted at Gangarampur/Buniadpur in the district of Dakshin\n\nDinajpur.   The allegation against the appellant is that he opened a\n Facebook account in the name of his colleague, a Judicial Magistrate at\n\nGangarampur. He, without her consent, posted her photographs with an\n\nintention to harass and defame her.       A complaint was received from\n\nJudicial Magistrate by the High Court on its administrative side.       It\n\nappears that the Judicial Magistrate, Gangarampur, lodged a complaint\n\nwith the Banshihari Police Station, Dakshin Dinajpur being FIR No.194\n\nof 2012 dated 20th September, 2012. The case was investigated by the\n\nCyber Crime Cell, CID, West Bengal. After investigation, a charge-sheet\n\nwas submitted by the Investigating Officer before the competent Court on\n\n15th January, 2014. The charge framed was under <a href=\"/doc/127128852/\" id=\"a_1\">Section 66A(b)</a> of the\n\nInformation Technology Act, 2000.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_2\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Facts\" id=\"p_3\">2.    The Administrative Committee of this Court resolved on 26th\n\nAugust, 2014 that a disciplinary enquiry should be initiated against the\n\nappellant for harassing/annoying a lady Judicial Officer. The decision of\n\nthe Administrative Committee was ratified by the Full Court on 5th\n\nSeptember, 2014.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_4\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Facts\" id=\"p_5\">3.    On 18th September, 2014 the appellant was served with a copy of\n\nthe order of suspension, the memorandum of charge with imputations\n\nand a letter indicating that the Registrar (Listing) of the Calcutta High\n\nCourt had been appointed as Enquiry Officer with the Registrar\n\n(Vigilance and Protocol), High Court at Calcutta as the Presenting Officer\n\nto enquire into the charge levelled against the appellant.\n</p><p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_6\"> 4.     The appellant was informed that he was required to submit his\n\nwritten statement under Rule 11(4) of the West Bengal Judicial Service\n\n(Classification, Control and Appeal) Rules, 2007 [hereinafter referred to\n\nas &quot;the Rules&quot;] within 30 days from the receipt of the memorandum of\n\ncharge. He was also expected to indicate whether he desired to be heard\n\nin person.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_7\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_8\">5.     The appellant submitted his reply/written statement on 17th\n\nOctober, 2014.     In his written statement he answered the charges\n\nlevelled against him in the charge-sheet.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_9\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_10\">6.     The criminal case started against the appellant resulted in his\n\nacquittal on the basis of a statement made by the complainant. By a\n\njudgment dated 23rd July, 2014, the appellant was acquitted of the\n\ncharge punishable under <a href=\"/doc/127128852/\" id=\"a_2\">Section 66A(b)</a> of the Information Technology\n\nAct.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_11\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_12\">7.     The decision to initiate disciplinary action against the appellant\n\nwas taken only after he had been acquitted by the criminal Court.\n\nWithout waiting for the disciplinary authority to take any action in\n\nrespect of the reply submitted by him, the petitioner preferred a writ\n\npetition being WP No. 29139(W) of 2014 on 29th October, 2014. In his\n\nwrit petition, the appellant prayed for the withdrawal or revocation of the\n\norders dated 18th September, 2014 issued to him by the High Court\n\nthrough the Registrar (Judicial Service) which were the order of\n suspension, memorandum of charge together with imputation and the\n\nletter appointing an enquiry officer.   He also prayed for a stay of the\n\naforesaid orders.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_13\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_14\">8.    By the decision of 13th November, 2014 which is impugned in the\n\npresent appeal, the learned Single Judge dismissed the writ petition as\n\nhe was of the opinion that there was no need to quash the disciplinary\n\nproceedings at that stage. The appellant was granted liberty to renew his\n\nprayer for revocation of the order of suspension before the appropriate\n\nauthority. It appears from the order of the learned Judge that the main\n\ncontentions raised on behalf of the petitioner were (i) that he was\n\ncontinued on suspension despite the fact that he had been acquitted by\n\nthe criminal court, and (ii) that the departmental proceedings would be\n\nan empty formality as he had already been acquitted by the criminal\n\ncourt in respect of same charge which was to be enquired into by the\n\nEnquiry Officer.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_15\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_16\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"PARTY\" data-docid=\"110813550\" id=\"span_1\">9.    The learned Counsel for the appellant has submitted before us that\n\nthe issuance of the charge-sheet and the order appointing an Enquiry\n\nOfficer simultaneously is contrary to and in breach of the provisions of\n\nthe Rules of 2007. He pointed out that under Rule 11, the procedure for\n\nimposing major penalties on a Judicial Officer has been prescribed from\n\nwhich there could be no deviation. He urged that under Rule 11(4), the\n\nappellant has a right to have his written statement of his defence to the\n\ncharge-sheet issued to him considered by the disciplinary authority\n before the disciplinary authority appoints an Enquiry Officer.       The\n\nlearned Counsel submitted therefore that by not waiting to read the reply\n\nof the appellant to the charge-sheet and appointing an Enquiry Officer\n\nsimultaneously, the disciplinary authority has breached the Rules of\n\n2007 and therefore the enquiry proposed to be held against the appellant\n\nmust be quashed.      The learned Counsel also submitted that the\n\nappellant had been acquitted of the charge under <a href=\"/doc/127128852/\" id=\"a_3\">Section 66A(b)</a> of the\n\nInformation Technology Act and consequently the charge-sheet issued by\n\nthe disciplinary authority ought to be withdrawn. The learned Counsel\n\npointed out that <a href=\"/doc/127128852/\" id=\"a_4\">Section 66A(b)</a> has now been held ultra vires by the\n\nSupreme Court in its judgement dated 24th March, 2015 in the case of\n\n<a href=\"/doc/110813550/\" id=\"a_5\">Shreya Singhal v. Union of India</a> in Writ Petition (Criminal) No. 167 of\n\n2012 along with other writ petitions.      A charge-sheet based on a\n\nprovision of law which has been held to be ultra vires must be quashed\n\nby this Court, urged the learned Counsel. He submitted further that the\n\nappellant has been under suspension from 18th September, 2014\n\nwhereas the Supreme Court has deprecated such long suspensions. He,\n\ntherefore, submitted that suspension order must be revoked.</span>\n\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_17\">10.   Mr. Saptangshu Basu, learned Counsel for the Respondent\n\nNo.1/High Court submitted that the charge-sheet is not merely based on\n\nthe provisions of <a href=\"/doc/789969/\" id=\"a_6\">Section 66A(b)</a> of the IT Act and therefore, the enquiry\n\ncan continue with respect to the other charges.    The learned Counsel\n\nfurther submitted that the appellant has been unable to point out any\n\nprejudice which has been caused by appointing an Enquiry Officer\n simultaneously with the issuance of the charge-sheet to him.             He\n\nsubmitted that the disciplinary authority had considered the reply of the\n\nappellant and had sent the same to the Enquiry Officer. The Enquiry\n\nOfficer would naturally consider that reply before concluding the enquiry,\n\naccording to the learned Counsel. As regards the revocation of the order\n\nof suspension, the learned Counsel pointed out that the learned Single\n\nJudge has granted liberty to the petitioner to renew his prayer for the\n\nrevocation of the order of suspension before the appropriate authority\n\nand therefore, this Court need not consider that aspect of the case at all.\n\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_18\">11.   It would be appropriate to set out certain provisions of the Rules\n\nwhich are relevant for deciding the present case. These provisions are as\n\nfollows:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_1\">            11. Procedure for imposing major penalties.-\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_2\">            (1) No order imposing any of the penalties\n            specified in items (i) to (v) of clause (b)\n            of rule 10, shall be made except after an\n            enquiry     held,   as   far    as    may   be,   in   the\n            manner provided in this rule.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_3\">            (2) The Disciplinary authority shall draw up\n            or cause to be drawn up -\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_4\">            (a)   the    substance     of   the    imputations     of\n            misconduct or misbehaviour into definite and\n            distinct articles of charge;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_5\">            (b) a statement of imputations of misconduct\n            or misbehaviour in support of each articles\n            of charge which shall contain -\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_6\"> (i) a statement of relevant facts including\nany    admission         or    confession       made       by    the\nJudicial Officer;\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_7\">(ii) a list of documents by which and a list\nof witnesses by whom, the articles of charge\nare proposed to be substantiated.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_8\">(3) The Disciplinary authority shall deliver\nor    cause    to    be    delivered       to   the    Judicial\nOfficer a copy of the articles of charge and\nthe statement of imputations of misconduct\nor misbehaviour prepared under clause (b) of\nsub-rule (2) and shall require the Judicial\nOfficer       to     submit         to    the    disciplinary\nauthority within 30 (thirty) days a written\nstatement       of       his    defence      and      to        state\nwhether he desires to be heard in person.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_9\">(4)    Where       the    Judicial       Officer   submits         a\nwritten       statement        of   his   defence      and       the\nDisciplinary authority, having regard to the\narticles of charge and the written statement\nof defence thereto, is of the opinion that -\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_10\">(a) any of the penalties specified in items\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_11\">(i) to (iii) of clause (a) of rule 10 shall\nbe imposed, it may pass appropriate orders\nin the case;\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_12\">(b) in any other penalties specified in rule\n10, the Disciplinary authority shall appoint\nan enquiring Officer, not being below the\nrank of a District Judge or an Additional\n            District Judge for the purpose of holding an\n           enquiry into the charges and forward to it-\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_13\">           (i) a copy of the articles of charge and the\n           statement of imputations or misbehaviour,\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_14\">           (ii)   a   copy       of   the    written      statement       of\n           defence;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_15\">           (iii) copies of the statement of witnesses,\n           if any.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_16\">           (5) ...............\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_17\">           to\n           (20) ...............\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_18\">           16.    Appeal     -    (1)       An    aggrieved       Judicial\n           Officer     may        prefer         appeal    before        the\n           Governor     against       any        order    made    by     the\n           Disciplinary      authority           including       an    order\n           imposing any of the penalties specified in\n           items (i) to (v) of clause (b) of rule 10.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_19\">           (2) ...........................\n           to\n           (6) ..............................\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_20\">12.   Rule 16 makes it clear that the aggrieved Judicial Officer can\n\nprefer an appeal before the Governor in respect of any order passed by\n\nthe disciplinary authority. Thus, if the appellant was aggrieved by the\n\nissuance of the charge-sheet simultaneously with the appointment of the\n\nEnquiry Officer, the appellant ought to have challenged those orders in\n\nappeal before the Governor.       Instead of exhausting that remedy the\n appellant approached this Court directly by filing a writ under <a href=\"/doc/1712542/\" id=\"a_7\">Article\n\n226</a> of the Constitution of India.     Unfortunately, this Rule does not\n\nappear to have been pointed out to the learned Single Judge when the\n\nimpugned order was passed as we are sure that the petition would not\n\nhave been entertained in view of the alternate remedy being available to\n\nthe appellant.\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_19\">13.   However, as the petition was entertained and an order was passed\n\nthereon, we will proceed to consider whether the impugned order has\n\nbeen justifiably passed.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_20\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_21\">14.   As seen from the provision of Rule 11(1), the enquiry is to be held\n\nin respect of the charges under Clause (b) of Rule 10 which constitute\n\nmajor penalties only after an enquiry is held &quot;as far as may be in the\n\nmanner provided in this rule&quot;.    Obviously therefore the procedure <a href=\"/doc/110813550/\" id=\"a_8\">laid\n\ndown in</a> this Rule is not sacrosanct but would have to be adhered to as\n\nfar as possible in order to comply with the rules of natural justice while\n\ndealing with the misbehaviour or misconduct of a Judicial Officer. It is\n\ntrue that the Judicial Officer is expected to be given 30 days to submit a\n\nwritten statement of defence and to state whether he desires to be heard\n\nin person after he has been delivered a copy of the articles of charge and\n\nthe statement of imputation or misconduct or misbehaviour prepared\n\nunder Clause (b) of Sub-Rule (2) of Rule 11.      If after considering the\n\nwritten statement of defence, the disciplinary authority is of the opinion\n\nthat any minor penalty specified in Clauses (i) to (iii) of Sub-Rule (a) of\n Rule 10 is to be imposed, the disciplinary authority may pass an\n\nappropriate order.   These minor penalties are censure, withholding of\n\nincrement of pay or promotion, recovery from pay of the whole or part of\n\nany pecuniary loss caused to the State and/or properties in the custody\n\nor under the control of the Court concerned by negligence or breach of\n\nrules or orders provided that the loss or damage is quantified and\n\ncharged for. It is apparent that these punishments would be imposed\n\nwhen the misconduct alleged against the delinquent is not grave or\n\nserious. Clause (b) of Sub-Rule (4) of Rule 11 stipulates that in respect\n\nof other penalties specified in Rule 10 which means major penalties, the\n\ndisciplinary authority shall appoint an enquiry officer not below the rank\n\nof a District Judge or an Additional District Judge for the purpose of\n\nholding an enquiry into the charges.        Considering the charge levelled\n\nagainst the appellant, the disciplinary authority found it to be a case\n\nwhere a major penalty would have to be imposed if the appellant was\n\nfound guilty of the charge levelled against him.             It is in these\n\ncircumstances     that   the   Enquiry      Officer   has   been   appointed\n\nsimultaneously with the issuance of the charge-sheet.         However, this\n\ndoes not mean that the written statement in defence filed by the\n\nappellant will not be considered by the Enquiry Officer while arriving at a\n\nconclusion or by the disciplinary authority in order to ascertain whether\n\nany punishment should be imposed if the Enquiry Officer has found the\n\ndelinquent Judicial Officer to be guilty.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_22\"> 15.   In our opinion, there is no need to quash the enquiry at this stage.\n\nThe appellant has not been able to demonstrate the prejudice which has\n\nbeen caused to him by this procedure being adopted. It would be open\n\nfor him to show that he has been prejudiced by the aforesaid procedure\n\nbeing followed when the enquiry is conducted.        This aspect will be\n\nconsidered by the Enquiry Officer while concluding the enquiry and by\n\nthe disciplinary authority in case any punishment is to be imposed on\n\nthe appellant.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_23\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_24\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"PARTY\" data-docid=\"1648971\" id=\"span_2\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"PARTY\" data-docid=\"41616264\" id=\"span_3\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"PARTY\" data-docid=\"840463\" id=\"span_4\">16.   We must mention here that the appellant has not raised this issue\n\nregarding the nonconformity in the procedure followed by the disciplinary\n\nauthority in his written statement. This aspect has been raised in the\n\nwrit petition but it appears that the contention was not pursued while\n\narguing the writ petition. Considering all aspects of the matter, in our\n\nopinion, there is no need to quash the enquiry. The learned Counsel for\n\nthe appellant has pressed into service several judgments, including the\n\njudgements of the Supreme court in <a href=\"/doc/840463/\" id=\"a_9\">State of Punjab v. V K Khanna &amp;\n\nOrs</a> reported in (2001) 2 SCC 330, G. M. Tank v. State of Gujarat &amp;\n\nOrs reported in (<a href=\"/doc/41616264/\" id=\"a_10\">2006) 5 SCC 446, and S. Bhaskar Reddy &amp; Anr. v.\n\nSuperintendent of Police &amp; Anr</a> reported in (2015) 2 SCC 365, the\n\njudgment of a learned Single Judge of the Madras High Court in the case\n\nof <a href=\"/doc/1648971/\" id=\"a_11\">A Baliah David v. Central Bank of India</a> reported in 2002 (1) LLN\n\n1053 and the decision in WPST 677 of 2009 of a Division Bench of this\n\nCourt dated 3rd May, 2010 in support of his contention that once the\n\nappellant has been found not guilty and acquitted by the criminal Court,\n the disciplinary enquiry should not be held. A careful reading of these\n\njudgments does not, in our opinion, fortify the argument of the learned\n\nCounsel. It is now well-settled by a catena of judgments of the Supreme\n\nCourt that criminal proceedings and a disciplinary enquiry are two\n\ndifferent aspects of a case. The evidence in a criminal proceeding has to\n\nbe proved against the accused to their hilt and beyond reasonable doubt\n\nwhereas in the case of disciplinary enquiries the evidence has to be\n\nassessed on the basis of preponderance of probabilities.       It is not\n\nincumbent on the disciplinary authority to discontinue a disciplinary\n\nenquiry merely because a delinquent has been acquitted by the same\n\ncharge by a criminal court. The acquittal may have been because there\n\nwas no evidence or because witnesses did not testify before the Court or\n\nfor a myriad other reasons.      It would no doubt be true that the\n\ndisciplinary authority must consider the effect of an acquittal by a\n\ncompetent criminal court and conclude whether any action should be\n\ntaken against the delinquent.</span></span></span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_25\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_26\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Neg\" data-docid=\"8766447\" id=\"span_5\">17.   The case of <a href=\"/doc/8766447/\" id=\"a_12\">Ajay Kumar Chaudhary v. Union of India</a> through\n\nits Secretary &amp; Anr (Civil Appeal No. 1912 of 2015 decided on February\n\n16, 2015), in our opinion, has no application in this case as the learned\n\nSingle Judge has granted leave to the appellant to move the appropriate\n\nauthority for revocation of the suspension order. If any such application\n\nis submitted by the appellant the disciplinary authority would surely\n\ntake into consideration the observations of the Supreme Court in the\n\naforesaid decision.</span>\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_27\"> 18.   Considering all aspects of the matter, in our opinion, the order of\n\nthe learned Single Judge deserves to be upheld. The appeal is dismissed.\n\nNo order as to costs. Consequently the application being CAN No.11742\n\nof 2014 is also dismissed.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_28\">\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_29\">19.   Urgent certified photocopies of this judgment, if applied for, be\n\ngiven to the learned Advocates for the parties upon compliance of all\n\nformalities.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_30\">\n\n\n</p><pre id=\"pre_2\">(R. K. Bag, J.)                                    (Nishita Mhatre, J.)\n </pre>",

    "title": "Suraj S vs Inspector Of Police on 26 March, 2013",
    "doc": "<h2 class=\"doc_title\">Suraj S vs Inspector Of Police on 26 March, 2013</h2>\n\n<h3 class=\"doc_author\">Author: <a href=\"/search/?formInput=authorid:p-bhavadasan\">P.Bhavadasan</a></h3>\n\n<h3 class=\"doc_bench\">Bench: <a href=\"/search/?formInput=benchid:p-bhavadasan\">P.Bhavadasan</a></h3>\n\n<pre id=\"pre_1\">       \n\n  \n\n  \n\n \n \n                            IN THE HIGH COURT OF KERALA AT ERNAKULAM\n\n                                                        PRESENT:\n\n                            THE HONOURABLE MR.JUSTICE P.BHAVADASAN\n\n                  TUESDAY, THE 26TH DAY OF MARCH 2013/5TH CHAITHRA 1935\n\n                                           Bail Appl..No. 2000 of 2013 ()\n                                                -------------------------------\n           CRIME NO. 415/2012 OF CYBER CRIME CELL, CENTRAL CRIME BRANCH,\n                                        EGMORE, CHENNAI, TAMIL NADU\n                                                    -----------------------\n\nPETITIONER/ACCUSED :\n------------------------------------\n\n            SURAJ S., S/O.I.SASIDHARA KURUP,\n            AGED 27 YEARS, ANANDAVILASOM,\n            THATTA P.O., PATHANAMTHITTA - 691 525\n\n            BY ADV. SMT.ASHA ELIZABETH MATHEW\n\nRESPONDENTS/COMPLAINANT &amp; S I OF POLICE :\n------------------------------------------------------------------------\n\n        1. INSPECTOR OF POLICE, CYBER CRIME CELL,\n            CENTRAL CRIME BRANCH,\n            EGMORE, CHENNAI - 220.\n\n        2. SUB INSPECTOR OF POLICE,\n            POLICE STATION, KODUMON\n            PATHANAMTHITTA DISTRICT.\n\n            R1 &amp; R2 BY PUBLIC PROSECUTOR SRI. ROY THOMAS\n\n\n            THIS BAIL APPLICATION HAVING COME UP FOR ADMISSION ON 26-03-2013,\n            THE COURT ON THE SAME DAY PASSED THE FOLLOWING:\n\n\nMn\n\f\n\n\n                      P.BHAVADASAN, J.\n              ---------------------------------------\n              Bail Application No.2000 OF 2013\n              ---------------------------------------\n            Dated this the 26th day of March, 2013.\n\n\n                            O R D E R\n</pre>\n\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_1\">      The petitioner is accused in Crime No.415/2012 of Cyber\n\nCrime Cell, Central Crime Branch, Chennai who is alleged to have\n\ncommitted the offences punishable under Section 4 of the\n\nWomen Harassment Act, 2000, <a href=\"/doc/68146/\" id=\"a_1\">Section 509</a> of the Indian Penal\n\nCode read with <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_2\">Section 66A</a> of the Information Technology Act.\n\nThe allegation against the petitioner is that he had taken the\n\nphotographs of the de facto complainant, blackmailed her and in\n\nspite of the warning given by the Police, he published the\n\nphotographs of her in the face book.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_2\">      2. The petitioner points out that he has not done anything\n\nand he is totally innocent and has been falsely implicated in the\n\nmatter. At any rate, according to him, he may be granted some\n\ntime to surrender before the court concerned, and it is submitted\n\nthat in the meanwhile, he may not be arrested. <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"PARTY\" data-docid=\"658859\" id=\"span_1\">In support of the\n\ncontention that this Court is competent to grant anticipatory bail,\n\f\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_2\">B.A.No.2000/2013                  2</span>\n\n\nthe learned counsel relied on the decision in <a href=\"/doc/658859/\" id=\"a_3\">Madhusoodan vs.\n\nSuperintendent of Police</a> (1992 KHC 301).</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_3\">       3.     Learned Public Prosecutor opposed the petition and\n\npointed out that the offences are of serious nature and the\n\ninvestigation is going on. Even in the complaint, a copy of which\n\nis produced along with the application, it is mentioned that the\n\npetitioner had been cautioned on two earlier occasions and still\n\nhe continued his activities.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_4\">       4. After having heard the learned counsel for the petitioner,\n\nthe learned Public Prosecutor and after having perused the\n\nrecords, the claim of the petitioner that he is totally innocent\n\nmay not be acceptable as of now. What are the nature of the\n\nmaterials collected by the investigating officer are not available\n\nto be considered by this Court at this stage. The crime has been\n\nregistered in Tamil Nadu namely, in the Cyber Crime Cell and\n\ngoing by the records, First Information Report has been filed\n\nbefore the Chief Metropolitan Magistrate.\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_5\">       Even assuming that the petitioner is to be arrested within\n\nthe jurisdiction of this Court, <a href=\"/doc/1932336/\" id=\"a_4\">Section 81</a> of the Code of Criminal\n\f\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_3\">B.A.No.2000/2013                 3</span>\n\n\nProcedure take care of the situation.     There is no reason to\n\nbelieve that the investigating officer or the officer carrying out\n\narrest will not act in accordance with law. This Court finds no\n\nground to exercise its extra ordinary jurisdiction in favour of the\n\npetitioner.     The application is devoid of merits and it is\n\naccordingly dismissed.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_6\">\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_7\">                                      P.BHAVADASAN\n                                           JUDGE\n\n\n\nsmp\n\f\n\n\n</p>",

    "tid": 76238264,
    "publishdate": "2015-03-24",
    "title": "Shreya Singhal vs U.O.I. on 24 March, 2015",
    "doc": "<h2 class=\"doc_title\">Shreya Singhal vs U.O.I. on 24 March, 2015</h2>\n\n<h3 class=\"doc_author\">Author: <a href=\"/search/?formInput=authorid:r-f-nariman\">R.F. Nariman</a></h3>\n\n<h3 class=\"doc_bench\">Bench: <a href=\"/search/?formInput=benchid:r-f-nariman\">R.F. Nariman</a></h3>\n\n<pre id=\"pre_1\">                                                          1\n\n                                                                             REPORTABLE\n\n                                          IN THE SUPREME COURT OF INDIA\n                                       CRIMINAL/CIVIL ORIGINAL JURISDICTION\n                                     WRIT PETITION (CRIMINAL) NO.167 OF 2012\n\n                         SHREYA SINGHAL                               \u2026 PETITIONER\n                                                      VERSUS\n                         UNION OF INDIA                              \u2026 RESPONDENT\n                                                          WITH\n\n                                        WRIT PETITION (CIVIL) NO.21 OF 2013\n                                        WRIT PETITION (CIVIL) NO.23 OF 2013\n                                        WRIT PETITION (CIVIL) NO. 97 OF 2013\n                                     WRIT PETITION (CRIMINAL) NO.199 OF 2013\n                                       WRIT PETITION (CIVIL) NO. 217 OF 2013\n                                     WRIT PETITION (CRIMINAL) NO.222 OF 2013\n                                     WRIT PETITION (CRIMINAL) NO.225 OF 2013\n                                        WRIT PETITION (CIVIL) NO.758 OF 2014\n                                     WRIT PETITION (CRIMINAL) NO.196 OF 2014\n\n\n                                                     JUDGMENT\n</pre>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_1\">                         R.F. NARIMAN, J.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_2\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_3\">                         1.        This batch of writ petitions filed under <a href=\"/doc/981147/\" id=\"a_1\">Article 32</a> of the\n\n                         Constitution of India raises very important and far-reaching\n\n                         questions relatable primarily to the fundamental right of free\n\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_1\">Signature Not Verified   speech and expression guaranteed by Article 19(1)(a) of the</span>\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_2\">Digitally signed by</span>\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_3\">Om Parkash Sharma</span>\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_4\">Date: 2015.03.24</span>\n\n                         Constitution of India. The immediate cause for concern in these\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_5\">13:27:50 IST</span>\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_6\">Reason:</span>\n\n\n\n\n                         petitions is <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_2\">Section 66A</a> of the Information Technology Act of 2000.\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_7\">\f                                  2</span>\n\nThis Section was not in the Act as originally enacted, but came into\n\nforce by virtue of an Amendment Act of 2009 with effect from\n\n27.10.2009. Since all the arguments raised by several counsel for\n\nthe petitioners deal with the unconstitutionality of this Section it is\n\nset out hereinbelow:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_1\">      \u201c66-A. Punishment for sending offensive messages\n      through communication service, etc.\u2014Any person\n      who sends, by means of a computer resource or a\n      communication device,\u2014\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_2\">            (a) any information that is grossly offensive\n            or has menacing character; or\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_3\">            (b) any information which he knows to be\n            false, but for the purpose of causing\n            annoyance,      inconvenience,   danger,\n            obstruction,   insult,   injury, criminal\n            intimidation, enmity, hatred or ill will,\n            persistently by making use of such\n            computer resource or a communication\n            device; or\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_4\">            (c) any electronic mail or electronic mail\n            message for the purpose of causing\n            annoyance or inconvenience or to deceive\n            or to mislead the addressee or recipient\n            about the origin of such messages,\n\n\n      shall be punishable with imprisonment for a term which\n      may extend to three years and with fine.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_4\">      Explanation.\u2014 For the purposes of this section, terms\n      \u201celectronic mail\u201d and \u201celectronic mail message\u201d means\n      a message or information created or transmitted or\n      received on a computer, computer system, computer\n      resource     or    communication     device   including\n      attachments in text, image, audio, video and any other\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_8\">\f                                                          3</span>\n\n                 electronic record, which may be transmitted with the\n                 message.\u201d1\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_5\">        2.       A related challenge is also made to <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_3\">Section 69A</a> introduced\n\n        by the same amendment which reads as follows:-\n\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_5\">                 \u201c69-A. Power to issue directions for blocking for\n                 public access of any information through any\n                 computer resource.\u2014(1) Where the Central\n                 Government or any of its officers specially authorised\n                 by it in this behalf is satisfied that it is necessary or\n                 expedient so to do, in the interest of sovereignty and\n                 integrity of India, defence of India, security of the State,\n                 friendly relations with foreign States or public order or\n                 for preventing incitement to the commission of any\n\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_9\">1</span>\n         The genealogy of this Section may be traced back to Section 10(2)(a) of the U.K. Post Office\n(Amendment) Act, 1935, which made it an offence to send any message by telephone which is grossly offensive\nor of an indecent, obscene, or menacing character. This Section was substantially reproduced by Section 66 of\nthe UK Post Office Act, 1953 as follows:\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_6\">         66. Prohibition of sending offensive or false telephone messages or false telegrams, etc.\n         If any person\u2014\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_7\">         (a)sends any message by telephone which is grossly offensive or of an indecent, obscene or\nmenacing character ;\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_6\">         (b)sends any message by telephone, or any telegram, which he knows to be false, for the\npurpose of causing annoyance, inconvenience or needless anxiety to any other person ; or\n</p><p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_7\">         (c)persistently makes telephone calls without reasonable cause and for any such purpose as\naforesaid,\n         he shall be liable on summary conviction to a fine not exceeding ten pounds, or to\nimprisonment for a term not exceeding one month, or to both.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_8\">         This Section in turn was replaced by Section 49 of the British Telecommunication Act, 1981 and\nSection 43 of the British Telecommunication Act, 1984. In its present form in the UK, it is Section 127 of the\nTelecommunication Act, 2003 which is relevant and which is as follows:-\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_8\">         127.     Improper use of public electronic communications network\n                   (1)      A person is guilty of an offence if he -\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_9\">                   (a)      sends by means of a public electronic communications network a message\n                       or other matter that is grossly offensive or of an indecent, obscene or menacing\n                       character; or\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_10\">                   (b)      cause any such message or matter to be so sent.\n                   (2)      A person is guilty of an offence if, for the purpose of causing annoyance,\n                       inconvenience or needless anxiety to another, he-</p><pre id=\"pre_2\">\n                   (a)      sends by means of a public electronic communications network, a message\n                       that he knows to be false,\n                   (b)      causes such a message to be sent; or\n                   (c)      persistently makes use of a public electronic communications network.\n                   (3)      A person guilty of an offence under this section shall be liable, on summary\n</pre><p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_11\">                       conviction, to imprisonment for a term not exceeding six months or to a fine not\n                       exceeding level 5 on the standard scale, or to both.\n                   (4)      Subsections (1) and (2) do not apply to anything done in the course of\n                       providing a programme service (within the meaning of the Broadcasting Act\n                       1990 (c.42)).\n</p><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_10\">\f                                  4</span><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_12\">\n\n      cognizable offence relating to above, it may subject to\n      the provisions of sub-section (2), for reasons to be\n      recorded in writing, by order, direct any agency of the\n      Government or intermediary to block for access by the\n      public or cause to be blocked for access by the public\n      any information generated, transmitted, received,\n      stored or hosted in any computer resource.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_13\">\n      (2) The procedure and safeguards subject to which\n      such blocking for access by the public may be carried\n      out, shall be such as may be prescribed.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Facts\" id=\"p_14\">\n      (3) The intermediary who fails to comply with the\n      direction issued under sub-section (1) shall be\n      punished with an imprisonment for a term which may\n      extend to seven years and shall also be liable to fine.\u201d\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_15\">3.   The Statement of Objects and Reasons appended to the Bill\n\nwhich introduced the Amendment Act stated in paragraph 3 that:\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_9\">      \u201c3. A rapid increase in the use of computer and internet\n      has given rise to new forms of crimes like publishing\n      sexually explicit materials in electronic form, video\n      voyeurism and breach of confidentiality and leakage of\n      data by intermediary, e-commerce frauds like\n      personation commonly known as Phishing, identity\n      theft and offensive messages through communication\n      services. So, penal provisions are required to be\n      included in the <a href=\"/doc/1965344/\" id=\"a_4\">Information Technology Act</a>, the Indian\n      Penal code, the <a href=\"/doc/1953529/\" id=\"a_5\">Indian Evidence Act</a> and the code of\n      Criminal Procedure to prevent such crimes.\u201d\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_16\">4.   The petitioners contend that the very basis of <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_6\">Section 66A</a> -\n\nthat it has given rise to new forms of crimes - is incorrect, and that\n\n<a href=\"/doc/48199014/\" id=\"a_7\">Sections 66B</a> to <a href=\"/doc/91763765/\" id=\"a_8\">67C</a> and various Sections of the <a href=\"/doc/1569253/\" id=\"a_9\">Indian Penal\n\nCode</a> (which will be referred to hereinafter) are good enough to\n\ndeal with all these crimes.\n</p><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_11\">\f                                   5</span><p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_17\">\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_18\">5.    The petitioners\u2019 various counsel raised a large number of\n\npoints as to the constitutionality of <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_10\">Section 66A</a>.        According to\n\nthem, first and foremost <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_11\">Section 66A</a> infringes the fundamental\n\nright to free speech and expression and is not saved by any of the\n\neight subjects covered in <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_12\">Article 19(2).</a> According to them, the\n\ncausing of annoyance, inconvenience, danger, obstruction, insult,\n\ninjury, criminal intimidation, enmity, hatred or ill-will are all outside\n\nthe purview of <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_13\">Article 19(2).</a>       Further, in creating an offence,\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_14\">Section 66A</a> suffers from the vice of vagueness because unlike the\n\noffence created by <a href=\"/doc/326206/\" id=\"a_15\">Section 66</a> of the same Act, none of the\n\naforesaid terms are even attempted to be defined and cannot be\n\ndefined, the result being that innocent persons are roped in as well\n\nas those who are not. Such persons are not told clearly on which\n\nside of the line they fall; and it would be open to the authorities to\n\nbe as arbitrary and whimsical as they like in booking such persons\n\nunder the said Section.       In fact, a large number of innocent\n\npersons have been booked and many instances have been given\n\nin the form of a note to the Court. The enforcement of the said\n\nSection would really be an insidious form of censorship which\n\nimpairs a core value contained in <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_16\">Article 19(1)(a).</a> In addition, the\n\nsaid Section has a chilling effect on the freedom of speech and\n\nexpression. Also, the right of viewers is infringed as such chilling\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_12\">\f                                    6</span>\n\neffect would not give them the benefit of many shades of grey in\n\nterms of various points of view that could be viewed over the\n\ninternet.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_19\">       The petitioners also contend that their rights under <a href=\"/doc/367586/\" id=\"a_17\">Articles\n\n14</a> and <a href=\"/doc/1199182/\" id=\"a_18\">21</a> are breached inasmuch there is no intelligible differentia\n\nbetween those who use the internet and those who by words\n\nspoken or written use other mediums of communication. To punish\n\nsomebody        because      he   uses   a    particular   medium    of\n\ncommunication is itself a discriminatory object and would fall foul\n\nof <a href=\"/doc/367586/\" id=\"a_19\">Article 14</a> in any case.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_20\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_21\">6.    In reply, Mr. Tushar Mehta, learned Additional Solicitor\n\nGeneral defended the constitutionality of <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_20\">Section 66A</a>. He argued\n\nthat the legislature is in the best position to understand and\n\nappreciate the needs of the people.          The Court will, therefore,\n\ninterfere with the legislative process only when a statute is clearly\n\nviolative of the rights conferred on the citizen under Part-III of the\n\nConstitution.       There is a presumption in favour of the\n\nconstitutionality of an enactment.       Further, the Court would so\n\nconstrue a statute to make it workable and in doing so can read\n\ninto it or read down the provisions that are impugned.              The\n\nConstitution does not impose impossible standards of determining\n\nvalidity.   Mere possibility of abuse of a provision cannot be a\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_13\">\f                                   7</span>\n\nground to declare a provision invalid. Loose language may have\n\nbeen used in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_21\">Section 66A</a> to deal with novel methods of disturbing\n\nother people\u2019s rights by using the internet as a tool to do so.\n\nFurther, vagueness is not a ground to declare a statute\n\nunconstitutional if the statute is otherwise legislatively competent\n\nand non-arbitrary. He cited a large number of judgments before us\n\nboth from this Court and from overseas to buttress his\n\nsubmissions.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_22\">Freedom of Speech and Expression\n\n<a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_22\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> of the Constitution of India states as follows:\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_10\">      \u201cArticle 19. Protection of certain rights regarding\n      freedom of speech, etc.\u2014(1) All citizens shall have\n      the right\u2014\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_11\">      (a) to freedom of speech and expression;\u201d\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_23\">7.    <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_23\">Article 19(2)</a> states:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_12\">      \u201cArticle 19. Protection of certain rights regarding\n      freedom of speech, etc.\u2014(2) Nothing in sub-clause\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_13\">      (a) of clause (1) shall affect the operation of any\n      existing law, or prevent the State from making any law,\n      in so far as such law imposes reasonable restrictions\n      on the exercise of the right conferred by the said\n      sub-clause in the interests of the sovereignty and\n      integrity of India, the security of the State, friendly\n      relations with foreign States, public order, decency or\n      morality or in relation to contempt of court, defamation\n      or incitement to an offence.\u201d\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_24\">8.    The Preamble of the Constitution of India inter alia speaks of\n\nliberty of thought, expression, belief, faith and worship. It also\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_14\">\f                                                            8</span>\n\n         says that India is a sovereign democratic republic. It cannot be\n\n         over emphasized that when it comes to democracy, liberty of\n\n         thought and expression is a cardinal value that is of paramount\n\n         significance under our constitutional scheme.\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_25\">         <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"456839\" id=\"span_15\">9.       Various judgments of this Court have referred to the\n\n         importance of freedom of speech and expression both from the\n\n         point of view of the liberty of the individual and from the point of\n\n         view of our democratic form of government. For example, in the\n\n         early case of <a href=\"/doc/456839/\" id=\"a_24\">Romesh Thappar v. State of Madras</a>, [1950] S.C.R.\n\n         594 at 602, this Court stated that freedom of speech lay at the\n\n         foundation of all democratic organizations.</span> <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"243002\" id=\"span_16\">In <a href=\"/doc/243002/\" id=\"a_25\">Sakal Papers (P)\n\n         Ltd. &amp; Ors. v. Union of India</a>, [1962] 3 S.C.R. 842 at 866, a\n\n         Constitution Bench of this Court said freedom of speech and\n\n         expression of opinion is of paramount importance under a\n\n         democratic           constitution          which        envisages           changes          in     the\n\n         composition of legislatures and governments and must be\n\n         preserved.</span>          <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"125596\" id=\"span_17\">In a separate concurring judgment Beg,J. said, in\n\n         <a href=\"/doc/125596/\" id=\"a_26\">Bennett Coleman &amp; Co. &amp; Ors. v. Union of India &amp; Ors.</a>, [1973]\n\n         2 S.C.R. 757 at 829, that the freedom of speech and of the press\n\n         is the Ark of the Covenant of Democracy because public criticism\n\n         is essential to the working of its institutions. 2\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_18\">2</span>\n          Incidentally, the Ark of the Covenant is perhaps the single most important focal point in Judaism. The\noriginal ten commandments which the Lord himself gave to Moses was housed in a wooden chest which was gold plated\nand called the Ark of the Covenant and carried by the Jews from place to place until it found its final repose in the first</span>\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_19\">\f                                                9</span>\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_26\">         <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1327342\" id=\"span_20\">10.     Equally, in <a href=\"/doc/1327342/\" id=\"a_27\">S. Khushboo v. Kanniamal &amp; Anr.</a>, (2010) 5\n\n         SCC 600 this Court stated, in paragraph 45 that the importance of\n\n         freedom of speech and expression though not absolute was\n\n         necessary as we need to tolerate unpopular views. This right\n\n         requires the free flow of opinions and ideas essential to sustain the\n\n         collective life of the citizenry. While an informed citizenry is a\n\n         pre-condition for meaningful governance, the culture of open\n\n         dialogue is generally of great societal importance.</span>\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_27\">         11.     This last judgment is important in that it refers to the \u201cmarket\n\n         place of ideas\u201d concept that has permeated American Law. This\n\n         was put in the felicitous words of Justice Holmes in his famous\n\n         dissent in Abrams v. United States, 250 US 616 (1919), thus:\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_14\">                  \u201cBut when men have realized that time has upset many\n                  fighting faiths, they may come to believe even more\n                  than they believe the very foundations of their own\n                  conduct that the ultimate good desired is better\n                  reached by free trade in ideas-that the best test of truth\n                  is the power of thought to get itself accepted in the\n                  competition of the market, and that truth is the only\n                  ground upon which their wishes safely can be carried\n                  out. That at any rate is the theory of our Constitution.\u201d\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_28\">         12.     Justice Brandeis in his famous concurring judgment in\n\n         Whitney v. California, 71 L. Ed. 1095 said:\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_29\">\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_15\">                  \u201cThose who won our independence believed that the\n                  final end of the state was to make men free to develop\n\ntemple - that is the temple built by Solomon.\n</blockquote><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_21\">\f                            10</span>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_16\">their faculties, and that in its government the\ndeliberative forces should prevail over the arbitrary.\nThey valued liberty both as an end and as a means.\nThey believed liberty to be the secret of happiness and\ncourage to be the secret of liberty. They believed that\nfreedom to think as you will and to speak as you think\nare means indispensable to the discovery and spread\nof political truth; that without free speech and assembly\ndiscussion would be futile; that with them, discussion\naffords ordinarily adequate protection against the\ndissemination of noxious doctrine; that the greatest\nmenace to freedom is an inert people; that public\ndiscussion is a political duty; and that this should be a\nfundamental principle of the American government.\nThey recognized the risks to which all human\ninstitutions are subject. But they knew that order\ncannot be secured merely through fear of punishment\nfor its infraction; that it is hazardous to discourage\nthought, hope and imagination; that fear breeds\nrepression; that repression breeds hate; that hate\nmenaces stable government; that the path of safety\nlies in the opportunity to discuss freely supposed\ngrievances and proposed remedies; and that the fitting\nremedy for evil counsels is good ones. Believing in the\npower of reason as applied through public discussion,\nthey eschewed silence coerced by law-the argument of\nforce in its worst form. Recognizing the occasional\ntyrannies of governing majorities, they amended the\nConstitution so that free speech and assembly should\nbe guaranteed.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_17\">Fear of serious injury cannot alone justify suppression\nof free speech and assembly. Men feared witches and\nburnt women. It is the function of speech to free men\nfrom the bondage of irrational fears. To justify\nsuppression of free speech there must be reasonable\nground to fear that serious evil will result if free speech\nis practiced. There must be reasonable ground to\nbelieve that the danger apprehended is imminent.\nThere must be reasonable ground to believe that the\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_22\">\f                                                         11</span>\n\n                 evil to be prevented is a serious one. Every\n                 denunciation of existing law tends in some measure to\n                 increase the probability that there will be violation of\n                 it. Condonation of a breach enhances the probability.\n                 Expressions of approval add to the probability.\n                 Propagation of the criminal state of mind by teaching\n                 syndicalism increases it. Advocacy of lawbreaking\n                 heightens it still further. But even advocacy of violation,\n                 however reprehensible morally, is not a justification for\n                 denying free speech where the advocacy falls short of\n                 incitement and there is nothing to indicate that the\n                 advocacy would be immediately acted on. The wide\n                 difference between advocacy and incitement, between\n                 preparation and attempt, between assembling and\n                 conspiracy, must be borne in mind. In order to support\n                 a finding of clear and present danger it must be shown\n                 either that immediate serious violence was to be\n                 expected or was advocated, or that the past conduct\n                 furnished reason to believe that such advocacy was\n                 then contemplated.\u201d (at page 1105, 1106)\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_30\">        13.      This leads us to a discussion of what is the content of the\n\n        expression \u201cfreedom of speech and expression\u201d. There are three\n\n        concepts which are fundamental in understanding the reach of this\n\n        most basic of human rights. The first is discussion, the second is\n\n        advocacy, and the third is incitement. Mere discussion or even\n\n        advocacy of a particular cause howsoever unpopular is at the\n\n        heart of <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_28\">Article 19(1)(a).</a>                 It is only when such discussion or\n\n        advocacy reaches the level of incitement that <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_29\">Article 19(2)</a> kicks\n\n        in.3 It is at this stage that a law may be made curtailing the speech\n\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_23\">3</span>\n          A good example of the difference between advocacy and incitement is Mark Antony\u2019s speech in Shakespeare\u2019s\nimmortal classic Julius Caesar. Mark Antony begins cautiously. Brutus is chastised for calling Julius Caesar ambitious\nand is repeatedly said to be an \u201chonourable man\u201d. He then shows the crowd Caesar\u2019s mantle and describes who struck\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_24\">\f                                                             12</span>\n\n         or expression that leads inexorably to or tends to cause public\n\n         disorder or tends to cause or tends to affect the sovereignty &amp;\n\n         integrity of India, the security of the State, friendly relations with\n\n         foreign States, etc. Why it is important to have these three\n\n         concepts in mind is because most of the arguments of both\n\n         petitioners and respondents tended to veer around the expression\n\n         \u201cpublic order\u201d.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_31\">         14.      It is at this point that a word needs to be said about the use\n\n         of American judgments in the context of <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_30\">Article 19(1)(a).</a>                                            In\n\n         virtually every significant judgment of this Court, reference has\n\n         been made to judgments from across the Atlantic. Is it safe to do\n\n         so?\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_32\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_33\">         15.      It is significant to notice first the differences between the US\n\n\nCaesar where. It is at this point, after the interjection of two citizens from the crowd, that Antony says-\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_34\">        \u201cANTONY- Good friends, sweet friends, let me not stir you up\n        To such a sudden flood of mutiny.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_35\">        They that have done this deed are honourable:\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_18\">        What private griefs they have, alas, I know not,\n        That made them do it: they are wise and honourable,\n        And will, no doubt, with reasons answer you.\n        I come not, friends, to steal away your hearts:\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_19\">        I am no orator, as Brutus is;\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_20\">        But, as you know me all, a plain blunt man,\n        That love my friend; and that they know full well\n        That gave me public leave to speak of him:\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_21\">        For I have neither wit, nor words, nor worth,\n        Action, nor utterance, nor the power of speech,\n        To stir men&#x27;s blood: I only speak right on;\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_22\">        I tell you that which you yourselves do know;\n        Show you sweet Caesar&#x27;s wounds, poor poor dumb mouths,\n        And bid them speak for me: but were I Brutus,\n        And Brutus Antony, there were an Antony\n        Would ruffle up your spirits and put a tongue\n        In every wound of Caesar that should move\n        The stones of Rome to rise and mutiny.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_23\">        ALL- We&#x27;ll mutiny.\u201d\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_25\">\f                                  13</span>\n\nFirst Amendment and <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_31\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> read with <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_32\">Article 19(2).</a> The\n\nfirst important difference is the absoluteness of the U.S. first\n\nAmendment \u2013 Congress shall make no law which abridges the\n\nfreedom of speech. Second, whereas the U.S. First Amendment\n\nspeaks of freedom of speech and of the press, without any\n\nreference to \u201cexpression\u201d, <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_33\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> speaks of freedom of\n\nspeech and expression without any reference to \u201cthe press\u201d.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_24\">Third, under the US Constitution, speech may be abridged,\n\nwhereas under our Constitution, reasonable restrictions may be\n\nimposed. Fourth, under our Constitution such restrictions have to\n\nbe in the interest of eight designated subject matters - that is any\n\nlaw seeking to impose a restriction on the freedom of speech can\n\nonly pass muster if it is proximately related to any of the eight\n\nsubject matters set out in <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_34\">Article 19(2).</a>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_36\">16.   Insofar as the first apparent difference is concerned, the U.S.\n\nSupreme Court has never given literal effect to the declaration that\n\nCongress shall make no law abridging the freedom of speech.\n\nThe approach of the Court which is succinctly stated in one of the\n\nearly U.S. Supreme Court Judgments, continues even today. In\n\nChaplinsky v. New Hampshire, 86 L. Ed. 1031, Justice Murphy\n\nwho delivered the opinion of the Court put it thus:-\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_25\">      \u201cAllowing the broadest scope to the language and\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_26\">\f                                 14</span>\n\n      purpose of the Fourteenth Amendment, it is well\n      understood that the right of free speech is not absolute\n      at all times and under all circumstances. There are\n      certain well-defined and narrowly limited classes of\n      speech, the prevention and punishment of which has\n      never been thought to raise any Constitutional\n      problem. These include the lewd and obscene, the\n      profane, the libelous, and the insulting or &#x27;fighting&#x27;\n      words\u2014those which by their very utterance inflict injury\n      or tend to incite an immediate breach of the peace. It\n      has been well observed that such utterances are no\n      essential part of any exposition of ideas, and are of\n      such slight social value as a step to truth that any\n      benefit that may be derived from them is clearly\n      outweighed by the social interest in order and\n      morality. &#x27;Resort to epithets or personal abuse is not in\n      any proper sense communication of information or\n      opinion safeguarded by the Constitution, and its\n      punishment as a criminal act would raise no question\n      under that instrument.&#x27; Cantwell v. Connecticut, 310\n      U.S. 296, 309, 310, <a href=\"/doc/1408526/\" id=\"a_35\">60</a> S.Ct. 900, 906, 84 L.Ed.1213,\n      128 A.L.R. 1352.\u201d       (at page 1035)\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_37\">17.   So far as the second apparent difference is concerned, the\n\nAmerican Supreme Court has included \u201cexpression\u201d as part of\n\nfreedom of speech and this Court has included \u201cthe press\u201d as\n\nbeing covered under <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_36\">Article 19(1)(a)</a>, so that, as a matter of judicial\n\ninterpretation, both the US and India protect the freedom of\n\nspeech and expression as well as press freedom.           Insofar as\n\nabridgement and reasonable restrictions are concerned, both the\n\nU.S. Supreme Court and this Court have held that a restriction in\n\norder to be reasonable must be narrowly tailored or narrowly\n\ninterpreted so as to abridge or restrict only what is absolutely\n\nnecessary. It is only when it comes to the eight subject matters\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_27\">\f                                 15</span>\n\nthat there is a vast difference. In the U.S., if there is a compelling\n\nnecessity to achieve an important governmental or societal goal, a\n\nlaw abridging freedom of speech may pass muster. But in India,\n\nsuch law cannot pass muster if it is in the interest of the general\n\npublic. Such law has to be covered by one of the eight subject\n\nmatters set out under <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_37\">Article 19(2).</a> If it does not, and is outside\n\nthe pale of 19(2), Indian courts will strike down such law.\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_38\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"687159\" id=\"span_28\">18.   Viewed from the above perspective, American judgments\n\nhave great persuasive value on the content of freedom of speech\n\nand expression and the tests laid down for its infringement. It is\n\nonly when it comes to sub-serving the general public interest that\n\nthere is the world of a difference. This is perhaps why in\n\n<a href=\"/doc/687159/\" id=\"a_38\">Kameshwar Prasad &amp; Ors. v. The State of Bihar &amp; Anr.</a>, 1962\n\nSupp. (3) S.C.R. 369, this Court held:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_26\">      \u201cAs regards these decisions of the American Courts, it\n      should be borne in mind that though the First\n      Amendment to the Constitution of the United State\n      reading &quot;Congress shall make no law.... abridging the\n      freedom of speech...&quot; appears to confer no power on\n      the Congress to impose any restriction on the exercise\n      of the guaranteed right, still it has always been\n      understood that the freedom guaranteed is subject to\n      the police power - the scope of which however has not\n      been defined with precision or uniformly. It is on the\n      basis of the police power to abridge that freedom that\n      the constitutional validity of laws penalising libels, and\n      those relating to sedition, or to obscene publications\n      etc., has been sustained. The resultant flexibility of the\n      restrictions that could be validly imposed renders the\n      American decisions inapplicable to and without much\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_29\">\f                                  16</span>\n\n      use for resolving the questions arising under\n      <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_39\">Art. 19(1) (a)</a> or (b) of our Constitution wherein the\n      grounds on which limitations might be placed on the\n      guaranteed right are set out with definiteness and\n      precision.\u201d ( At page 378)\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_39\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"223504\" id=\"span_30\">19.   But when it comes to understanding the impact and content\n\nof freedom of speech, in <a href=\"/doc/223504/\" id=\"a_40\">Indian Express Newspapers (Bombay)\n\nPrivate Limited &amp; Ors. v. Union of India &amp; Ors.</a>, (1985) 2 SCR\n\n287, Venkataramiah,J. stated:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_27\">       \u201cWhile examining the constitutionality of a law which is\n      alleged to contravene <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_41\">Article 19 (1) (a)</a> of the\n      Constitution, we cannot, no doubt, be solely guided by\n      the decisions of the Supreme Court of the United\n      States of America. But in order to understand the basic\n      principles of freedom of speech and expression and\n      the need for that freedom in a democratic country, we\n      may take them into consideration. The pattern of <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_42\">Article\n      19 (1) (a)</a> and of <a href=\"/doc/935769/\" id=\"a_43\">Article 19 (1) (g)</a> of our constitution is\n      different from the pattern of the First Amendment to the\n      American Constitution which is almost absolute in its\n      terms. The rights guaranteed under <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_44\">Article 19 (1) (a)</a>\n      and <a href=\"/doc/935769/\" id=\"a_45\">Article 19 (1) (g)</a> of the Constitution are to be read\n      along with clauses (2) and (6) of <a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_46\">Article 19</a> which carve\n      out areas in respect of which valid legislation can be\n      made.\u201d (at page 324)\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_28\">20.   With these prefatory remarks, we will now go to the other\n\naspects of the challenge made in these writ petitions and argued\n\nbefore us.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_40\">A. <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_47\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> \u2013\n\n      <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_48\">Section 66A</a> has been challenged on the ground that it casts\n\nthe net very wide \u2013 \u201call information\u201d that is disseminated over the\n\ninternet is included within its reach. It will be useful to note that\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_31\">\f                                  17</span>\n\n<a href=\"/doc/629401/\" id=\"a_49\">Section 2(v)</a> of Information Technology Act, 2000 defines\n\ninformation as follows:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_29\">      \u201c2. Definitions.\u2014(1) In this Act, unless the context\n      otherwise requires,\u2014\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_30\">      (v) \u201cInformation\u201d includes data, message, text, images,\n      sound, voice, codes, computer programmes, software\n      and databases or micro film or computer generated\n      micro fiche.\u201d\n\n      Two things will be noticed. The first is that the definition is an\n\ninclusive one. Second, the definition does not refer to what the\n\ncontent of information can be. In fact, it refers only to the medium\n\nthrough which such information is disseminated.            It is clear,\n\ntherefore, that the petitioners are correct in saying that the public\u2019s\n\nright to know is directly affected by <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_50\">Section 66A</a>. Information of all\n\nkinds is roped in \u2013 such information may have scientific, literary or\n\nartistic value, it may refer to current events, it may be obscene or\n\nseditious. That such information may cause annoyance or\n\ninconvenience to some is how the offence is made out. It is clear\n\nthat the right of the people to know \u2013 the market place of ideas \u2013\n\nwhich the internet provides to persons of all kinds is what attracts\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_51\">Section 66A</a>.     That the information sent has to be annoying,\n\ninconvenient, grossly offensive etc., also shows that no distinction\n\nis made between mere discussion or advocacy of a particular point\n\nof view which may be annoying or inconvenient or grossly\n\noffensive to some and incitement by which such words lead to an\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_32\">\f                                 18</span>\n\nimminent causal connection with public disorder, security of State\n\netc. The petitioners are right in saying that <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_52\">Section 66A</a> in creating\n\nan offence against persons who use the internet and annoy or\n\ncause inconvenience to others very clearly affects the freedom of\n\nspeech and expression of the citizenry of India at large in that such\n\nspeech or expression is directly curbed by the creation of the\n\noffence contained in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_53\">Section 66A</a>.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_41\">      In this regard, the observations of Justice Jackson in\n\nAmerican Communications Association v. Douds, 94 L. Ed.\n\n925 are apposite:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_31\">      \u201cThought control is a copyright of totalitarianism, and\n      we have no claim to it. It is not the function of our\n      Government to keep the citizen from falling into error; it\n      is the function of the citizen to keep the Government\n      from falling into error. We could justify any censorship\n      only when the censors are better shielded against error\n      than the censored.\u201d\n\nB. <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_54\">Article 19(2)</a>\n\n\n      One challenge to <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_55\">Section 66A</a> made by the petitioners\u2019\n\ncounsel is that the offence created by the said Section has no\n\nproximate relation with any of the eight subject matters contained\n\nin <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_56\">Article 19(2).</a> We may incidentally mention that the State has\n\nclaimed that the said Section can be supported under the heads of\n\npublic order, defamation, incitement to an offence and decency or\n\nmorality.\n</blockquote><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_33\">\f                                  19</span>\n\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_32\">21.   Under our constitutional scheme, as stated earlier, it is not\n\nopen to the State to curtail freedom of speech to promote the\n\ngeneral public interest. <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"243002\" id=\"span_34\">In <a href=\"/doc/243002/\" id=\"a_57\">Sakal Papers (P) Ltd. &amp; Ors. v. Union\n\nof India</a>, [1962] 3 S.C.R. 842, this Court said:</span>\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_33\">      \u201cIt may well be within the power of the State to place,\n      in the interest of the general public, restrictions upon\n      the right of a citizen to carry on business but it is not\n      open to the State to achieve this object by directly and\n      immediately curtailing any other freedom of that citizen\n      guaranteed by the Constitution and which is not\n      susceptible of abridgment on the same grounds as are\n      set out in clause (6) of <a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_58\">Article 19.</a> Therefore, the right of\n      freedom of speech cannot be taken away with the\n      object of placing restrictions on the business activities\n      of a citizen. Freedom of speech can be restricted only\n      in the interests of the security of the State, friendly\n      relations with foreign State, public order, decency or\n      morality or in relation to contempt of court, defamation\n      or incitement to an offence. It cannot, like the freedom\n      to carry on business, be curtailed in the interest of the\n      general public. If a law directly affecting it is\n      challenged, it is no answer that the restrictions enacted\n      by it are justifiable under clauses (3) to (6). For, the\n      scheme of <a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_59\">Article 19</a> is to enumerate different\n      freedoms separately and then to specify the extent of\n      restrictions to which they may be subjected and the\n      objects for securing which this could be done. A citizen\n      is entitled to enjoy each and every one of the freedoms\n      together and clause (1) does not prefer one freedom to\n      another. That is the plain meaning of this clause. It\n      follows from this that the State cannot make a law\n      which directly restricts one freedom even for securing\n      the better enjoyment of another freedom. All the\n      greater reason, therefore for holding that the State\n      cannot directly restrict one freedom by placing an\n      otherwise permissible restriction on another freedom.\u201d\n      (at page 863)\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_34\">22.   Before we come to each of these expressions, we must\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_35\">\f                                 20</span>\n\nunderstand what is meant by the expression \u201cin the interests of\u201d.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_42\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1386353\" id=\"span_36\">In <a href=\"/doc/1386353/\" id=\"a_60\">The Superintendent, Central Prison, Fatehgarh v. Ram\n\nManohar Lohia</a>, [1960] 2 S.C.R. 821, this Court laid down:</span>\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_35\">     \u201cWe do not understand the observations of the Chief\n     Justice to mean that any remote or fanciful connection\n     between the impugned Act and the public order would\n     be sufficient to sustain its validity. The learned Chief\n     Justice was only making a distinction between an Act\n     which expressly and directly purported to maintain\n     public order and one which did not expressly state the\n     said purpose but left it to be implied there from; and\n     between an Act that directly maintained public order\n     and that indirectly brought about the same result. The\n     distinction does not ignore the necessity for intimate\n     connection between the Act and the public order\n     sought to be maintained by the Act.\u201d (at pages 834,\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_36\">     835)\n     \u201cThe restriction made &quot;in the interests of public order&quot;\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_37\">     must also have reasonable relation to the object to be\n     achieved, i.e., the public order. If the restriction has no\n     proximate relationship to the achievement of public\n     order, it cannot be said that the restriction is a\n     reasonable restriction within the meaning of the said\n     clause.\u201d (at page 835)\n\n     \u201cThe decision, in our view, lays down the correct test.\n     The limitation imposed in the interests of public order\n     to be a reasonable restriction, should be one which\n     has a proximate connection or nexus with public order,\n     but not one far-fetched, hypothetical or problematical\n     or too remote in the chain of its relation with the public\n     order.\u2026\u2026\u2026There is no proximate or even foreseeable\n     connection between such instigation and the public\n     order sought to be protected under section. We cannot\n     accept the argument of the learned Advocate General\n     that instigation of a single individual not to pay tax or\n     dues is a spark which may in the long run ignite a\n     revolutionary movement destroying public order\u201d (at\n     page 836).\n</blockquote><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_37\">\f                                 21</span>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_38\">Reasonable Restrictions:\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_39\">23.   This Court has laid down what \u201creasonable restrictions\u201d\n\nmeans in several cases.      <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1256541\" id=\"span_38\">In <a href=\"/doc/1256541/\" id=\"a_61\">Chintaman Rao v. The State of\n\nMadhya Pradesh</a>, [1950] S.C.R. 759, this Court said:</span>\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_40\">             \u201cThe phrase &quot;reasonable restriction&quot; connotes\n      that the limitation imposed on a person in enjoyment of\n      the right should not be arbitrary or of an excessive\n      nature, beyond what is required in the interests of the\n      public. The word &quot;reasonable&quot; implies intelligent care\n      and deliberation, that is, the choice of a course which\n      reason dictates.
