Legislation which arbitrarily or\n      excessively invades the right cannot be said to contain\n      the quality of reasonableness and unless it strikes a\n      proper balance between the freedom guaranteed in\n      <a href=\"/doc/935769/\" id=\"a_62\">article 19(1)(g)</a> and the social control permitted by\n      clause (6) of <a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_63\">article 19</a>, it must be held to be wanting in\n      that quality.\u201d (at page 763)\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_41\">24.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"554839\" id=\"span_39\">In <a href=\"/doc/554839/\" id=\"a_64\">State of Madras v. V.G. Row</a>, [1952] S.C.R. 597, this\n\nCourt said:</span>\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_42\">      \u201cThis Court had occasion in Dr. Khare&#x27;s case (1950)\n      S.C.R. 519 to define the scope of the judicial review\n      under clause (5) of Article19 where the phrase\n      &quot;imposing reasonable restriction on the exercise of the\n      right&quot; also occurs and four out of the five Judges\n      participating in the decision expressed the view (the\n      other Judge leaving the question open) that both the\n      substantive and the procedural aspects of the\n      impugned restrictive law should be examined from the\n      point of view of reasonableness; that is to say, the\n      Court should consider not only factors such as the\n      duration and the extent of the restrictions, but also the\n      circumstances under which and the manner in which\n      their imposition has been authorised. It is important in\n      this context to bear in mind that the test of\n      reasonableness, where ever prescribed, should be\n      applied to each, individual statute impugned and no\n      abstract     standard,    or   general      pattern     of\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_40\">\f                                 22</span>\n\n      reasonableness can be laid down as applicable to all\n      cases. The nature of the right alleged to have been\n      infringed, the underlying purpose of the restriction\n      imposed, the extent and urgency of the evil sought to\n      be remedied thereby, the disproportion of the\n      imposition, the prevailing conditions at the time, should\n      all enter into the judicial verdict. In evaluating such\n      elusive factors and forming their own conception of\n      what is reasonable, in all the circumstances of a given\n      case, it is inevitable that the social philosophy and the\n      scale of values of the judges participating in the\n      decision should play an important part, and the limit to\n      their interference with legislative judgment in such\n      cases can only be dictated by their sense of\n      responsibility and self- restraint and the sobering\n      reflection that the Constitution is meant not only for\n      people of their way of thinking but for all, and that the\n      majority of the elected representatives of the people\n      have, in authorising the imposition of the restrictions,\n      considered them to be reasonable.\u201d (at page 606-607)\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_43\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1849054\" id=\"span_41\">25.   Similarly, in <a href=\"/doc/1849054/\" id=\"a_65\">Mohd. Faruk v. State of Madhya Pradesh &amp;\n\nOrs.</a>, [1970] 1 S.C.R. 156, this Court said:</span>\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_44\">      \u201cThe Court must in considering the validity of the\n      impugned law imposing a prohibition on the carrying\n      on of a business or profession, attempt an evaluation\n      of its direct and immediate impact upon the\n      fundamental rights of the citizens affected thereby and\n      the larger public interest sought to be ensured in the\n      light of the object sought to be achieved, the necessity\n      to restrict the citizen&#x27;s freedom, the inherent pernicious\n      nature of the act prohibited or its capacity or tendency\n      to be harmful to the general public, the possibility of\n      achieving the object by imposing a less drastic\n      restraint, and in the absence of exceptional situations\n      such      as    the     prevalence    of   a    state   of\n      emergency-national or local-or the necessity to\n      maintain essential supplies, or the necessity to stop\n      activities inherently dangerous, the existence of a\n      machinery to satisfy the administrative authority that no\n      case for imposing the restriction is made out or that a\n      less drastic restriction may ensure the object intended\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_42\">\f                                 23</span>\n\n      to be achieved.\u201d (at page 161)\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_45\">26.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1321505\" id=\"span_43\">In <a href=\"/doc/1321505/\" id=\"a_66\">Dr. N. B. Khare v. State of Delhi</a>, [1950] S.C.R. 519, a\n\nConstitution Bench also spoke of reasonable restrictions when it\n\ncomes to procedure. It said:</span>\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_46\">      \u201cWhile the reasonableness of the restrictions has to be\n      considered with regard to the exercise of the right, it\n      does not necessarily exclude from the consideration of\n      the Court the question of reasonableness of the\n      procedural part of the law. It is obvious that if the law\n      prescribes five years externment or ten years\n      externment, the question whether such period of\n      externment is reasonable, being the substantive part,\n      is necessarily for the consideration of the court under\n      clause (5). Similarly, if the law provides the procedure\n      under which the exercise of the right may be restricted,\n      the same is also for the consideration of the Court, as\n      it has to determine if the exercise of the right has been\n      reasonably restricted.\u201d (at page 524)\n\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_47\">27.   It was argued by the learned Additional Solicitor General that\n\na relaxed standard of reasonableness of restriction should apply\n\nregard being had to the fact that the medium of speech being the\n\ninternet differs from other mediums on several grounds.           To\n\nappreciate the width and scope of his submissions, we are setting\n\n\nout his written submission verbatim:\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_48\">      \u201c(i) the reach of print media is restricted to one state\n      or at the most one country while internet has no\n      boundaries and its reach is global;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_49\">      (ii)   the recipient of the free speech and expression\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_44\">\f                                    24</span>\n\n         used in a print media can only be literate persons while\n         internet can be accessed by literate and illiterate both\n         since one click is needed to download an objectionable\n         post or a video;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_50\">         (iii) In case of televisions serials [except live shows]\n         and movies, there is a permitted pre- censorship&#x27; which\n         ensures right of viewers not to receive any information\n         which is dangerous to or not in conformity with the\n         social interest. While in the case of an internet, no\n         such pre-censorship is possible and each individual is\n         publisher, printer, producer, director and broadcaster of\n         the content without any statutory regulation;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_51\">(iv)     In case of print media or medium of television and films\n         whatever is truly recorded can only be published or\n         broadcasted I televised I viewed. While in case of an\n         internet, morphing of images, change of voices and\n         many other technologically advance methods to create\n         serious potential social disorder can be applied.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_52\">(v)       By the medium of internet, rumors having a serious\n         potential of creating a serious social disorder can be\n         spread to trillions of people without any check which is\n         not possible in case of other mediums.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_53\">(vi)       In case of mediums like print media, television and\n         films, it is broadly not possible to invade privacy of\n         unwilling persons. While in case of an internet, it is\n         very easy to invade upon the privacy of any individual\n         and thereby violating his right under <a href=\"/doc/1199182/\" id=\"a_67\">Article 21</a> of the\n         Constitution of India.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_54\">(vii)     By its very nature, in the mediums like newspaper,\n         magazine, television or a movie, it is not possible to\n         sexually harass someone, outrage the modesty of\n         anyone, use unacceptable filthy language and evoke\n         communal frenzy which would lead to serious social\n         disorder. While in the case of an internet, it is easily\n         possible to do so by a mere click of a button without\n         any geographical limitations and almost in all cases\n         while ensuring anonymity of the offender.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_55\">(viii)   By the very nature of the medium, the width and reach\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_45\">\f                           25</span>\n\nof internet is manifold as against newspaper and films.\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_43\">The said mediums have inbuilt limitations i.e. a person\nwill have to buy / borrow a newspaper and / or will\nhave to go to a theater to watch a movie. For television\nalso one needs at least a room where a television is\nplaced and can only watch those channels which he\nhas subscribed and that too only at a time where it is\nbeing telecast. While in case of an internet a person\nabusing the internet, can commit an offence at any\nplace at the time of his choice and maintaining his\nanonymity in almost all cases.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_44\">(ix) In case of other mediums, it is impossible to\nmaintain anonymity as a result of which speech ideal\nopinions films having serious potential of creating a\nsocial disorder never gets generated since its origin is\nbound to be known. While in case of an internet mostly\nits abuse takes place under the garb of anonymity\nwhich can be unveiled only after thorough\ninvestigation.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_45\">(x) In case of other mediums like newspapers,\ntelevision or films, the approach is always\ninstitutionalized approach governed by industry\nspecific ethical norms of self conduct. Each newspaper\n/ magazine / movie production house / TV Channel will\nhave their own institutionalized policies in house which\nwould generally obviate any possibility of the medium\nbeing abused. As against that use of internet is solely\nbased upon individualistic approach of each individual\nwithout any check, balance or regulatory ethical norms\nfor exercising freedom of speech and expression under\n<a href=\"/doc/1142233/\" id=\"a_68\">Article 19[ 1] [</a>a].\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_46\">(xi)      In the era limited to print media and\ncinematograph; or even in case of publication through\nairwaves, the chances of abuse of freedom of\nexpression was less due to inherent infrastructural and\nlogistical constrains. In the case of said mediums, it\nwas almost impossible for an individual to create and\npublish an abusive content and make it available to\ntrillions of people. Whereas, in the present internet age\nthe said infrastructural and logistical constrains have\ndisappeared as any individual using even a smart\nmobile phone or a portable computer device can\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_46\">\f                                  26</span>\n\n      create and publish abusive material on its own, without\n      seeking help of anyone else and make it available to\n      trillions of people by just one click.\u201d\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_47\">28.   As stated, all the above factors may make a distinction\n\nbetween the print and other media as opposed to the internet and\n\nthe legislature may well, therefore, provide for separate offences\n\nso far as free speech over the internet is concerned. There is,\n\ntherefore, an intelligible differentia having a rational relation to the\n\nobject sought to be achieved \u2013 that there can be creation of\n\noffences which are applied to free speech over the internet alone\n\nas opposed to other mediums of communication. Thus, an <a href=\"/doc/367586/\" id=\"a_69\">Article\n\n14</a> challenge has been repelled by us on this ground later in this\n\njudgment. But we do not find anything in the features outlined by\n\nthe learned Additional Solicitor General to relax the Court\u2019s\n\nscrutiny of the curbing of the content of free speech over the\n\ninternet.   While it may be possible to narrowly draw a Section\n\ncreating a new offence, such as <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_70\">Section 69A</a> for instance, relatable\n\nonly to speech over the internet, yet the validity of such a law will\n\nhave to be tested on the touchstone of the tests already indicated\n\nabove.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_48\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_49\">29.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"539407\" id=\"span_47\">In fact, this aspect was considered in <a href=\"/doc/539407/\" id=\"a_71\">Secretary Ministry of\n\nInformation &amp; Broadcasting, Government of India v. Cricket\n\nAssociation of Bengal</a>, (1995) 2 SCC 161 in para 37, where the\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_48\">\f                                27</span>\n\nfollowing question was posed:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_56\">     \u201cThe next question which is required to be answered is\n     whether there is any distinction between the freedom\n     of the print media and that of the electronic media such\n     as radio and television, and if so, whether it\n     necessitates more restrictions on the latter media.\u201d\n\nThis question was answered in para 78 thus:\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_57\">     \u201cThere is no doubt that since the airwaves/frequencies\n     are a public property and are also limited, they have to\n     be used in the best interest of the society and this can\n     be done either by a central authority by establishing its\n     own broadcasting network or regulating the grant of\n     licences to other agencies, including the private\n     agencies. What is further, the electronic media is the\n     most powerful media both because of its audio-visual\n     impact and its widest reach covering the section of the\n     society where the print media does not reach. The right\n     to use the airwaves and the content of the\n     programmes, therefore, needs regulation for balancing\n     it and as well as to prevent monopoly of information\n     and views relayed, which is a potential danger flowing\n     from     the    concentration     of    the    right   to\n     broadcast/telecast in the hands either of a central\n     agency or of few private affluent broadcasters. That is\n     why the need to have a central agency representative\n     of all sections of the society free from control both of\n     the Government and the dominant influential sections\n     of the society. This is not disputed. But to contend that\n     on that account the restrictions to be imposed on the\n     right under <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_72\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> should be in addition to\n     those permissible under <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_73\">Article 19(2)</a> and dictated by\n     the use of public resources in the best interests of the\n     society at large, is to misconceive both the content of\n     the freedom of speech and expression and the\n     problems posed by the element of public property in,\n     and the alleged scarcity of, the frequencies as well as\n     by the wider reach of the media. If the right to freedom\n     of speech and expression includes the right to\n     disseminate information to as wide a section of the\n     population as is possible, the access which enables\n     the right to be so exercised is also an integral part of\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_49\">\f                            28</span>\n\nthe said right. The wider range of circulation of\ninformation or its greater impact cannot restrict the\ncontent of the right nor can it justify its denial. The\nvirtues of the electronic media cannot become its\nenemies. It may warrant a greater regulation over\nlicensing and control and vigilance on the content of\nthe programme telecast. However, this control can only\nbe exercised within the framework of <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_74\">Article 19(2)</a> and\nthe dictates of public interests. To plead for other\ngrounds is to plead for unconstitutional measures. It is\nfurther difficult to appreciate such contention on the\npart of the Government in this country when they have\na complete control over the frequencies and the\ncontent of the programme to be telecast. They control\nthe sole agency of telecasting. They are also armed\nwith the provisions of <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_75\">Article 19(2)</a> and the powers of\npre-censorship under the <a href=\"/doc/980182/\" id=\"a_76\">Cinematograph Act</a> and\nRules. The only limitation on the said right is, therefore,\nthe limitation of resources and the need to use them\nfor the benefit of all. When, however, there are surplus\nor unlimited resources and the public interests so\ndemand or in any case do not prevent telecasting, the\nvalidity of the argument based on limitation of\nresources disappears. It is true that to own a frequency\nfor the purposes of broadcasting is a costly affair and\neven when there are surplus or unlimited frequencies,\nonly the affluent few will own them and will be in a\nposition to use it to subserve their own interest by\nmanipulating news and views. That also poses a\ndanger to the freedom of speech and expression of the\nhave-nots by denying them the truthful information on\nall sides of an issue which is so necessary to form a\nsound view on any subject. That is why the doctrine of\nfairness has been evolved in the US in the context of\nthe private broadcasters licensed to share the limited\nfrequencies with the central agency like the FCC to\nregulate the programming. But this phenomenon\noccurs even in the case of the print media of all the\ncountries. Hence the body like the Press Council of\nIndia which is empowered to enforce, however\nimperfectly, the right to reply. The print media further\nenjoys as in our country, freedom from pre-censorship\nunlike the electronic media.\u201d\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_50\">\f                                29</span>\n\nPublic Order\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_50\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"456839\" id=\"span_51\">30.   In <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_77\">Article 19(2) (as it originally stood)</a> this sub-head was\n\nconspicuously absent. Because of its absence, challenges made\n\nto an order made under Section 7 of the Punjab Maintenance of\n\nPublic Order Act and to an order made under Section 9 (1)(a) of\n\nthe Madras Maintenance of Public Order Act were allowed in two\n\nearly judgments by this Court. Thus in <a href=\"/doc/456839/\" id=\"a_78\">Romesh Thappar v. State\n\nof Madras</a>, [1950] S.C.R. 594, this Court held that an order made\n\nunder Section 9(1)(a) of the Madras Maintenance of Public Order\n\nAct (XXIII of 1949) was unconstitutional and void in that it could\n\nnot be justified as a measure connected with security of the State.\n\nWhile dealing with the expression \u201cpublic order\u201d, this Court held\n\nthat \u201cpublic order\u201d is an expression which signifies a state of\n\ntranquility which prevails amongst the members of a political\n\nsociety as a result of the internal regulations enforced by the\n\nGovernment which they have established.</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_51\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_52\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"43023\" id=\"span_52\">31.   Similarly, in <a href=\"/doc/43023/\" id=\"a_79\">Brij Bhushan &amp; Anr. v. State of Delhi</a>, [1950]\n\nS.C.R. 605, an order made under Section 7 of the East Punjab\n\nPublic Safety Act, 1949, was held to be unconstitutional and void\n\nfor the self-same reason.</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_53\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_54\">32.   As an aftermath of these judgments, the Constitution First\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_53\">\f                                 30</span>\n\nAmendment added the words \u201cpublic order\u201d to <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_80\">Article 19(2).</a>\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_55\">33.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1386353\" id=\"span_54\">In <a href=\"/doc/1386353/\" id=\"a_81\">Superintendent, Central Prison, Fatehgarh v. Ram\n\nManohar Lohia</a>, [1960] 2 S.C.R. 821, this Court held that public\n\norder is synonymous with public safety and tranquility; it is the\n\nabsence of disorder involving breaches of local significance in\n\ncontradistinction to national upheavals, such as revolution, civil\n\nstrife, war, affecting the security of the State.</span> <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1733535\" id=\"span_55\">This definition was\n\nfurther refined in <a href=\"/doc/1733535/\" id=\"a_82\">Dr. Ram Manohar Lohia v. State of Bihar &amp;\n\nOrs.</a>, [1966] 1 S.C.R. 709, where this Court held:</span>\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_58\">      \u201cIt will thus appear that just as &quot;public order&quot; in the\n      rulings of this Court (earlier cited) was said to\n      comprehend disorders of less gravity than those\n      affecting &quot;security of State&quot;, &quot;law and order&quot; also\n      comprehends disorders of less gravity than those\n      affecting &quot;public order&quot;. One has to imagine three\n      concentric circles. Law and order represents the\n      largest circle within which is the next circle\n      representing public order and the smallest circle\n      represents security of State. It is then easy to see that\n      an act may affect law and order but not public order\n      just as an act may affect public order but not security of\n      the State.\u201d (at page 746)\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_56\">34.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"720977\" id=\"span_56\">In <a href=\"/doc/720977/\" id=\"a_83\">Arun Ghosh v. State of West Bengal</a>, [1970] 3 S.C.R.\n\n288, Ram Manohar Lohia\u2019s case was referred to with approval in\n\nthe following terms:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_59\">      <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1386353\" id=\"span_57\"><a href=\"/doc/1386353/\" id=\"a_84\">\u201cIn Dr. Ram Manohar Lohia</a>&#x27;s case this Court pointed\n      out the difference between maintenance of law and\n      order and its disturbance and the maintenance of\n      public order and its disturbance. Public order was said\n      to embrace more of the community than law and order.</span>\n</blockquote><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_58\">\f                           31</span>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_60\">Public order is the even tempo of the life of the\ncommunity taking the country as a whole or even a\nspecified locality. Disturbance of public order is to be\ndistinguished, from acts directed against individuals\nwhich do not disturb the society to the extent of\ncausing a general disturbance of public tranquility. It is\nthe degree of disturbance and its effect upon the life of\nthe community in a locality which determines whether\nthe disturbance amounts only to a breach of law and\norder. Take for instance, a man stabs another. People\nmay be shocked and even disturbed, but the life of the\ncommunity keeps moving at an even tempo, however\nmuch one may dislike the act. Take another case of a\ntown where there is communal tension. A man stabs a\nmember of the other community. This is an act of a\nvery different sort. Its implications are deeper and it\naffects the even tempo of life and public order is\njeopardized because the repercussions of the act\nembrace large Sections of the community and incite\nthem to make further breaches of the law and order\nand to subvert the public order. An act by itself is not\ndeterminant of its own gravity. In its quality it may not\ndiffer from another but in its potentiality it may be very\ndifferent. Take the case of assault on girls. A guest at a\nhotel may kiss or make advances to half a dozen\nchamber maids. He may annoy them and also the\nmanagement but he does not cause disturbance of\npublic order. He may even have a fracas with the\nfriends of one of the girls but even then it would be a\ncase of breach of law and order only. Take another\ncase of a man who molests women in lonely places. As\na result of his activities girls going to colleges and\nschools are in constant danger and fear. Women going\nfor their ordinary business are afraid of being waylaid\nand assaulted. The activity of this man in its essential\nquality is not different from the act of the other man but\nin its potentiality and in its effect upon the public\ntranquility there is a vast difference. The act of the man\nwho molests the girls in lonely places causes a\ndisturbance in the even tempo of living which is the\nfirst requirement of public order. He disturbs the\nsociety and the community. His act makes all the\nwomen apprehensive of their honour and he can be\nsaid to be causing disturbance of public order and not\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_59\">\f                                  32</span>\n\n      merely committing individual actions which may be\n      taken note of by the criminal prosecution agencies. It\n      means therefore that the question whether a man has\n      only committed a breach of law and order or has acted\n      in a manner likely to cause a disturbance of the public\n      order is a question of degree and the extent of the\n      reach of the act upon the society. The French\n      distinguish law and order and public order by\n      designating the latter as order publique. The latter\n      expression has been recognised as meaning\n      something more than ordinary maintenance of law and\n      order. Justice Ramaswami in Writ Petition No. 179 of\n      1968 drew a line of demarcation between the serious\n      and aggravated forms of breaches of public order\n      which affect the community or endanger the public\n      interest at large from minor breaches of peace which\n      do not affect the public at large. He drew an analogy\n      between public and private crimes. The analogy is\n      useful but not to be pushed too far. A large number of\n      acts directed against persons or individuals may total\n      up into a breach of public order. <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1386353\" id=\"span_60\">In <a href=\"/doc/1386353/\" id=\"a_85\">Dr. Ram Manohar\n      Lohia</a>&#x27;s case examples were given by Sarkar, and\n      Hidayatullah, JJ. They show how similar acts in\n      different contexts affect differently law and order on the\n      one hand and public order on the other. It is always a\n      question of degree of the harm and its effect upon the\n      community. The question to ask is: Does it lead to\n      disturbance of the current of life of the community so\n      as to amount to a disturbance of the public order or\n      does it affect merely an individual leaving the tranquility\n      of the society undisturbed? This question has to be\n      faced in every case on facts. There is no formula by\n      which one case can be distinguished from another.\u201d (at\n      pages 290 and 291).</span>\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_57\">35.   This decision lays down the test that has to be formulated in\n\nall these cases. We have to ask ourselves the question: does a\n\nparticular act lead to disturbance of the current life of the\n\ncommunity or does it merely affect an individual leaving the\n\ntranquility of society undisturbed? Going by this test, it is clear that\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_61\">\f                                  33</span>\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_86\">Section 66A</a> is intended to punish any person who uses the\n\ninternet to disseminate any information that falls within the\n\nsub-clauses of <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_87\">Section 66A</a>. It will be immediately noticed that the\n\nrecipient of the written word that is sent by the person who is\n\naccused of the offence is not of any importance so far as this\n\nSection is concerned. (Save and except where under sub-clause\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_58\">(c) the addressee or recipient is deceived or misled about the\n\norigin of a particular message.) It is clear, therefore, that the\n\ninformation that is disseminated may be to one individual or\n\nseveral individuals.   The Section makes no distinction between\n\nmass dissemination and dissemination to one person. Further, the\n\nSection does not require that such message should have a clear\n\ntendency to disrupt public order. Such message need not have any\n\npotential which could disturb the community at large. The nexus\n\nbetween the message and action that may be taken based on the\n\nmessage is conspicuously absent \u2013 there is no ingredient in this\n\noffence of inciting anybody to do anything which a reasonable man\n\nwould then say would have the tendency of being an immediate\n\nthreat to public safety or tranquility. On all these counts, it is clear\n\nthat the Section has no proximate relationship to public order\n\nwhatsoever. The example of a guest at a hotel `annoying\u2019 girls is\n\ntelling \u2013 this Court has held that mere `annoyance\u2019 need not cause\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_62\">\f                                 34</span>\n\ndisturbance of public order. Under <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_88\">Section 66A</a>, the offence is\n\ncomplete by sending a message for the purpose of causing\n\nannoyance, either `persistently\u2019 or otherwise without in any\n\nmanner impacting public order.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_59\">Clear and present danger \u2013 tendency to affect.\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_60\">36.   It will be remembered that Justice Holmes in Schenck v.\n\nUnited States, 63 L. Ed. 470 enunciated the clear and present\n\ndanger test as follows:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_61\">      \u201c\u2026The most stringent protection of free speech would\n      not protect a man in falsely shouting fire in a theatre\n      and causing a panic. It does not even protect a man\n      from an injunction against uttering words that may\n      have all the effect of force. Gompers v. Buck&#x27;s Stove &amp;\n      Range Co., 221 U. S. 418, 439, <a href=\"/doc/63476/\" id=\"a_89\">31</a> Sup. Ct. 492, 55 L.\n      ed. 797, 34 L. R. A. (N. S.) 874. The question in every\n      case is whether the words used are used in such\n      circumstances and are of such a nature as to create a\n      clear and present danger that they will bring about the\n      substantive evils that Congress has a right to prevent.\n      It is a question of proximity and degree.\u201d (At page 473,\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_62\">      474)\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_61\">37.   This was further refined in Abrams v. Unites States 250\n\nU.S. 616 (1919), this time in a Holmesian dissent, to be clear and\n\nimminent danger. However, in most of the subsequent judgments\n\nof the U.S. Supreme Court, the test has been understood to mean\n\nto be \u201cclear and present danger\u201d. The test of \u201cclear and present\n\ndanger\u201d has been used by the U.S. Supreme Court in many\n\nvarying situations and has been adjusted according to varying fact\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_63\">\f                                                            35</span>\n\n         situations.         It appears to have been repeatedly applied, see-\n\n         Terminiello v. City of Chicago 93 L. Ed. 1131 (1949) at page\n\n         1134-1135, Brandenburg v. Ohio 23 L. Ed. 2d 430 (1969) at\n\n         434-435 &amp; 436, Virginia v. Black 155 L. Ed. 2d 535 (2003) at\n\n         page 551, 552 and 5534.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_62\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_63\">         <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"341773\" id=\"span_64\">38.      We have echoes of it in our law as well <a href=\"/doc/341773/\" id=\"a_90\">S. Rangarajan v. P.\n\n         Jagjivan &amp; Ors.</a>, (1989) 2 SCC 574 at paragraph 45:</span>\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_63\">                  \u201c45. The problem of defining the area of freedom of\n                  expression when it appears to conflict with the various\n                  social interests enumerated under <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_91\">Article 19(2)</a> may\n                  briefly be touched upon here. There does indeed have\n                  to be a compromise between the interest of freedom of\n                  expression and special interests. But we cannot simply\n                  balance the two interests as if they are of equal weight.\n                  Our commitment of freedom of expression demands\n                  that it cannot be suppressed unless the situations\n                  created by allowing the freedom are pressing and the\n                  community interest is endangered. The anticipated\n                  danger should not be remote, conjectural or\n                  far-fetched. It should have proximate and direct nexus\n                  with the expression. The expression of thought should\n                  be intrinsically dangerous to the public interest. In\n                  other words, the expression should be inseparably\n                  locked up with the action contemplated like the\n\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_65\">4</span>\n           In its present form the clear and present danger test has been reformulated to say that:\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_64\">          \u201cThe constitutional guarantees of free speech and free press do not permit a State to forbid or proscribe\nadvocacy of the use of force or of law violation except where such advocacy is directed to inciting or producing\nimminent lawless action and is likely to incite or produce such action.\u201d\n          Interestingly, the US Courts have gone on to make a further refinement. The State may ban what is called a\n\u201ctrue threat\u201d.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_65\">          \u201c\u2019True threats\u2019 encompass those statements where the speaker means to communicate a serious expression of an\nintent to commit an act of unlawful violence to a particular individual or group of individuals.\u201d\n          \u201cThe speaker need not actually intend to carry out the threat. Rather, a prohibition on true threats protects\nindividuals from the fear of violence and from the disruption that fear engenders, in addition to protecting people from\nthe possibility that the threatened violence will occur. Intimidation in the constitutionally proscribable sense of the word\nis a type of true threat, where a speaker directs a threat to a person or group of persons with the intent of placing the\nvictim in fear of bodily harm or death.\u201d\n          See Virginia v. Black (Supra) and Watts v. United States 22 L. Ed. 2d. 664 at 667\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_66\">\f                                 36</span>\n\n      equivalent of a \u201cspark in a powder keg\u201d.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_64\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"792995\" id=\"span_67\">39.   This Court has used the expression \u201ctendency\u201d to a particular\n\nact. Thus, in <a href=\"/doc/792995/\" id=\"a_92\">State of Bihar v. Shailabala Devi</a>, [1952] S.C.R.\n\n654, an early decision of this Court said that an article, in order to\n\nbe banned must have a tendency to excite persons to acts of\n\nviolence (at page 662-663). The test <a href=\"/doc/792995/\" id=\"a_93\">laid down in</a> <a href=\"/doc/792995/\" id=\"a_94\">the said decision</a>\n\nwas that the article should be considered as a whole in a fair free\n\nliberal spirit and then it must be decided what effect it would have\n\non the mind of a reasonable reader. (at pages 664-665)</span>\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_65\">40.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"553290\" id=\"span_68\">In <a href=\"/doc/553290/\" id=\"a_95\">Ramji Lal Modi v. The State of U.P.</a>, [1957] S.C.R. 860 at\n\npage 867, this court upheld <a href=\"/doc/1803184/\" id=\"a_96\">Section 295A</a> of the Indian Penal Code\n\nonly because it was read down to mean that aggravated forms of\n\ninsults to religion must have a tendency to disrupt public order.</span>\n\n<span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"111867\" id=\"span_69\">Similarly, in <a href=\"/doc/111867/\" id=\"a_97\">Kedar Nath Singh v. State of Bihar</a>, 1962 Supp. (2)\n\nS.C.R. 769, <a href=\"/doc/1641007/\" id=\"a_98\">Section 124A</a> of the Indian Penal Code was upheld by\n\nconstruing it narrowly and stating that the offence would only be\n\ncomplete if the words complained of have a tendency of creating\n\npublic disorder by violence. It was added that merely creating\n\ndisaffection or creating feelings of enmity in certain people was not\n\ngood enough or else it would violate the fundamental right of free\n\nspeech under <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_99\">Article 19(1)(a).</a></span> <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"925631\" id=\"span_70\">Again, in <a href=\"/doc/925631/\" id=\"a_100\">Dr. Ramesh Yeshwant\n\nPrabhoo v. Prabhakar Kashinath Kunte &amp; Ors.</a>, 1996 (1) SCC\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_71\">\f                                37</span>\n\n130, <a href=\"/doc/55081742/\" id=\"a_101\">Section 123 (3A)</a> of the Representation of People Act was\n\nupheld only if the enmity or hatred that was spoken about in the\n\nSection would tend to create immediate public disorder and not\n\notherwise.</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_66\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_67\">41.   Viewed at either by the standpoint of the clear and present\n\ndanger test or the tendency to create public disorder, <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_102\">Section 66A</a>\n\nwould not pass muster as it has no element of any tendency to\n\ncreate public disorder which ought to be an essential ingredient of\n\nthe offence which it creates.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_68\">\nDefamation\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_69\">42.   Defamation is defined in Section 499 of the Penal Code as\n\nfollows:\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_70\">\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_66\">      \u201c499. Defamation.\u2014Whoever, by words either spoken\n      or intended to be read, or by signs or by visible\n      representations, makes or publishes any imputation\n      concerning any person intending to harm, or knowing\n      or having reason to believe that such imputation will\n      harm, the reputation of such person, is said, except in\n      the cases hereinafter excepted, to defame that person.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_67\">      Explanation 1.\u2014It may amount to defamation to impute\n      anything to a deceased person, if the imputation would\n      harm the reputation of that person if living, and is\n      intended to be hurtful to the feelings of his family or\n      other near relatives.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_68\">      Explanation 2.\u2014It may amount to defamation to make\n      an imputation concerning a company or an association\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_72\">\f                                  38</span>\n\n      or collection of persons as such.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_69\">      Explanation 3.\u2014An imputation in the form of an\n      alternative or expressed ironically, may amount to\n      defamation.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_70\">      Explanation 4.\u2014No imputation is said to harm a\n      person&#x27;s reputation, unless that imputation directly or\n      indirectly, in the estimation of others, lowers the moral\n      or intellectual character of that person, or lowers the\n      character of that person in respect of his caste or of his\n      calling, or lowers the credit of that person, or causes it\n      to be believed that the body of that person is in a\n      loathsome state, or in a state generally considered as\n      disgraceful.\u201d\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_71\">43.   It will be noticed that for something to be defamatory, injury\n\nto reputation is a basic ingredient. <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_103\">Section 66A</a> does not concern\n\nitself with injury to reputation. Something may be grossly offensive\n\nand may annoy or be inconvenient to somebody without at all\n\naffecting his reputation. It is clear therefore that the Section is not\n\naimed at defamatory statements at all.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_72\">\nIncitement to an offence:\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_73\">44.   Equally, <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_104\">Section 66A</a> has no proximate connection with\n\nincitement to commit an offence.             Firstly, the information\n\ndisseminated over the internet need not be information which\n\n\u201cincites\u201d anybody at all. Written words may be sent that may be\n\npurely in the realm of \u201cdiscussion\u201d or \u201cadvocacy\u201d of a \u201cparticular\n\npoint of view\u201d.      Further, the mere causing of annoyance,\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_73\">\f                                 39</span>\n\ninconvenience, danger etc., or being grossly offensive or having a\n\nmenacing character are not offences under the Penal Code at all.\n\nThey may be ingredients of certain offences under the Penal Code\n\nbut are not offences in themselves. For these reasons, <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_105\">Section\n\n66A</a> has nothing to do with \u201cincitement to an offence\u201d. As <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_106\">Section\n\n66A</a> severely curtails information that may be sent on the internet\n\nbased on whether it is grossly offensive, annoying, inconvenient,\n\netc. and being unrelated to any of the eight subject matters under\n\n<a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_107\">Article 19(2)</a> must, therefore, fall foul of <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_108\">Article 19(1)(a)</a>, and not\n\nbeing saved under <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_109\">Article 19(2)</a>, is declared as unconstitutional.\n\n\nDecency or Morality\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_74\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1623275\" id=\"span_74\">45.   This Court in <a href=\"/doc/1623275/\" id=\"a_110\">Ranjit Udeshi v. State of Maharashtra</a> [1965]\n\n1 S.C.R. 65 took a rather restrictive view of what would pass\n\nmuster as not being obscene. The Court followed the test <a href=\"/doc/1623275/\" id=\"a_111\">laid\n\ndown in</a> the old English judgment in Hicklin\u2019s case which was\n\nwhether the tendency of the matter charged as obscene is to\n\ndeprave and corrupt those whose minds are open to such immoral\n\ninfluences and into whose hands a publication of this sort may fall.\n\nGreat strides have been made since this decision in the UK,\n\nUnited States as well as in our country.</span>         <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"206282\" id=\"span_75\">Thus, in <a href=\"/doc/206282/\" id=\"a_112\">Director\n\nGeneral, Directorate General of Doordarshan               v. Anand\n\nPatwardhan</a>, 2006 (8) SCC 433, this Court noticed the law in the\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_76\">\f                                  40</span>\n\nUnited States and said that a material may be regarded as\n\nobscene if the average person applying contemporary community\n\nstandards would find that the subject matter taken as a whole\n\nappeals to the prurient interest and that taken as a whole it\n\notherwise lacks serious literary artistic, political, educational or\n\nscientific value (see Para 31).</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_75\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_76\">46.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"195958005\" id=\"span_77\">In a recent judgment of this Court, <a href=\"/doc/195958005/\" id=\"a_113\">Aveek Sarkar v. State of\n\nWest Bengal</a>, 2014 (4) SCC 257, this Court referred to English,\n\nU.S. and Canadian judgments and moved away from the Hicklin\n\ntest and applied the contemporary community standards test.</span>\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_77\">47.   What has been said with regard to public order and\n\nincitement to an offence equally applies here. <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_114\">Section 66A</a> cannot\n\npossibly be said to create an offence which falls within the\n\nexpression \u2018decency\u2019 or \u2018morality\u2019 in that what may be grossly\n\noffensive or annoying under the Section need not be obscene at\n\nall \u2013 in fact the word \u2018obscene\u2019 is conspicuous by its absence in\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_115\">Section 66A</a>.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_78\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_79\">48.   However, the learned Additional Solicitor General asked us\n\nto read into <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_116\">Section 66A</a> each of the subject matters contained in\n\n<a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_117\">Article 19(2)</a> in order to save the constitutionality of the provision.\n\nWe are afraid that such an exercise is not possible for the simple\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_78\">\f                                41</span>\n\nreason that when the legislature intended to do so, it provided for\n\nsome of the subject matters contained in <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_118\">Article 19(2)</a> in <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_119\">Section\n\n69A</a>. We would be doing complete violence to the language of\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_120\">Section 66A</a> if we were to read into it something that was never\n\nintended to be read into it. Further, he argued that the statute\n\nshould be made workable, and the following should be read into\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_121\">Section 66A</a>:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_71\">     \u201c(i) Information which would appear highly abusive,\n     insulting, pejorative, offensive by reasonable person in\n     general, judged by the standards of an open and just\n     multi-caste, multi-religious, multi racial society;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_72\">     - Director of Public Prosecutions v. Collins \u2013\n       (2006) 1 WLR 2223 @ para 9 and 21\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_73\">     - Connolly v. Director of Public Prosecutions\n       reported in [2008] 1 W.L.R. 276/2007 [1] All ER\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_79\">       1012</span>\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_74\">     - House of Lords Select Committee 1 st Report of\n       Session 2014-2015 on Communications titled as\n       \u201cSocial Media And Criminal Offences\u201d @ pg 260 of\n       compilation of judgments Vol I Part B\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_75\">     (ii) Information which is directed to incite or can\n     produce imminent lawless action Brandenburg v.\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_80\">     Ohio 395 U.S. 444 (1969);\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_81\">     (iii) Information which may constitute credible threats of\n     violence to the person or damage;\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_82\">     (iv) Information which stirs the public to anger, invites\n     violent disputes brings about condition of violent unrest\n     and disturbances;\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_83\">     Terminiello v. Chicago 337 US 1 (1949)\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_84\">     (v) Information which advocates or teaches the duty,\n     necessity or proprietary of violence as a means of\n     accomplishing political, social or religious reform\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_80\">\f                          42</span>\n\nand/or justifies commissioning of violent acts with an\nintent to exemplify glorify such violent means to\naccomplish political, social, economical or religious\nreforms\n[Whitney vs. California 274 US 357];\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_85\">(vi) Information which contains fighting or abusive\nmaterial;\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_86\">Chaplinsky v. New Hampshire, 315 U.S. 568 (1942)\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_87\">(vii) Information which promotes hate speech i.e.\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_88\">  (a)Information which propagates hatred towards\n     individual or a groups, on the basis of race,\n     religion, religion, casteism, ethnicity,\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_89\">  (b)Information which is intended to show the\n     supremacy of one particular religion/race/caste\n     by making disparaging, abusive and/or highly\n     inflammatory remarks against religion/race/caste.\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_90\">  (c) Information depicting religious deities, holy\n      persons, holy symbols, holy books which are\n      created to insult or to show contempt or lack of\n      reverence for such religious deities, holy\n      persons, holy symbols, holy books or towards\n      something which is considered sacred or\n      inviolable.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_91\">(viii) Satirical or iconoclastic cartoon and caricature\nwhich fails the test laid down in Hustler Magazine,\nInc. v. Falwell 485 U.<a href=\"/doc/1076139/\" id=\"a_122\">S. 46 (1988)</a>\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_92\">(ix) Information which glorifies terrorism and use of\ndrugs;\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_93\">(x) Information which infringes right of privacy of the\nothers and includes acts of cyber bullying, harassment\nor stalking.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_94\">(xi) Information which is obscene and has the tendency\nto arouse feeling or revealing an overt sexual desire\nand should be suggestive of deprave mind and\ndesigned to excite sexual passion in persons who are\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_81\">\f                                   43</span>\n\n        likely to see it.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_95\">        <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"195958005\" id=\"span_82\"><a href=\"/doc/195958005/\" id=\"a_123\">Aveek Sarkar and Anr. vs. State of West Bengal\n        and Ors.</a> (2014) 4 SCC 257.</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_96\">        <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"195958005\" id=\"span_83\">(xii) Context and background test of obscenity.\n        Information which is posted in such a context or\n        background which has a consequential effect of\n        outraging the modesty of the pictured individual.\n        <a href=\"/doc/195958005/\" id=\"a_124\">Aveek Sarkar and Anr. vs. State of West Bengal\n        and Ors.</a> (2014) 4 SCC 257.\u201d</span>\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_97\">  49.   What the learned Additional Solicitor General is asking us to\n\n  do is not to read down <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_125\">Section 66A</a> \u2013 he is asking for a wholesale\n\n  substitution of the provision which is obviously not possible.\n\n\n  Vagueness\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_98\">  50.   Counsel for the petitioners argued that the language used in\n\n  <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_126\">Section 66A</a> is so vague that neither would an accused person be\n\n  put on notice as to what exactly is the offence which has been\n\n  committed nor would the authorities administering the Section be\n\n  clear as to on which side of a clearly drawn line a particular\n\n  communication will fall.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_99\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_100\">  51.   We were given Collin\u2019s dictionary, which defined most of the\n\n  terms used in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_127\">Section 66A</a>, as follows:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_76\"> \u201cOffensive:-\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_77\">1. Unpleasant or disgusting, as to the senses\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_78\">2. Causing anger or annoyance; insulting\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_79\">3. For the purpose of attack rather than defence.\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_101\"> Menace:-\n</p><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_84\">\f                                      44</span><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_102\">\n\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_80\">1.   To threaten with violence, danger, etc.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_81\">2.   A threat of the act of threatening\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_82\">3.   Something menacing; a source of danger\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_83\">4.   A nuisance\n     Annoy:-\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_84\">1. To irritate or displease\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_85\">2. To harass with repeated attacks\n\n     Annoyance\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_86\">1. The feeling of being annoyed\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_87\">2. The act of annoying.\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_103\"> Inconvenience\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_104\">1. The state of quality of being inconvenient\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_105\">2. Something inconvenient; a hindrance, trouble, or difficulty\n Danger:-\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_88\">1. The state of being vulnerable to injury, loss, or evil risk\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_89\">2. A person or a thing that may cause injury pain etc.\n Obstruct:-\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_90\">1. To block (a road a passageway, etc.) with an obstacle\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_91\">2. To make (progress or activity) difficult.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_92\">3. To impede or block a clear view of.\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_106\"> Obstruction:- a person or a thing that obstructs.\n\n Insult:-\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_93\">1. To treat, mention, or speak to rudely; offend; affront\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_94\">2. To assault; attack\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_95\">3. An offensive or contemptuous remark or action; affront; slight\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_96\">4. A person or thing producing the effect of an affront = some\n   television is an insult to intelligence\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_97\">5. An injury or trauma.\u201d\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_98\">     52.    The U.S. Supreme Court has repeatedly held in a series of\n\n     judgments that where no reasonable standards are laid down to\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_85\">\f                                 45</span>\n\ndefine guilt in a Section which creates an offence, and where no\n\nclear guidance is given to either law abiding citizens or to\n\nauthorities and courts, a Section which creates an offence and\n\nwhich is vague must be struck down as being arbitrary and\n\nunreasonable. Thus, in Musser v. Utah, 92 L. Ed. 562, a Utah\n\nstatute which outlawed conspiracy to commit acts injurious to\n\npublic morals was struck down.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_99\">53.   In Winters v. People of State of New York, 92 L. Ed. 840, a\n\nNew York Penal Law read as follows:-\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_100\">      \u201c1141. Obscene prints and articles\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_101\">      1. A person\u2026\u2026who,\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_102\">      2. Prints, utters, publishes, sells, lends, gives away,\n      distributes or shows, or has in his possession with\n      intent to sell, lend, give away, distribute or show, or\n      otherwise offers for sale, loan, gift or distribution, any\n      book, pamphlet, magazine, newspaper or other printed\n      paper devoted to the publication, and principally made\n      up of criminal news, police reports, or accounts of\n      criminal deeds, or pictures, or stories of deeds of\n      bloodshed,             lust           or            crime;\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_107\">      \u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026..\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_108\">\n      &#x27;Is guilty of a misdemeanor, \u2026..&#x27;\u201d (at page 846)\n\n\n\n\nThe court in striking down the said statute held:\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_86\">\f                           46</span>\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_103\">\u201cThe impossibility of defining the precise line between\npermissible uncertainty in statutes caused by\ndescribing crimes by words well understood through\nlong use in the criminal law - obscene, lewd,\nlascivious, filthy, indecent or disgusting\u2014and the\nunconstitutional vagueness that leaves a person\nuncertain as to the kind of prohibited conduct\u2014\nmassing stories to incite crime\u2014has resulted in three\narguments of this case in this Court. The legislative\nbodies in draftsmanship obviously have the same\ndifficulty as do the judicial in interpretation.\nNevertheless despite the difficulties, courts must do\ntheir best to determine whether or not the vagueness is\nof such a character &#x27;that men of common intelligence\nmust necessarily guess at its meaning.&#x27; Connally v.\nGeneral Constr. Co., 269 U.S. 385, 391, <a href=\"/doc/1076139/\" id=\"a_128\">46</a> S.Ct. 126,\n127, 70 L.Ed. 322. The entire text of the statute or the\nsubjects dealt with may furnish an adequate standard.\nThe present case as to a vague statute abridging free\nspeech involves the circulation of only vulgar\nmagazines. The next may call for decision as to free\nexpression of political views in the light of a statute\nintended to punish subversive activities.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_104\">The subsection of the New York Penal Law, as now\ninterpreted by the Court of Appeals prohibits\ndistribution of a magazine principally made up of\ncriminal news or stories of deeds of bloodshed, or lust,\nso massed as to become vehicles for inciting violent\nand depraved crimes against the person. But even\nconsidering the gloss put upon the literal meaning by\nthe Court of Appeals&#x27; restriction of the statute to\ncollections of stories &#x27;so massed as to become\nvehicles for inciting violent and depraved crimes\nagainst the person * * * not necessarily * * * sexual\npassion,&#x27; we find the specification of publications,\nprohibited from distribution, too uncertain and indefinite\nto justify the conviction of this petitioner. Even though\nall detective tales and treatises on criminology are not\nforbidden, and though publications made up of criminal\ndeeds not characterized by bloodshed or lust are\nomitted from the interpretation of the Court of Appeals,\nwe think fair use of collections of pictures and stories\nwould be interdicted because of the utter impossibility\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_87\">\f                                 47</span>\n\n      of the actor or the trier to know where this new\n      standard of guilt would draw the line between the\n      allowable and the forbidden publications. No intent or\n      purpose is required\u2014no indecency or obscenity in any\n      sense heretofore known to the law. &#x27;So massed as to\n      incite to crime&#x27; can become meaningful only by\n      concrete instances. This one example is not enough.\n      The clause proposes to punish the printing and\n      circulation of publications that courts or juries may\n      think influence generally persons to commit crime of\n      violence against the person. No conspiracy to commit\n      a crime is required. See Musser v. State of Utah, 68\n      S.Ct. 397, this Term. It is not an effective notice of new\n      crime. The clause has no technical or common law\n      meaning. Nor can light as to the meaning be gained\n      from the section as a whole or the Article of the Penal\n      Law under which it appears. <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"125596\" id=\"span_88\">As said in the <a href=\"/doc/125596/\" id=\"a_129\">Cohen\n      Grocery Co.</a> case, supra, 255 U.S. at page 89, 41\n      S.Ct. at page 300, 65 L.Ed. 516, 14 A.L.R. 1045:</span>\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_105\">      &#x27;It leaves open, therefore, the widest conceivable\n      inquiry, the scope of which no one can foresee and the\n      result of which no one can foreshadow or adequately\n      guard against.&#x27;\n\n\n      The statute as construed by the Court of Appeals does\n      not limit punishment to the indecent and obscene, as\n      formerly understood. When stories of deeds of\n      bloodshed, such as many in the accused magazines,\n      are massed so as to incite to violent crimes, the statute\n      is violated. it does not seem to us that an honest\n      distributor of publications could know when he might\n      be held to have ignored such a prohibition. Collections\n      of tales of war horrors, otherwise unexceptionable,\n      might well be found to be &#x27;massed&#x27; so as to become\n      &#x27;vehicles for inciting violent and depraved crimes.&#x27;\n      Where a statute is so vague as to make criminal an\n      innocent act, a conviction under it cannot be sustained.\n      Herndon v. Lowry, 301 U.S. 242, 259, <a href=\"/doc/1616195/\" id=\"a_130\">57</a> S.Ct. 732,\n      739, 81 L.Ed. 1066.\u201d (at page 851-852)\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_106\">54.   In Burstyn v. Wilson, 96 L. Ed. 1098, sacrilegious writings\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_89\">\f                                  48</span>\n\nand utterances were outlawed. Here again, the U.S. Supreme\n\nCourt stepped in to strike down the offending Section stating:\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_107\">      \u201cIt is not a sufficient answer to say that &#x27;sacrilegious&#x27; is\n      definite, because all subjects that in any way might be\n      interpreted as offending the religious beliefs of any one\n      of the 300 sects of the United States are banned in\n      New York. To allow such vague, undefinable powers of\n      censorship to be exercised is bound to have stultifying\n      consequences on the creative process of literature and\n      art\u2014for the films are derived largely from literature.\n      History does not encourage reliance on the wisdom\n      and moderation of the censor as a safeguard in the\n      exercise of such drastic power over the minds of men.\n      We not only do not know but cannot know what is\n      condemnable by &#x27;sacrilegious.&#x27; And if we cannot tell,\n      how are those to be governed by the statute to tell? (at\n      page 1121)\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_108\">55.   In City of Chicago v. Morales et al, 527 U.<a href=\"/doc/1215878/\" id=\"a_131\">S. 41 (1999)</a>, a\n\nChicago Gang Congregation Ordinance prohibited criminal street\n\ngang members from loitering with one another or with other\n\npersons in any public place for no apparent purpose. The Court\n\nreferred to an earlier judgment in United States v. Reese 92 U.S.\n\n214 (1875) at 221 in which it was stated that the Constitution does\n\nnot permit a legislature to set a net large enough to catch all\n\npossible offenders and leave it to the Court to step in and say who\n\ncould be rightfully detained and who should be set at liberty. It was\n\nheld that the broad sweep of the Ordinance violated the\n\nrequirement that a legislature needs to meet: to establish minimum\n\nguidelines to govern law enforcement. As the impugned Ordinance\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_90\">\f                                  49</span>\n\ndid not have any such guidelines, a substantial amount of innocent\n\nconduct would also be brought within its net, leading to its\n\nunconstitutionality.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_109\">56.   It was further held that a penal law is void for vagueness if it\n\nfails to define the criminal offence with sufficient definiteness.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_109\">Ordinary people should be able to understand what conduct is\n\nprohibited and what is permitted. Also, those who administer the\n\nlaw must know what offence has been committed so that arbitrary\n\nand discriminatory enforcement of the law does not take place.\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_110\">57.   Similarly, in Grayned v. City of Rockford, 33 L.Ed. 2d. 222,\n\nthe State of Illinois provided in an anti noise ordinance as follows:\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_110\">      \u201c&#x27;(N)o person, while on public or private grounds\n      adjacent to any building in which a school or any class\n      thereof is in session, shall willfully make or assist in the\n      making of any noise or diversion which disturbs or\n      tends to disturb the peace or good order of such\n      school session or class thereof. . . .&#x27; Code of\n      Ordinances, c. 28, \u00a7 19.2(a).\u201d\n\n      The law on the subject of vagueness was clearly stated thus:\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_111\">      \u201cIt is a basic principle of due process that an\n      enactment is void for vagueness if its prohibitions are\n      not clearly defined. Vague laws offend several\n      important values. First, because we assume that man\n      is free to steer between lawful and unlawful conduct,\n      we insist that laws give the person of ordinary\n      intelligence a reasonable opportunity to know what is\n      prohibited, so that he may act accordingly. Vague laws\n      may trap the innocent by not providing fair warning.\n      Second, if arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement is\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_91\">\f                                 50</span>\n\n      to be prevented, laws must provide explicit standards\n      for those who apply them. A vague law impermissibly\n      delegates basic policy matters to policemen, judges,\n      and juries for resolution on an ad hoc and subjective\n      basis, with the attendant dangers of arbitrary and\n      discriminatory application. Third, but related, where a\n      vague statute &#x27;abut(s) upon sensitive areas of basic\n      First Amendment freedoms, it \u2018operates to inhibit the\n      exercise of (those) freedoms.\u2019 Uncertain meanings\n      inevitably lead citizens to &quot;steer far wider of the\n      unlawful zone&#x27; . . . than if the boundaries of the\n      forbidden areas were clearly marked.&#x27;\u201d(at page\n      227-228)\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_111\">58.   The anti noise ordinance was upheld on facts in that case\n\nbecause it fixed the time at which noise disrupts school activity \u2013\n\nwhile the school is in session \u2013 and at a fixed place \u2013 \u2018adjacent\u2019 to\n\nthe school.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_112\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_113\">59.   Secondly, there had to be demonstrated a causality between\n\ndisturbance that occurs and the noise or diversion. Thirdly, acts\n\nhave to be willfully done. It is important to notice that the Supreme\n\nCourt specifically held that \u201cundesirables\u201d or their \u201cannoying\n\nconduct\u201d may not be punished. It is only on these limited grounds\n\nthat the said Ordinance was considered not to be impermissibly\n\nvague.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_114\">\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_115\">60.   In Reno, Attorney General of the United States, et al. v.\n\nAmerican Civil Liberties Union et al., 521 U.S. 844 (1997), two\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_92\">\f                                51</span>\n\nprovisions of the Communications Decency Act of 1996 which\n\nsought to protect minors from harmful material on the internet were\n\nadjudged unconstitutional. This judgment is a little important for\n\ntwo basic reasons \u2013 that it deals with a penal offence created for\n\npersons who use the internet as also for the reason that the\n\nstatute which was adjudged unconstitutional uses the expression\n\n\u201cpatently offensive\u201d   which comes extremely close          to   the\n\nexpression \u201cgrossly offensive\u201d used by the impugned <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_132\">Section 66A</a>.\n\nSection 223(d), which was adjudged unconstitutional, is set out\n\nhereinbelow:-\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_116\">\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_112\">     \u201c223 (d) Whoever\u2014\n\n\n     \u201c(1) in interstate or foreign communications knowingly\u2014\n\n\n     (A) uses an interactive computer service to send to a\n     specific person or persons under 18 years of age, or\n\n\n     (B) uses any interactive computer service to display in\n     a manner available to a person under 18 years of age,\n     \u201cany comment, request, suggestion, proposal, image,\n     or other communication that, in context, depicts or\n     describes, in terms patently offensive as measured by\n     contemporary community standards, sexual or\n     excretory activities or organs, regardless of whether\n     the user of such service placed the call or initiated the\n     communication; or\n\n\n     (2) knowingly permits any telecommunications facility\n     under such person&#x27;s control to be used for an activity\n     prohibited by paragraph (1) with the intent that it be\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_93\">\f                                   52</span>\n\n        used for such activity,\n\n\n        shall be fined under Title 18, or imprisoned not more\n        than two years, or both.\u201d (at page 860)\n\n\n        Interestingly, the District Court Judge writing of the internet\n\nsaid:\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_113\">        \u201c[i]t is no exaggeration to conclude that the Internet has\n        achieved, and continues to achieve, the most\n        participatory marketplace of mass speech that this\n        country \u2013 and indeed the world \u2013 as yet seen. The\n        plaintiffs in these actions correctly describe the\n        \u2018democratizing\u2019 effects of Internet communication:\n        individual citizens of limited means can speak to a\n        worldwide audience on issues of concern to them.\n        Federalists and Anti-federalists may debate the\n        structure of their government nightly, but these debates\n        occur in newsgroups or chat rooms rather than in\n        pamphlets. Modern-day Luthers still post their theses,\n        but to electronic bulletins boards rather than the door\n        of the Wittenberg Schlosskirche. More mundane (but\n        from a constitutional perspective, equally important)\n        dialogue occurs between aspiring artists, or French\n        cooks, or dog lovers, or fly fishermen.\u201d 929 F. Supp. At\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_114\">        881. (at page 425)\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_117\">61.     The Supreme Court held that the impugned statute lacked\n\nthe precision that the first amendment required when a statute\n\nregulates the content of speech. In order to deny minors access to\n\npotentially    harmful   speech,    the   impugned     Act   effectively\n\nsuppresses a large amount of speech that adults have a\n\nconstitutional right to receive and to address to one another.\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_118\">62.     Such a burden on adult speech is unacceptable if less\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_94\">\f                                  53</span>\n\nrestrictive alternatives would be as effective in achieving the\n\nlegitimate purpose that the statute was enacted to serve. It was\n\nheld that the general undefined term \u201cpatently offensive\u201d covers\n\nlarge amounts of         non-pornographic material with serious\n\neducational or other value and was both vague and over broad.\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_119\">      It was, thus, held that the impugned statute was not narrowly\n\ntailored and would fall foul of the first amendment.\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_120\">63.   In   Federal    Communications        Commission        v.    Fox\n\nTelevision Stations, 132 S.Ct. 2307, it was held:\n\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_115\">      \u201cA fundamental principle in our legal system is that\n      laws which regulate persons or entities must give fair\n      notice of conduct that is forbidden or required.\n      See Connally v. General Constr. Co., 269 U. S. 385,\n      391 (1926) (\u201c[A] statute which either forbids or requires\n      the doing of an act in terms so vague that men of\n      common intelligence must necessarily guess at its\n      meaning and differ as to its application, violates the\n      first essential of due process of law\u201d); Papachristou v.\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_121\">      Jacksonville, 405 U. S. 156, 162 (1972) (\u201cLiving under\n      a rule of law entails various suppositions, one of which\n      is that \u2018[all persons] are entitled to be informed as to\n      what      the     State     commands       or     forbids\u2019\u201d\n      (quoting Lanzetta v. New Jersey, 306 U. S. 451, 453\n      (1939) (alteration in original))). This requirement of\n      clarity in regulation is essential to the protections\n      provided by the Due Process Clause of the Fifth\n      Amendment. See United States v. Williams, 553 U. S.\n      285, 304 (2008). It requires the invalidation of laws that\n      are impermissibly vague. A conviction or punishment\n      fails to comply with due process if the statute or\n      regulation under which it is obtained \u201cfails to provide a\n      person of ordinary intelligence fair notice of what is\n      prohibited, or is so standardless that it authorizes or\n      encourages              seriously           discriminatory\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_95\">\f                                  54</span>\n\n      enforcement.\u201d Ibid. As this Court has explained, a\n      regulation is not vague because it may at times be\n      difficult to prove an incriminating fact but rather\n      because it is unclear as to what fact must be proved.\n      See id., at 306.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_122\">\n      Even when speech is not at issue, the void for\n      vagueness doctrine addresses at least two connected\n      but discrete due process concerns: first, that regulated\n      parties should know what is required of them so they\n      may act accordingly; second, precision and guidance\n      are necessary so that those enforcing the law do not\n      act in an arbitrary or discriminatory way. See Grayned\n      v. City of Rockford, 408 U. S. 104, 108\u2013109 (1972).\n      When speech is involved, rigorous adherence to those\n      requirements is necessary to ensure that ambiguity\n      does not chill protected speech.\u201d(at page 2317)\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_123\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"882909\" id=\"span_96\">64.   Coming to this Court\u2019s judgments, in <a href=\"/doc/882909/\" id=\"a_133\">State of Madhya\n\nPradesh v. Baldeo Prasad</a>, [1961] 1 S.C.R. 970 an inclusive\n\ndefinition of the word \u201cgoonda\u201d was held to be vague and the\n\noffence created by <a href=\"/doc/28632144/\" id=\"a_134\">Section 4A</a> of the Goondas Act was, therefore,\n\nviolative of <a href=\"/doc/1024002/\" id=\"a_135\">Article 19(1)(d)</a> and (e) of the Constitution.       It was\n\nstated:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_116\">      \u201cIncidentally it would also be relevant to point out that\n      the definition of the word &quot;goonda&quot; affords no\n      assistance in deciding which citizen can be put under\n      that category. It is an inclusive definition and it does not\n      indicate which tests have to be applied in deciding\n      whether a person falls in the first part of the definition.\n      Recourse to the dictionary meaning of the word would\n      hardly be of any assistance in this matter. After all it\n      must be borne in mind that the Act authorises the\n      District Magistrate to deprive a citizen of his\n      fundamental right under <a href=\"/doc/1024002/\" id=\"a_136\">Art. 19(1)(d)</a> and (e), and\n      though the object of the Act and its purpose would\n      undoubtedly attract the provisions of <a href=\"/doc/1801593/\" id=\"a_137\">Art. 19(5)</a> care\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_97\">\f                             55</span>\n\nmust always be taken in passing such acts that they\nprovide sufficient safeguards against casual, capricious\nor even malicious exercise of the powers conferred by\nthem. It is well known that the relevant provisions of\nthe Act are initially put in motion against a person at a\nlower level than the District magistrate, and so it is\nalways necessary that sufficient safeguards should be\nprovided by the Act to protect the fundamental rights of\ninnocent citizens and to save them from unnecessary\nharassment. That is why we think the definition of the\nword &quot;goonda&quot; should have given necessary\nassistance to the District Magistrate in deciding\nwhether a particular citizen falls under the category of\ngoonda or not; that is another infirmity in the Act.
