As we\nhave already pointed out s. 4-A suffers from the same\ninfirmities as<a href=\"/doc/492974/\" id=\"a_138\"> s. 4</a>.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_117\">Having regard to the two infirmities in <a href=\"/doc/492974/\" id=\"a_139\">Sections 4</a>, 4-A\nrespectively we do not think it would be possible to\naccede to the argument of the Learned\nAdvocate-General that the operative portion of the Act\ncan fall under <a href=\"/doc/1801593/\" id=\"a_140\">Art. 19(5)</a> of the Constitution. The person\nagainst whom action can be taken under the Act is not\nentitled to know the source of the information received\nby the District Magistrate; he is only told about his\nprejudicial activities on which the satisfaction of the\nDistrict Magistrate is based that action should be taken\nagainst him under<a href=\"/doc/492974/\" id=\"a_141\"> s.4</a> or s. 4-A. In such a case it is\nabsolutely essential that the Act must clearly indicate\nby a proper definition or otherwise when and under\nwhat circumstances a person can be called a goonda,\nand it must impose an obligation on the District\nMagistrate to apply his mind to the question as to\nwhether the person against whom complaints are\nreceived is such a goonda or not. It has been urged\nbefore us that such an obligation is implicit in\n<a href=\"/doc/492974/\" id=\"a_142\">Sections 4</a> and 4-A. We are, however, not impressed\nby this argument. Where a statute empowers the\nspecified authorities to take preventive action against\nthe citizens it is essential that it should expressly make\nit a part of the duty of the said authorities to satisfy\nthemselves about the existence of what the statute\nregards as conditions precedent to the exercise of the\nsaid authority. If the statute is silent in respect of one of\nsuch conditions precedent it undoubtedly constitutes a\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_98\">\f                                   56</span>\n\n      serious infirmity which would inevitably take it out of\n      the provisions of <a href=\"/doc/1801593/\" id=\"a_143\">Art. 19(5).</a> The result of this infirmity is\n      that it has left to the unguided and unfettered discretion\n      of the authority concerned to treat any citizen as a\n      goonda. In other words, the restrictions which it allows\n      to be imposed on the exercise of the fundamental right\n      of a citizen guaranteed by <a href=\"/doc/1024002/\" id=\"a_144\">Art. 19(1)(d)</a> and (e) must in\n      the circumstances be held to be unreasonable. That is\n      the view taken by the High court and we see no reason\n      to differ from it.\u201d (at pages 979, 980)\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_124\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1719619\" id=\"span_99\">65.   At one time this Court seemed to suggest that the doctrine of\n\nvagueness was no part of the Constitutional Law of India. That\n\nwas dispelled in no uncertain terms in <a href=\"/doc/1719619/\" id=\"a_145\">K.A. Abbas v. The Union\n\nof India &amp; Another</a>, [1971] 2 S.C.R. 446:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_118\">      \u201cThis brings us to the manner of the exercise of control\n      and restriction by the directions. Here the argument is\n      that most of the regulations are vague and further that\n      they leave no scope for the exercise of creative genius\n      in the field of art. This poses the first question before\n      us whether the &#x27;void for vagueness&#x27; doctrine is\n      applicable. Reliance in this connection is placed on\n      Municipal Committee Amritsar and Anr. v. The State of\n      Rajasthan . In that case a Division Bench of this Court\n      lays down that an Indian Act cannot be declared invalid\n      on the ground that it violates the due process clause or\n      that it is vague\u2026\u2026\u201d (at page 469)\n\n      \u201cThese observations which are clearly obiter are apt to\n      be too generally applied and need to be explained.\n      While it is true that the principles evolved by the\n      Supreme Court of the United States of America in the\n      application of the Fourteenth Amendment were\n      eschewed in our Constitution and instead the limits of\n      restrictions on each fundamental right were indicated\n      in the clauses that follow the first clause of the\n      nineteenth article, it cannot be said as an absolute\n      principle that no law will be considered bad for sheer\n      vagueness. There is ample authority for the proposition\n      that a law affecting fundamental rights may be so\n      considered. <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"882909\" id=\"span_100\">A very pertinent example is to be found in\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_101\">\f                                 57</span>\n\n      <a href=\"/doc/882909/\" id=\"a_146\">State of Madhya Pradesh and Anr. v. Baldeo Prasad</a>,\n      1961 (1) SCR 970 where the Central Provinces and\n      Berar Goondas Act 1946 was declared void for\n      uncertainty. The condition for the application of\n      <a href=\"/doc/492974/\" id=\"a_147\">Sections 4</a> and 4A was that the person sought to be\n      proceeded against must be a goonda but the definition\n      of goonda in the Act indicated no tests for deciding\n      which person fell within the definition. The provisions\n      were therefore held to be uncertain and vague.\n        The real rule is that if a law is vague or appears to be\n      so, the court must try to construe it, as far as may be,\n      and language permitting, the construction sought to be\n      placed on it, must be in accordance with the intention\n      of the legislature. Thus if the law is open to diverse\n      construction, that construction which accords best with\n      the intention of the legislature and advances the\n      purpose of legislation, is to be preferred. Where\n      however the law admits of no such construction and\n      the persons applying it are in a boundless sea of\n      uncertainty and the law prima facie takes away a\n      guaranteed freedom, the law must be held to offend\n      the Constitution as was done in the case of the\n      <a href=\"/doc/28632144/\" id=\"a_148\">Goonda Act</a>. This is not application of the doctrine of\n      due process. The invalidity arises from the probability\n      of the misuse of the law to the detriment of the\n      individual. If possible, the Court instead of striking\n      down the law may itself draw the line of demarcation\n      where possible but this effort should be sparingly made\n      and only in the clearest of cases.\u201d (at pages 470, 471)</span>\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_125\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"77461\" id=\"span_102\">66.   Similarly, in <a href=\"/doc/77461/\" id=\"a_149\">Harakchand Ratanchand Banthia &amp; Ors. v.\n\nUnion of India &amp; Ors.</a>, 1969 (2) SCC 166, <a href=\"/doc/193148174/\" id=\"a_150\">Section 27</a> of the Gold\n\nControl Act was struck down on the ground that the conditions\n\nimposed by it for the grant of renewal of licences are uncertain,\n\nvague and unintelligible. The Court held:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_119\">      \u201c21. We now come to <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_151\">Section 27</a> of the Act which\n      relates to licensing of dealers. It was stated on behalf\n      of the petitioners that the conditions imposed by\n      sub-section (6) of <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_152\">Section 27</a> for the grant or renewal\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_103\">\f                           58</span>\n\nof licences are uncertain, vague and unintelligible and\nconsequently wide and unfettered power was\nconferred upon the statutory authorities in the matter of\ngrant or renewal of licence. In our opinion this\ncontention is well founded and must be accepted as\ncorrect. <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_153\">Section 27(6)(a)</a> states that in the matter of\nissue or renewal of licences the Administrator shall\nhave regard to \u201cthe number of dealers existing in the\nregion in which the applicant intends to carry on\nbusiness as a dealer\u201d. But the word \u201cregion\u201d is\nnowhere defined in the Act. Similarly <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_154\">Section 27(6)(b)</a>\nrequires the Administrator to have regard to \u201cthe\nanticipated demand, as estimated by him, for\nornaments in that region.\u201d The expression \u201canticipated\ndemand\u201d is a vague expression which is not capable of\nobjective assessment and is bound to lead to a great\ndeal of uncertainty. Similarly the expression \u201csuitability\nof the applicant\u201d in <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_155\">Section 27(6)(e)</a> and \u201cpublic\ninterest\u201d in <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_156\">Section 27(6)(g)</a> do not provide any\nobjective standard or norm or guidance. For these\nreasons it must be held that clauses (a),(d),(e) and (g)\nof <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_157\">Section 27(6)</a> impose unreasonable restrictions on\nthe fundamental right of the petitioner to carry on\nbusiness and are constitutionally invalid. It was also\ncontended that there was no reason why the\nconditions for renewal of licence should be as rigorous\nas the conditions for initial grant of licence. The\nrequirement of strict conditions for the renewal of\nlicence renders the entire future of the business of the\ndealer uncertain and subjects it to the caprice and\narbitrary will of the administrative authorities. There is\njustification for this argument and the requirement of\n<a href=\"/doc/402501/\" id=\"a_158\">Section 26</a> of the Act imposing the same conditions for\nthe renewal of the licence as for the initial grant\nappears to be unreasonable. In our opinion clauses\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_120\">(a), (b), (e) and (g) are inextricably bound up with the\nother clauses of <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_159\">Section 27(6)</a> and form part of a single\nscheme. The result is that clauses (a), (b), (c), (e) and\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_121\">(g) are not severable and the entire <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_160\">Section 27(6)</a> of\nthe Act must be held invalid. <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_161\">Section 27(2)(d)</a> of the Act\nstates that a valid licence issued by the Administrator\n\u201cmay contain such conditions, limitations and\nrestrictions as the Administrator may think fit to impose\nand different conditions, limitations and restrictions\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_104\">\f                                 59</span>\n\n      may be imposed for different classes of dealers\u201d. On\n      the face of it, this sub-section confers such wide and\n      vague power upon the Administrator that it is difficult to\n      limit its scope. In our opinion <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_162\">Section 27(2)(d)</a> of the\n      Act must be struck down as an unreasonable\n      restriction on the fundamental right of the petitioners to\n      carry on business. It appears, however, to us that if\n      <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_163\">Section 27(2)(d)</a> and <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_164\">Section 27(6)</a> of the Act are\n      invalid the licensing scheme contemplated by the rest\n      of <a href=\"/doc/1534498/\" id=\"a_165\">Section 27</a> of the Act cannot be worked in practice. It\n      is, therefore, necessary for Parliament to enact fresh\n      legislation imposing appropriate conditions and\n      restrictions for the grant and renewal of licences to\n      dealers. In the alternative the Central Government may\n      make appropriate rules for the same purpose in\n      exercise of its rule-making power under <a href=\"/doc/1965344/\" id=\"a_166\">Section 114</a> of\n      the Act.\u201d\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_126\">67.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"50294192\" id=\"span_105\">In <a href=\"/doc/50294192/\" id=\"a_167\">A.K. Roy &amp; Ors. v. Union of India &amp; Ors.</a>, [1982] 2\n\nS.C.R. 272, a part of <a href=\"/doc/1869099/\" id=\"a_168\">Section 3</a> of the National Security Ordinance\n\nwas read down on the ground that \u201cacting in any manner\n\nprejudicial to the maintenance of supplies and services essential to\n\nthe community\u201d is an expression so vague that it is capable of\n\nwanton abuse. The Court held:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_122\">      \u201cWhat we have said above in regard to the expressions\n      \u2018defence of India\u2019, \u2018security of India\u2019, &#x27;security of the\n      State&#x27; and \u2018relations of India with foreign powers\u2019\n      cannot apply to the expression \u201cacting in any manner\n      prejudicial to the maintenance of supplies and services\n      essential to the community\u201d which occurs in <a href=\"/doc/1262152/\" id=\"a_169\">Section\n      3(2)</a> of the Act. Which supplies and services are\n      essential to the community can easily be defined by\n      the legislature and indeed, legislations which regulate\n      the prices and possession of essential commodities\n      either enumerate those commodities or confer upon\n      the appropriate Government the power to do so. In the\n      absence of a definition of \u2018supplies and services\n      essential to the community\u2019, the detaining authority will\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_106\">\f                             60</span>\n\nbe free to extend the application of this clause of\nsub-section (2) to any commodities or services the\nmaintenance of supply of which, according to him, is\nessential to the community.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_123\">But that is not all. The Explanation to sub-section (2)\ngives to the particular phrase in that sub-section a\nmeaning which is not only uncertain but which, at any\ngiven point of time, will be difficult to ascertain or fasten\nupon. According to the Explanation, no order of\ndetention can be made under the <a href=\"/doc/190229/\" id=\"a_170\">National Security Act</a>\non any ground on which an order of detention may be\nmade under the <a href=\"/doc/1701160/\" id=\"a_171\">Prevention of Blackmarketing and\nMaintenance of Supplies of Essential Commodities\nAct, 1980</a>. The reason for this, which is stated in the\nExplanation itself, is that for the purposes of\nsub-section (2), \u201cacting in any manner prejudicial to the\nmaintenance of supplies essential to the community\u201d\ndoes not include \u201cacting in any manner prejudicial to\nthe maintenance of supplies of commodities essential\nto the community\u201d as defined in the Explanation to\nsub-section (1) of <a href=\"/doc/1869099/\" id=\"a_172\">Section 3</a> of the Act of 1980. Clauses\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_124\">(a) and (b) of the Explanation to <a href=\"/doc/1558162/\" id=\"a_173\">Section 3(1)</a> of the Act\nof 1980 exhaust almost the entire range of essential\ncommodities. Clause (a) relates to committing or\ninstigating any person to commit any offence\npunishable under the Essential Commodities Act, 10 of\n1955, or under any other law for the time being in force\nrelating to the control of the production, supply or\ndistribution of, or trade and commerce in, any\ncommodity essential to the community. Clause (b) of\nthe Explanation to <a href=\"/doc/1558162/\" id=\"a_174\">Section 3(1)</a> of the Act of 1980\nrelates to dealing in any commodity which is an\nessential commodity as defined in the <a href=\"/doc/774360/\" id=\"a_175\">Essential\nCommodities Act, 1955</a>, or with respect to which\nprovisions have been made in any such other law as is\nreferred to in clause (a). We find it quite difficult to\nunderstand as to which are the remaining commodities\noutside the scope of the Act of 1980, in respect of\nwhich it can be said that the maintenance of their\nsupplies is essential to the community. The particular\nclause in sub-section (2) of <a href=\"/doc/682299/\" id=\"a_176\">Section 3</a> of the National\nSecurity Act is, therefore, capable of wanton abuse in\nthat, the detaining authority can place under detention\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_107\">\f                                  61</span>\n\n      any person for possession of any commodity on the\n      basis that the authority is of the opinion that the\n      maintenance of supply of that commodity is essential\n      to the community. We consider the particular clause\n      not only vague and uncertain but, in the context of the\n      Explanation, capable of being extended cavalierly to\n      supplies, the maintenance of which is not essential to\n      the community. To allow the personal liberty of the\n      people to be taken away by the application of that\n      clause would be a flagrant violation of the fairness and\n      justness of procedure which is implicit in the provisions\n      of Article 21.\u201d (at page 325-326)\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_127\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1813801\" id=\"span_108\">68.   Similarly, in <a href=\"/doc/1813801/\" id=\"a_177\">Kartar Singh v. State of Punjab</a>, (1994) 3 SCC\n\n569 at para 130-131, it was held:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_125\">      \u201c130. It is the basic principle of legal jurisprudence that\n      an enactment is void for vagueness if its prohibitions\n      are not clearly defined. Vague laws offend several\n      important values. It is insisted or emphasized that laws\n      should give the person of ordinary intelligence a\n      reasonable opportunity to know what is prohibited, so\n      that he may act accordingly. Vague laws may trap the\n      innocent by not providing fair warning. Such a law\n      impermissibly delegates basic policy matters to\n      policemen and also judges for resolution on an ad hoc\n      and subjective basis, with the attendant dangers of\n      arbitrary and discriminatory application. More so\n      uncertain and undefined words deployed inevitably\n      lead citizens to \u201csteer far wider of the unlawful zone \u2026\n      than if the boundaries of the forbidden areas were\n      clearly marked.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_126\">      131. Let us examine clause (i) of <a href=\"/doc/1890726/\" id=\"a_178\">Section 2(1)(a)</a>. This\n      section is shown to be blissfully and impermissibly\n      vague and imprecise. As rightly pointed out by the\n      learned counsel, even an innocent person who\n      ingenuously and undefiledly communicates or\n      associates without any knowledge or having no reason\n      to believe or suspect that the person or class of\n      persons with whom he has communicated or\n      associated is engaged in assisting in any manner\n      terrorists or disruptionists, can be arrested and\n      prosecuted by abusing or misusing or misapplying this\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_109\">\f                                  62</span>\n\n      definition. In ultimate consummation of the\n      proceedings, perhaps that guiltless and innoxious\n      innocent person may also be convicted.\u201d\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_127\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1813801\" id=\"span_110\">69.   Judged by the standards <a href=\"/doc/1813801/\" id=\"a_179\">laid down in</a> the aforesaid\n\njudgments, it is quite clear that the expressions used in 66A are\n\ncompletely open-ended and undefined.          <a href=\"/doc/326206/\" id=\"a_180\">Section 66</a> in stark\n\ncontrast to <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_181\">Section 66A</a> states:</span>\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_128\">      \u201c66. Computer related offences.\u2014If any person,\n      dishonestly or fraudulently, does any act referred to in\n      <a href=\"/doc/39800/\" id=\"a_182\">Section 43</a>, he shall be punishable with imprisonment\n      for a term which may extend to three years or with fine\n      which may extend to five lakh rupees or with both.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_129\">      Explanation.\u2014For the purposes of this section,\u2014\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_130\">      (a) the word \u201cdishonestly\u201d shall have the meaning\n      assigned to it in <a href=\"/doc/898393/\" id=\"a_183\">Section 24</a> of the Indian Penal Code\n      (45 of 1860);\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_131\">      (b) the word \u201cfraudulently\u201d shall have the meaning\n      assigned to it in <a href=\"/doc/1961417/\" id=\"a_184\">Section 25</a> of the Indian Penal Code\n      (45 of 1860).\u201d\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_132\">70.   It will be clear that in all computer related offences that are\n\nspoken of by <a href=\"/doc/326206/\" id=\"a_185\">Section 66</a>, mens rea is an ingredient and the\n\nexpression \u201cdishonestly\u201d and \u201cfraudulently\u201d are defined with some\n\ndegree of specificity, unlike the expressions used in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_186\">Section 66A</a>.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_133\">71.   The provisions contained in <a href=\"/doc/48199014/\" id=\"a_187\">Sections 66B</a> up to <a href=\"/doc/176300164/\" id=\"a_188\">Section 67B</a>\n\nalso provide for various punishments for offences that are clearly\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_111\">\f                                   63</span>\n\nmade out. For example, under <a href=\"/doc/48199014/\" id=\"a_189\">Section 66B</a>, whoever dishonestly\n\nreceives    or   retains     any   stolen   computer     resource   or\n\ncommunication device is punished with imprisonment.            Under\n\n<a href=\"/doc/118912881/\" id=\"a_190\">Section 66C</a>, whoever fraudulently or dishonestly makes use of\n\nany identification feature of another person is liable to punishment\n\nwith imprisonment.         Under <a href=\"/doc/121790054/\" id=\"a_191\">Section 66D</a>, whoever cheats by\n\npersonating becomes liable to punishment with imprisonment.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_128\">Section 66F again is a narrowly drawn section which inflicts\n\npunishment which may extend to imprisonment for life for persons\n\nwho threaten the unity, integrity, security or sovereignty of India.\n\n<a href=\"/doc/1318767/\" id=\"a_192\">Sections 67</a> to <a href=\"/doc/176300164/\" id=\"a_193\">67B</a> deal with punishment for offences for\n\npublishing or transmitting obscene material including depicting\n\nchildren in sexually explicit acts in electronic form.\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_129\">72.   In the <a href=\"/doc/1569253/\" id=\"a_194\">Indian Penal Code</a>, a number of the expressions that\n\noccur in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_195\">Section 66A</a> occur in Section 268.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_130\">      \u201c268. Public nuisance.\u2014A person is guilty of a public\n      nuisance who does any act or is guilty of an illegal\n      omission, which causes any common injury, danger or\n      annoyance to the public or to the people in general\n      who dwell or occupy property in the vicinity, or which\n      must necessarily cause injury, obstruction, danger or\n      annoyance to persons who may have occasion to use\n      any public right.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_131\">         A common nuisance is not excused on the ground\n      that it causes some convenience or advantage.\u201d\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_132\">73.   It is important to notice the distinction between the <a href=\"/doc/1962230/\" id=\"a_196\">Sections\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_112\">\f                                 64</span></a>\n\n268 and <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_197\">66A</a>. Whereas, in Section 268 the various expressions\n\nused are ingredients for the offence of a public nuisance, these\n\ningredients now become offences in themselves when it comes to\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_198\">Section 66A</a>. Further, under Section 268, the person should be\n\nguilty of an act or omission which is illegal in nature \u2013 legal acts\n\nare not within its net. A further ingredient is that injury, danger or\n\nannoyance must be to the public in general.        Injury, danger or\n\nannoyance are not offences by themselves howsoever made and\n\nto whomsoever made. The expression \u201cannoyance\u201d appears also\n\nin <a href=\"/doc/594493/\" id=\"a_199\">Sections 294</a> and <a href=\"/doc/529653/\" id=\"a_200\">510</a> of the IPC:\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_133\">\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_134\">      \u201c294. Obscene acts and songs.\u2014Whoever, to the\n      annoyance of others,\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_135\">      (a) does any obscene act in any public place, or\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_136\">      (b) sings, recites or utters any obscene songs, ballad\n      or words, in or near any public place,\n\n\n      shall be punished with imprisonment of either\n      description for a term which may extend to three\n      months, or with fine, or with both.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_134\">      510. Misconduct in public by a drunken person.\u2014\n      Whoever, in a state of intoxication, appears in any\n      public place, or in any place which it is a trespass in\n      him to enter, and there conducts himself in such a\n      manner as to cause annoyance to any person, shall be\n      punished with simple imprisonment for a term which\n      may extend to twenty-four hours, or with fine which\n      may extend to ten rupees, or with both.\u201d\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_113\">\f                                  65</span>\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_135\">74.   If one looks at Section 294, the annoyance that is spoken of\n\nis clearly defined - that is, it has to be caused by obscene\n\nutterances or acts. Equally, under Section 510, the annoyance\n\nthat is caused to a person must only be by another person who is\n\nin a state of intoxication and who annoys such person only in a\n\npublic place or in a place for which it is a trespass for him to enter.\n\nSuch narrowly and closely defined contours of offences made out\n\nunder the Penal Code are conspicuous by their absence in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_201\">Section\n\n66A</a> which in stark contrast uses completely open ended,\n\nundefined and vague language.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_136\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_137\">75.   Incidentally, none of the expressions used in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_202\">Section 66A</a> are\n\ndefined.   Even \u201ccriminal intimidation\u201d is not defined \u2013 and the\n\ndefinition clause of the <a href=\"/doc/1965344/\" id=\"a_203\">Information Technology Act</a>, <a href=\"/doc/629401/\" id=\"a_204\">Section 2</a> does\n\nnot say that words and expressions that are defined in the Penal\n\nCode will apply to this Act.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_138\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_139\">76.   Quite apart from this, as has been pointed out above, every\n\nexpression used is nebulous in meaning. What may be offensive\n\nto one may not be offensive to another. What may cause\n\nannoyance or inconvenience to one may not cause annoyance or\n\ninconvenience to another. Even the expression \u201cpersistently\u201d is\n\ncompletely imprecise \u2013 suppose a message is sent thrice, can it\n\nbe said that it was sent \u201cpersistently\u201d? Does a message have to\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_114\">\f                                66</span>\n\nbe sent (say) at least eight times, before it can be said that such\n\nmessage is \u201cpersistently\u201d sent?      There is no demarcating line\n\nconveyed by any of these expressions \u2013 and that is what renders\n\nthe Section unconstitutionally vague.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_140\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_141\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Neg\" data-docid=\"1537019\" id=\"span_115\">77.   However, the learned Additional Solicitor General argued\n\nbefore us that expressions that are used in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_205\">Section 66A</a> may be\n\nincapable of any precise definition but for that reason they are not\n\nconstitutionally vulnerable. He cited a large number of judgments\n\nin support of this submission. None of the cited judgments dealt\n\nwith a Section creating an offence which is saved despite its being\n\nvague and in capable of any precise definition. In fact, most of the\n\njudgments cited before us did not deal with criminal law at all. The\n\nfew that did are dealt with hereinbelow.     For instance, <a href=\"/doc/1537019/\" id=\"a_206\">Madan\n\nSingh v. State of Bihar</a>, (2004) 4 SCC 622 was cited before us.\n\nThe passage cited from the aforesaid judgment is contained in\n\npara 19 of the judgment. The cited passage is not in the context of\n\nan argument that the word \u201cterrorism\u201d not being separately defined\n\nwould, therefore, be struck down on the ground of vagueness.\n\nThe cited passage was only in the context of upholding the\n\nconviction of the accused <a href=\"/doc/1537019/\" id=\"a_207\">in that case</a>.</span>       <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"37174\" id=\"span_116\">Similarly, in <a href=\"/doc/37174/\" id=\"a_208\">Zameer\n\nAhmed Latifur Rehman Sheikh v. State of Maharashtra &amp; Ors.</a>,\n\n(2010) 5 SCC 246, the expression \u201cinsurgency\u201d was said to be\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_117\">\f                                67</span>\n\nundefined and would defy a precise definition, yet it could be\n\nunderstood to mean break down of peace and tranquility as also a\n\ngrave disturbance of public order so as to endanger the security of\n\nthe State and its sovereignty. This again was said in the context of\n\na challenge on the ground of legislative competence.            The\n\nprovisions of the <a href=\"/doc/38034089/\" id=\"a_209\">Maharashtra Control of Organised Crime Act</a>\n\nwere challenged on the ground that they were outside the\n\nexpression \u201cpublic order\u201d contained in Entry 1 of List I of the 7 th\n\nSchedule of the Constitution of India. This contention was repelled\n\nby saying that the expression \u201cpublic order\u201d was wide enough to\n\nencompass cases of \u201cinsurgency\u201d. This case again had nothing to\n\ndo with a challenge raised on the ground of vagueness.</span>\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_142\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Neg\" data-docid=\"666476\" id=\"span_118\">78.   Similarly, in <a href=\"/doc/666476/\" id=\"a_210\">State of M.P. v. Kedia Leather &amp; Liquor\n\nLimited</a>, (2003) 7 SCC 389, paragraph 8 was cited to show that\n\nthe expression \u201cnuisance\u201d appearing in <a href=\"/doc/983382/\" id=\"a_211\">Section 133</a> of the Code of\n\nCriminal Procedure was also not capable of precise definition.\n\nThis again was said in the context of an argument that <a href=\"/doc/983382/\" id=\"a_212\">Section 133</a>\n\nof the Code of Criminal Procedure was impliedly repealed by the\n\n<a href=\"/doc/867156/\" id=\"a_213\">Water (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act, 1974</a>.         This\n\ncontention was repelled by saying that the areas of operation of\n\nthe two provisions were completely different and they existed side\n\nby side being mutually exclusive. This case again did not contain\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_119\">\f                                68</span>\n\nany argument that the provision contained in Section 133 was\n\nvague and, therefore, unconstitutional.</span>      <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"514077\" id=\"span_120\">Similarly, in <a href=\"/doc/514077/\" id=\"a_214\">State of\n\nKarnataka v. Appa Balu Ingale</a>, 1995 Supp. (4) SCC 469, the\n\nword \u201cuntouchability\u201d was said not to be capable of precise\n\ndefinition. Here again, there was no constitutional challenge on\n\nthe ground of vagueness.</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_143\">79.   In fact, two English judgments cited by the learned Additional\n\nSolicitor General would demonstrate how vague the words used in\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_215\">Section 66A</a> are. In Director of Public Prosecutions v. Collins,\n\n(2006) 1 WLR 2223, the very expression \u201cgrossly offensive\u201d is\n\ncontained in Section 127(1)(1) of the U.K. Communications Act,\n\n2003. A 61 year old man made a number of telephone calls over\n\ntwo years to the office of a Member of Parliament.             In these\n\ntelephone calls and recorded messages Mr. Collins who held\n\nstrong views on immigration made a reference to \u201cWogs\u201d, \u201cPakis\u201d,\n\n\u201cBlack bastards\u201d and \u201cNiggers\u201d.      Mr. Collins was charged with\n\nsending   messages     which    were    grossly   offensive.       The\n\nLeicestershire Justices dismissed the case against Mr. Collins on\n\nthe ground that the telephone calls were offensive but not grossly\n\noffensive. A reasonable person would not so find the calls to be\n\ngrossly offensive. The Queen\u2019s Bench agreed and dismissed the\n\nappeal filed by the Director of Public Prosecutions. The House of\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_121\">\f                                 69</span>\n\nLords reversed the Queen\u2019s Bench stating:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_137\">      \u201c9. The parties agreed with the rulings of the Divisional\n      Court that it is for the Justices to determine as a\n      question of fact whether a message is grossly\n      offensive, that in making this determination the\n      Justices must apply the standards of an open and just\n      multi-racial society, and that the words must be judged\n      taking account of their context and all relevant\n      circumstances. I would agree also. Usages and\n      sensitivities may change over time. Language\n      otherwise insulting may be used in an unpejorative,\n      even affectionate, way, or may be adopted as a badge\n      of honour (\u201cOld Contemptibles\u201d). There can be no\n      yardstick of gross offensiveness otherwise than by the\n      application of reasonably enlightened, but not\n      perfectionist, contemporary standards to the particular\n      message sent in its particular context. The test is\n      whether a message is couched in terms liable to cause\n      gross offence to those to whom it relates.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_138\">      10. In contrast with section 127(2)(a) and its\n      predecessor subsections, which require proof of an\n      unlawful purpose and a degree of knowledge, section\n      127(1)(a) provides no explicit guidance on the state of\n      mind which must be proved against a defendant to\n      establish an offence against the subsection.\u201d\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_144\">80.   Similarly in Chambers v. Director of Public Prosecutions,\n\n[2013] 1 W.L.R. 1833, the Queen\u2019s Bench was faced with the\n\nfollowing facts:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_139\">      \u201cFollowing an alert on the Internet social network,\n      Twitter, the defendant became aware that, due to\n      adverse weather conditions, an airport from which he\n      was due to travel nine days later was closed. He\n      responded by posting several \u201ctweets\u201d on Twitter in his\n      own name, including the following: \u201cCrap1 Robin Hood\n      Airport is closed. You\u2019ve got a week and a bit to get\n      your shit together otherwise I am blowing the airport\n      sky high1\u201d None of the defendant\u2019s \u201cfollowers\u201d who\n      read the posting was alarmed by it at the time. Some\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_122\">\f                                 70</span>\n\n      five days after its posting the defendant\u2019s tweet was\n      read by the duty manager responsible for security at\n      the airport on a general Internet search for tweets\n      relating to the airport. Though not believed to be a\n      credible threat the matter was reported to the police.\n      In interview the defendant asserted that the tweet was\n      a joke and not intended to be menacing.             The\n      defendant was charged with sending by a public\n      electronic communications network a message of a\n      menacing character contrary to section 127(1)(a) of the\n      Communications Act 2003. He was convicted in a\n      magistrates\u2019 court and, on appeal, the Crown Court\n      upheld the conviction, being satisfied that the message\n      was \u201cmenacing per se\u201d and that the defendant was, at\n      the very least, aware that his message was of a\n      menacing character.\u201d\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_145\">81.   The Crown Court was satisfied that the message in question\n\nwas \u201cmenacing\u201d stating that an ordinary person seeing the tweet\n\nwould be alarmed and, therefore, such message would be\n\n\u201cmenacing\u201d.      The Queen\u2019s Bench Division reversed the Crown\n\nCourt stating:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_140\">      \u201c31. Before concluding that a message is criminal on\n      the basis that it represents a menace, its precise\n      terms, and any inferences to be drawn from its precise\n      terms, need to be examined in the context in and the\n      means by which the message was sent. The Crown\n      Court was understandably concerned that this\n      message was sent at a time when, as we all know,\n      there is public concern about acts of terrorism and the\n      continuing threat to the security of the country from\n      possible further terrorist attacks.      That is plainly\n      relevant to context, but the offence is not directed to\n      the inconvenience which may be caused by the\n      message. In any event, the more one reflects on it, the\n      clearer it becomes that this message did not represent\n      a terrorist threat, or indeed any other form of threat. It\n      was posted on \u201cTwitter\u201d for widespread reading, a\n      conversation piece for the defendant\u2019s followers,\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_123\">\f                                  71</span>\n\n      drawing attention to himself and his predicament.\n      Much more significantly, although it purports to address\n      \u201cyou\u201d, meaning those responsible for the airport, it was\n      not sent to anyone at the airport or anyone responsible\n      for airport security, or indeed any form of public\n      security. The grievance addressed by the message is\n      that the airport is closed when the writer wants it to be\n      open. The language and punctuation are inconsistent\n      with the writer intending it to be or it to be taken as a\n      serious warning. Moreover, as Mr. Armson noted, it is\n      unusual for a threat of a terrorist nature to invite the\n      person making it to be readily identified, as this\n      message did. Finally, although we are accustomed to\n      very brief messages by terrorists to indicate that a\n      bomb or explosive device has been put in place and\n      will detonate shortly, it is difficult to imagine a serious\n      threat in which warning of it is given to a large number\n      of tweet \u201cfollowers\u201d in ample time for the threat to be\n      reported and extinguished.\u201d\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_146\">82.   These two cases illustrate how judicially trained minds would\n\nfind a person guilty or not guilty depending upon the Judge\u2019s\n\nnotion of what is \u201cgrossly offensive\u201d or \u201cmenacing\u201d.        In Collins\u2019\n\ncase, both the Leicestershire Justices and two Judges of the\n\nQueen\u2019s Bench would have acquitted Collins whereas the House\n\nof Lords convicted him.      Similarly, in the Chambers case, the\n\nCrown Court would have convicted Chambers whereas the\n\nQueen\u2019s Bench acquitted him. If judicially trained minds can come\n\nto diametrically opposite conclusions on the same set of facts it is\n\nobvious that expressions such as \u201cgrossly offensive\u201d or \u201cmenacing\u201d\n\nare so vague that there is no manageable standard by which a\n\nperson can be said to have committed an offence or not to have\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_124\">\f                                  72</span>\n\ncommitted an offence. Quite obviously, a prospective offender of\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_216\">Section 66A</a> and the authorities who are to enforce <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_217\">Section 66A</a>\n\nhave absolutely no manageable standard by which to book a\n\nperson for an offence under <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_218\">Section 66A</a>. This being the case,\n\nhaving regard also to the two English precedents cited by the\n\nlearned Additional Solicitor General, it is clear that <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_219\">Section 66A</a> is\n\nunconstitutionally vague.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_147\">      Ultimately, applying the tests referred to in Chintaman Rao\n\nand V.G. Row\u2019s case, referred to earlier in the judgment, it is clear\n\nthat <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_220\">Section 66A</a> arbitrarily, excessively and disproportionately\n\ninvades the right of free speech and upsets the balance between\n\nsuch right and the reasonable restrictions that may be imposed on\n\nsuch right.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_148\">Chilling Effect And Overbreadth\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_149\">83.   Information that may be grossly offensive or which causes\n\nannoyance or inconvenience are undefined terms which take into\n\nthe net a very large amount of protected and innocent speech. A\n\nperson may discuss or even advocate by means of writing\n\ndisseminated over the internet information that may be a view or\n\npoint of view pertaining to governmental, literary, scientific or other\n\nmatters which may be unpalatable to certain sections of society. It\n\nis obvious that an expression of a view on any matter may cause\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_125\">\f                                  73</span>\n\nannoyance, inconvenience or may be grossly offensive to some. A\n\nfew examples will suffice.       A certain section of a particular\n\ncommunity     may     be    grossly    offended    or   annoyed     by\n\ncommunications over the internet by \u201cliberal views\u201d \u2013 such as the\n\nemancipation of women or the abolition of the caste system or\n\nwhether certain members of a non proselytizing religion should be\n\nallowed to bring persons within their fold who are otherwise\n\noutside the fold. Each one of these things may be grossly\n\noffensive, annoying, inconvenient, insulting or injurious to large\n\nsections of particular communities and would fall within the net\n\ncast by <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_221\">Section 66A</a>. In point of fact, <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_222\">Section 66A</a> is cast so widely\n\nthat virtually any opinion on any subject would be covered by it, as\n\nany serious opinion dissenting with the mores of the day would be\n\ncaught within its net. Such is the reach of the Section and if it is to\n\nwithstand the test of constitutionality, the chilling effect on free\n\nspeech would be total.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_150\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_151\">84.   Incidentally, some of our judgments have recognized this\n\nchilling effect of free speech. <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"501107\" id=\"span_126\">In <a href=\"/doc/501107/\" id=\"a_223\">R. Rajagopal v. State of T.N.</a>,\n\n(1994) 6 SCC 632, this Court held:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_141\">      \u201c19. The principle of Sullivan [376 US 254 : 11 L Ed 2d\n      686 (1964)] was carried forward \u2014 and this is relevant\n      to the second question arising in this case \u2014 in\n      Derbyshire County Council v. Times Newspapers\n      Ltd. [(1993) 2 WLR 449 : (1993) 1 All ER 1011, HL] , a\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_127\">\f                            74</span>\n\ndecision rendered by the House of Lords. The plaintiff,\na local authority brought an action for damages for libel\nagainst the defendants in respect of two articles\npublished in Sunday Times questioning the propriety of\ninvestments made for its superannuation fund. The\narticles were headed \u201cRevealed: Socialist tycoon deals\nwith Labour Chief\u201d and \u201cBizarre deals of a council\nleader and the media tycoon\u201d. A preliminary issue was\nraised whether the plaintiff has a cause of action\nagainst the defendant. The trial Judge held that such\nan action was maintainable but on appeal the Court of\nAppeal held to the contrary. When the matter reached\nthe House of Lords, it affirmed the decision of the\nCourt of Appeal but on a different ground. Lord Keith\ndelivered the judgment agreed to by all other learned\nLaw Lords. In his opinion, Lord Keith recalled that\nin Attorney General v. Guardian Newspapers Ltd. (No.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_142\">2)[(1990) 1 AC 109 : (1988) 3 All ER 545 : (1988) 3\nWLR 776, HL] popularly known as \u201cSpycatcher case\u201d,\nthe House of Lords had opined that \u201cthere are rights\navailable to private citizens which institutions of\u2026\nGovernment are not in a position to exercise unless\nthey can show that it is in the public interest to do so\u201d.\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_152\">It was also held therein that not only was there no\npublic interest in allowing governmental institutions to\nsue for libel, it was \u201ccontrary to the public interest\nbecause to admit such actions would place an\nundesirable fetter on freedom of speech\u201d and further\nthat action for defamation or threat of such action\n\u201cinevitably have an inhibiting effect on freedom of\nspeech\u201d. The learned Law Lord referred to the decision\nof the United States Supreme Court in New York\nTimes v. Sullivan [376 US 254 : 11 L Ed 2d 686 (1964)]\nand certain other decisions of American Courts and\nobserved \u2014 and this is significant for our purposes\u2014\n\n    \u201cwhile these decisions were related most directly to\nthe provisions of the American Constitution concerned\nwith securing freedom of speech, the public interest\nconsiderations which underlaid them are no less valid\nin this country. What has been described as \u2018the\nchilling effect\u2019 induced by the threat of civil actions for\nlibel is very important. Quite often the facts which\nwould justify a defamatory publication are known to be\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_128\">\f                                75</span>\n\n      true, but admissible evidence capable of proving those\n      facts is not available.\u201d\n      Accordingly, it was held that the action was not\n      maintainable in law.\u201d\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_153\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1327342\" id=\"span_129\">85.   Also in <a href=\"/doc/1327342/\" id=\"a_224\">S. Khushboo v. Kanniammal</a>, (2010) 5 SCC 600,\n\nthis Court said:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_143\">      \u201c47. In the present case, the substance of the\n      controversy does not really touch on whether\n      premarital sex is socially acceptable. Instead, the real\n      issue of concern is the disproportionate response to\n      the appellant&#x27;s remarks. If the complainants\n      vehemently disagreed with the appellant&#x27;s views, then\n      they should have contested her views through the\n      news media or any other public platform. The law\n      should not be used in a manner that has chilling effects\n      on the \u201cfreedom of speech and expression\u201d.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_154\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"539407\" id=\"span_130\">86.   That the content of the right under <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_225\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> remains\n\nthe same whatever the means of communication including internet\n\ncommunication is clearly established by Reno\u2019s case (<a href=\"/doc/539407/\" id=\"a_226\">supra) and\n\nby The Secretary, Ministry of Information &amp; Broadcasting v.\n\nCricket Association of Bengal &amp; Anr.</a>, (1995) SCC 2 161 at Para\n\n78 already referred to. It is thus clear that not only are the\n\nexpressions used in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_227\">Section 66A</a> expressions of inexactitude but\n\nthey are also over broad and would fall foul of the repeated\n\ninjunctions of this Court that restrictions on the freedom of speech\n\nmust be couched in the narrowest possible terms.</span> <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"111867\" id=\"span_131\">For example,\n\nsee, <a href=\"/doc/111867/\" id=\"a_228\">Kedar Nath Singh v. State of Bihar</a>, [1962] Supp. 2 S.C.R.\n\n769 at 808 -809. In point of fact, judgments of the Constitution\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_132\">\f                                76</span>\n\nBench of this Court have struck down sections which are similar in\n\nnature.</span> <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1386353\" id=\"span_133\">A prime example is the section struck down in the first\n\n<a href=\"/doc/1386353/\" id=\"a_229\">Ram Manohar Lohia</a> case, namely, Section 3 of the U.P. Special\n\nPowers Act, where the persons who \u201cinstigated\u201d expressly or by\n\nimplication any person or class of persons not to pay or to defer\n\npayment of any liability were punishable. This Court specifically\n\nheld that under the Section a wide net was cast to catch a variety\n\nof acts of instigation ranging from friendly advice to systematic\n\npropaganda. It was held that in its wide amplitude, the Section\n\ntakes in the innocent as well as the guilty, bonafide and malafide\n\nadvice and whether the person be a legal adviser, a friend or a\n\nwell wisher of the person instigated, he cannot escape the\n\ntentacles of the Section.   The Court held that it was not possible\n\nto predicate with some kind of precision the different categories of\n\ninstigation falling within or without the field of constitutional\n\nprohibitions.   It further held that the Section must be declared\n\nunconstitutional as the offence made out would depend upon\n\nfactors which are uncertain.</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_155\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_156\">87.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"687159\" id=\"span_134\">In <a href=\"/doc/687159/\" id=\"a_230\">Kameshwar Prasad &amp; Ors.         v. The State of Bihar &amp;\n\nAnr.</a>, [1962] Supp. 3 S.C.R. 369, Rule 4-A of the Bihar\n\nGovernment Servants Conduct Rules, 1956 was challenged. The\n\nrule states \u201cNo government servant shall participate in any\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_135\">\f                                  77</span>\n\ndemonstration or resort to any form of strike in connection with any\n\nmatter pertaining to his conditions of service.\u201d</span>\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_157\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"687159\" id=\"span_136\">88.   The aforesaid rule was challenged under <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_231\">Articles 19 (1)(a)</a>\n\nand (b) of the Constitution. The Court followed the law <a href=\"/doc/687159/\" id=\"a_232\">laid down in</a>\n\nRam Manohar Lohia\u2019s case [1960] 2 S.C.R. 821 and accepted\n\nthe challenge.    It first held that demonstrations are a form of\n\nspeech and then held:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_144\">      \u201cThe approach to the question regarding the\n      constitutionality of the rule should be whether the ban\n      that it imposes on demonstrations would be covered by\n      the limitation of the guaranteed rights contained in <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_233\">Art.\n      19 (2)</a> and <a href=\"/doc/1723400/\" id=\"a_234\">19(3).</a> In regard to both these clauses the\n      only relevant criteria which has been suggested by the\n      respondent-State is that the rule is framed &quot;in the\n      interest of public order&quot;. A demonstration may be\n      defined as &quot;an expression of one&#x27;s feelings by outward\n      signs.&quot; A demonstration such as is prohibited by, the\n      rule may be of the most innocent type - peaceful\n      orderly such as the mere wearing of a badge by a\n      Government servant or even by a silent assembly say\n      outside office hours - demonstrations which could in no\n      sense be suggested to involve any breach of\n      tranquility, or of a type involving incitement to or\n      capable of leading to disorder. If the rule had confined\n      itself to demonstrations of type which would lead to\n      disorder then the validity of that rule could have been\n      sustained but what the rule does is the imposition of a\n      blanket-ban on all demonstrations of whatever type -\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_158\">      innocent as well as otherwise - and in consequence its\n      validity cannot be upheld.\u201d (at page 374)\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_159\">89.   The Court further went on to hold that remote disturbances of\n\npublic order by demonstration would fall outside <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_235\">Article 19(2).</a> The\n\nconnection with public order has to be intimate, real and rational\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_137\">\f                                 78</span>\n\nand should arise directly from the demonstration that is sought to\n\nbe prohibited. Finally, the Court held:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_145\">      \u201cThe vice of the rule, in our opinion, consists in this that\n      it lays a ban on every type of demonstration - be the\n      same however innocent and however incapable of\n      causing a breach of public tranquility and does not\n      confine itself to those forms of demonstrations which\n      might lead to that result.\u201d (at page 384)\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_160\">90.   These two Constitution Bench decisions bind us and would\n\napply directly on <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_236\">Section 66A</a>. We, therefore, hold that the Section\n\nis unconstitutional also on the ground that it takes within its sweep\n\nprotected speech and speech that is innocent in nature and is\n\nliable therefore to be used in such a way as to have a chilling\n\neffect on free speech and would, therefore, have to be struck down\n\non the ground of overbreadth.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_161\">Possibility of an act being abused is not a ground to test its\nvalidity:\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_162\">91.   The learned Additional Solicitor General cited a large number\n\nof judgments on the proposition that the fact that <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_237\">Section 66A</a> is\n\ncapable of being abused by the persons who administered it is not\n\na ground to test its validity if it is otherwise valid.   He further\n\nassured us that this Government was committed to free speech\n\nand that <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_238\">Section 66A</a> would not be used to curb free speech, but\n\nwould be used only when excesses are perpetrated by persons on\n\nthe rights of others. <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1193965\" id=\"span_138\">In <a href=\"/doc/1193965/\" id=\"a_239\">The Collector of Customs, Madras v.\n\nNathella Sampathu Chetty &amp; Anr.</a>, [1962] 3 S.C.R. 786, this\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_139\">\f                                79</span>\n\nCourt observed:</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_163\">\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_146\">     \u201c\u2026.This Court has held in numerous rulings, to which it\n     is unnecessary to refer, that the possibility of the abuse\n     of the powers under the provisions contained in any\n     statute is no ground for declaring the provision to be\n     unreasonable or void. Commenting on a passage in\n     the judgment of the Court of Appeal of Northern Ireland\n     which stated:\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_147\">     <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"473527\" id=\"span_140\">\u201cIf such powers are capable of being exercised\n     reasonably it is impossible to say that they may not\n     also be exercised unreasonably\u201d\n\n\n     and treating this as a ground for holding the statute\n     invalid Viscount Simonds observed in <a href=\"/doc/473527/\" id=\"a_240\">Belfast\n     Corporation v. O.D. Commission</a> [ 1960 AC 490 at pp.\n     520-521] :</span>\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_148\">     \u201cIt appears to me that the short answer to this\n     contention (and I hope its shortness will not be\n     regarded as disrespect) is that the validity of a\n     measure is not to be determined by its application to\n     particular cases.\u2026 If it is not so exercised (i.e. if the\n     powers are abused) it is open to challenge and there is\n     no need for express provision for its challenge in the\n     statute.\u201d\n\n\n     The possibility of abuse of a statute otherwise valid\n     does not impart to it any element of invalidity. The\n     converse must also follow that a statute which is\n     otherwise invalid as being unreasonable cannot be\n     saved by its being administered in a reasonable\n     manner. The constitutional validity of the statute would\n     have to be determined on the basis of its provisions\n     and on the ambit of its operation as reasonably\n     construed. If so judged it passes the test of\n     reasonableness, possibility of the powers conferred\n     being improperly used is no ground for pronouncing\n     the law itself invalid and similarly if the law properly\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_141\">\f                                 80</span>\n\n      interpreted and tested in the light of the requirements\n      set out in Part III of the Constitution does not pass the\n      test it cannot be pronounced valid merely because it is\n      administered in a manner which might not conflict with\n      the constitutional requirements.\u201d (at page 825)\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_164\">92.   In this case, it is the converse proposition which would really\n\napply if the learned Additional Solicitor General\u2019s argument is to be\n\naccepted. If <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_241\">Section 66A</a> is otherwise invalid, it cannot be saved by\n\nan assurance from the learned Additional Solicitor General that it\n\nwill be administered in a reasonable manner. Governments may\n\ncome and Governments may go but <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_242\">Section 66A</a> goes on forever.\n\nAn assurance from the present Government even if carried out\n\nfaithfully would not bind any successor Government.          It must,\n\ntherefore, be held that <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_243\">Section 66A</a> must be judged on its own\n\nmerits without any reference to how well it may be administered.\n\n\nSeverability:\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"RespArg\" id=\"p_165\">93.   The argument of the learned Additional Solicitor General on\n\nthis score is reproduced by us verbatim from one of his written\n\nsubmissions:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_149\">      \u201cFurthermore it is respectfully submitted that in the\n      event of Hon\u2019ble Court not being satisfied about the\n      constitutional validity of either any expression or a part\n      of the provision, the Doctrine of Severability as\n      enshrined under <a href=\"/doc/134715/\" id=\"a_244\">Article 13</a> may be resorted to.\u201d\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_166\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"456839\" id=\"span_142\">94.   The submission is vague: the learned Additional Solicitor\n\nGeneral does not indicate which part or parts of <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_245\">Section 66A</a> can\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_143\">\f                                  81</span>\n\npossibly be saved. This Court in <a href=\"/doc/456839/\" id=\"a_246\">Romesh Thappar v. The State\n\nof Madras</a>, [1950] S.C.R. 594 repelled a contention of severability\n\nwhen it came to the courts enforcing the fundamental right under\n\n<a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_247\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> in the following terms:</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_167\">\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_150\">      \u201cIt was, however, argued that <a href=\"/doc/1004398/\" id=\"a_248\">Section 9</a>(1-A) could not\n      be considered wholly void, as, under <a href=\"/doc/1010805/\" id=\"a_249\">Article 13(1)</a>, an\n      existing law inconsistent with a fundamental right is\n      void only to the extent of the inconsistency and no\n      more. Insofar as the securing of the public safety or the\n      maintenance of public order would include the security\n      of the State, the impugned provision, as applied to the\n      latter purpose, was covered by clause (2) of <a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_250\">Article 19</a>\n      and must, it was said, be held to be valid. We are\n      unable to accede to this contention. Where a law\n      purports to authorise the imposition of restrictions on a\n      fundamental right in language wide enough to cover\n      restrictions both within and without the limits of\n      constitutionally permissible legislative action affecting\n      such right, it is not possible to uphold it even so far as\n      it may be applied within the constitutional limits, as it is\n      not severable. So long as the possibility of its being\n      applied for purposes not sanctioned by the Constitution\n      cannot be ruled out, it must be held to be wholly\n      unconstitutional and void. In other words, clause (2) of\n      <a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_251\">Article 19</a> having allowed the imposition of restrictions\n      on the freedom of speech and expression only in\n      cases where danger to the State is involved, an\n      enactment, which is capable of being applied to cases\n      where no such danger could arise, cannot be held to\n      be constitutional and valid to any extent.\u201d (At page\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_151\">      603)\n\n\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_168\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1719619\" id=\"span_144\">95.   It has been held by us that <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_252\">Section 66A</a> purports to authorize\n\nthe imposition of restrictions on the fundamental right contained in\n\n<a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_253\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> in language wide enough to cover restrictions both\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_145\">\f                                  82</span>\n\nwithin and without the limits of constitutionally permissible\n\nlegislative action.   We have held following <a href=\"/doc/1719619/\" id=\"a_254\">K.A. Abbas\u2019</a> case\n\n(Supra) that the possibility of <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_255\">Section 66A</a> being applied for\n\npurposes not sanctioned by the Constitution cannot be ruled out.\n\nIt must, therefore, be held to be wholly unconstitutional and void.</span>\n\n<span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"725224\" id=\"span_146\">Romesh      Thappar\u2019s     Case     was    distinguished     in   <a href=\"/doc/725224/\" id=\"a_256\">R.M.D.\n\nChamarbaugwalla v. The Union of India</a>, [1957] S.C.R. 930 in\n\nthe context of a right under <a href=\"/doc/935769/\" id=\"a_257\">Article 19(1)(g)</a> as follows:</span>\n\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_152\">      \u201c20. <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"456839\" id=\"span_147\">In <a href=\"/doc/456839/\" id=\"a_258\">Romesh Thappar v. State of Madras</a> [ (1950)\n      SCR 594] , the question was as to the validity of\n      <a href=\"/doc/1004398/\" id=\"a_259\">Section 9</a>(1-A) of the Madras Maintenance of Public\n      Order Act, 23 of 1949. That section authorised the\n      Provincial Government to prohibit the entry and\n      circulation within the State of a newspaper \u201cfor the\n      purpose of securing the public safety or the\n      maintenance of public order.\u201d Subsequent to the\n      enactment of this statute, the Constitution came into\n      force, and the validity of the impugned provision\n      depended on whether it was protected by <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_260\">Article\n      19(2)</a>, which saved \u201cexisting law insofar as it relates to\n      any matter which undermines the security of or tends\n      to overthrow the State.\u201d It was held by this Court that\n      as the purposes mentioned in <a href=\"/doc/1004398/\" id=\"a_261\">Section 9</a>(1-A) of the\n      Madras Act were wider in amplitude than those\n      specified in <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_262\">Article 19(2)</a>, and as it was not possible to\n      split up <a href=\"/doc/1004398/\" id=\"a_263\">Section 9</a>(1-A) into what was within and what\n      was without the protection of <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_264\">Article 19(2)</a>, the\n      provision must fail in its entirety. That is really a\n      decision that the impugned provision was on its own\n      contents inseverable. It is not an authority for the\n      position that even when a provision is severable, it\n      must be struck down on the ground that the principle\n      of severability is inadmissible when the invalidity of a\n      statute arises by reason of its contravening\n      constitutional prohibitions.</span> <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"424874\" id=\"span_148\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"334293\" id=\"span_149\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"456839\" id=\"span_150\">It should be mentioned that\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_151\">\f                                 83</span>\n\n      the   decision   in <a href=\"/doc/456839/\" id=\"a_265\">Romesh      Thappar v. State   of\n      Madras</a> [ (1950) SCR 594] was referred to in <a href=\"/doc/334293/\" id=\"a_266\">State of\n      Bombay v. F.N. Balsara</a> [ (<a href=\"/doc/424874/\" id=\"a_267\">1951) SCR 682] and State\n      of Bombay v. United Motors (India) Ltd.</a> [ (1953) SCR\n      1069 at 1098-99] and distinguished.\u201d</span></span></span>\n\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_169\">96.   The present being a case of an <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_268\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> violation,\n\nRomesh Thappar\u2019s judgment would apply on all fours.            In an\n\n<a href=\"/doc/935769/\" id=\"a_269\">Article 19(1)(g)</a> challenge, there is no question of a law being\n\napplied for purposes not sanctioned by the Constitution for the\n\nsimple reason that the eight subject matters of <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_270\">Article 19(2)</a> are\n\nconspicuous by their absence in <a href=\"/doc/626103/\" id=\"a_271\">Article 19(6)</a> which only speaks of\n\nreasonable restrictions in the interests of the general public. The\n\npresent is a case where, as has been held above, <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_272\">Section 66A</a>\n\ndoes not fall within any of the subject matters contained in <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_273\">Article\n\n19(2)</a> and the possibility of its being applied for purposes outside\n\nthose subject matters is clear. We therefore hold that no part of\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_274\">Section 66A</a> is severable and the provision as a whole must be\n\ndeclared unconstitutional.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_170\">\n<a href=\"/doc/367586/\" id=\"a_275\">Article 14</a>\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"RespArg\" id=\"p_171\">97.   Counsel for the petitioners have argued that <a href=\"/doc/367586/\" id=\"a_276\">Article 14</a> is also\n\ninfringed in that an offence whose ingredients are vague in nature\n\nis arbitrary and unreasonable and would result in arbitrary and\n\ndiscriminatory application of the criminal law. Further, there is no\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_152\">\f                                 84</span>\n\nintelligible differentia between the medium of print, broadcast, and\n\nreal live speech as opposed to speech on the internet and,\n\ntherefore, new categories of criminal offences cannot be made on\n\nthis ground. Similar offences which are committed on the internet\n\nhave a three year maximum sentence under <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_277\">Section 66A</a> as\n\nopposed to defamation which has a two year maximum sentence.\n\nAlso, defamation is a non-cognizable offence whereas under\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_153\"><a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_278\">Section 66A</a> the offence is cognizable.\n</blockquote>\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_172\">98.   We have already held that <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_279\">Section 66A</a> creates an offence\n\nwhich is vague and overbroad, and, therefore, unconstitutional\n\nunder <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_280\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> and not saved by <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_281\">Article 19(2).</a> We have\n\nalso held that the wider range of circulation over the internet\n\ncannot restrict the content of the right under <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_282\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> nor\n\ncan it justify its denial. However, when we come to discrimination\n\nunder <a href=\"/doc/367586/\" id=\"a_283\">Article 14</a>, we are unable to agree with counsel for the\n\npetitioners that there is no intelligible differentia between the\n\nmedium of print, broadcast and real live speech as opposed to\n\nspeech on the internet. The intelligible differentia is clear \u2013 the\n\ninternet gives any individual a platform which requires very little or\n\nno payment through which to air his views. The learned Additional\n\nSolicitor General has correctly said that something posted on a\n\nsite or website travels like lightning and can reach millions of\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_153\">\f                                  85</span>\n\npersons all over the world. If the petitioners were right, this <a href=\"/doc/367586/\" id=\"a_284\">Article\n\n14</a> argument would apply equally to all other offences created by\n\nthe <a href=\"/doc/1965344/\" id=\"a_285\">Information Technology Act</a> which are not the subject matter of\n\nchallenge in these petitions. We make it clear that there is an\n\nintelligible differentia between speech on the internet and other\n\nmediums of communication for which separate offences can\n\ncertainly be created by legislation. We find, therefore, that the\n\nchallenge on the ground of <a href=\"/doc/367586/\" id=\"a_286\">Article 14</a> must fail.\n\n\nProcedural Unreasonableness\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_173\">99.   One other argument must now be considered. According to\n\nthe petitioners, <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_287\">Section 66A</a> also suffers from the vice of\n\nprocedural unreasonableness.       In that, if, for example, criminal\n\ndefamation is alleged, the safeguards available under <a href=\"/doc/27007/\" id=\"a_288\">Section 199</a>\n\nCr.P.C. would not be available for a like offence committed under\n\n<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_289\">Section 66A</a>.     Such safeguards are that no court shall take\n\ncognizance of such an offence except upon a complaint made by\n\nsome person aggrieved by the offence and that such complaint will\n\nhave to be made within six months from the date on which the\n\noffence is alleged to have been committed. Further, safeguards\n\nthat are to be found in <a href=\"/doc/875455/\" id=\"a_290\">Sections 95</a> and <a href=\"/doc/1164912/\" id=\"a_291\">96</a> of the Cr.P.C. are also\n\nabsent when it comes to <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_292\">Section 66A</a>. For example, where any\n\nnewspaper book or document wherever printed appears to contain\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_154\">\f                                  86</span>\n\nmatter which is obscene, hurts the religious feelings of some\n\ncommunity, is seditious in nature, causes enmity or hatred to a\n\ncertain section of the public, or is against national integration, such\n\nbook, newspaper or document may be seized but under Section\n\n96 any person having any interest in such newspaper, book or\n\ndocument may within two months from the date of a publication\n\nseizing such documents, books or newspapers apply to the High\n\ncourt to set aside such declaration. Such matter is to be heard by\n\na Bench consisting of at least three Judges or in High Courts\n\nwhich consist of less than three Judges, such special Bench as\n\nmay be composed of all the Judges of that High Court.\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_174\">100. It is clear that <a href=\"/doc/875455/\" id=\"a_293\">Sections 95</a> and <a href=\"/doc/1164912/\" id=\"a_294\">96</a> of the Criminal Procedure\n\nCode reveal a certain degree of sensitivity to the fundamental right\n\nto free speech and expression. If matter is to be seized on specific\n\ngrounds which are relatable to the subject matters contained in\n\n<a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_295\">Article 19(2)</a>, it would be open for persons affected by such seizure\n\nto get a declaration from a High Court consisting of at least three\n\nJudges that in fact publication of the so-called offensive matter\n\ndoes not in fact relate to any of the specified subjects contained in\n\n<a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_296\">Article 19(2).</a>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_175\">      Further, <a href=\"/doc/967463/\" id=\"a_297\">Section 196</a> of the Cr.P.C. states:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_154\">      \u201c196. Prosecution for offences against the State\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_155\">\f                          87</span>\n\nand for criminal conspiracy to commit such\noffence.\u2014 (1) No Court shall take cognizance of\u2014\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_155\">(a) any offence punishable under Chapter VI or under\nSection 153-A, [Section 295-A or sub-section (1) of\nSection 505] of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 (45 of\n1860), or\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_156\">(b) a criminal conspiracy to commit such offence, or\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_157\">(c) any such abetment, as is described in <a href=\"/doc/1711828/\" id=\"a_298\">Section\n108-A</a> of the Indian Penal Code (45 of 1860),\nexcept with the previous sanction of the Central\nGovernment or of the State Government.\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_176\">[(1-A)\nNo Court shall take cognizance of\u2014\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_177\">(a) any offence punishable under Section 153-B or\nsub-section (2) or sub-section (3) of Section 505 of the\nIndian Penal Code, 1860 (45 of 1860), or\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_178\">(b) a criminal conspiracy to commit such offence,\nexcept with the previous sanction of the Central\nGovernment or of the State Government or of the\nDistrict Magistrate.]\n(2) No court shall take cognizance of the offence of any\ncriminal conspiracy punishable under <a href=\"/doc/1897847/\" id=\"a_299\">Section 120-B</a> of\nthe Indian Penal Code (45 of 1860), other than a\ncriminal conspiracy to commit [an offence] punishable\nwith death, imprisonment for life or rigorous\nimprisonment for a term of two years or upwards,\nunless the State Government or the District Magistrate\nhas consented in writing to the initiation of the\nproceedings:\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_158\">Provided that where the criminal conspiracy is one to\nwhich the provisions of Section 195 apply, no such\nconsent shall be necessary.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_159\"> (3) The Central Government or the State Government\nmay, before according sanction [under sub-section (1)\nor sub-section (1-A) and the District Magistrate may,\nbefore according sanction under sub-section (1-A)] and\nthe State Government or the District Magistrate may,\nbefore giving consent under sub-section (2), order a\npreliminary investigation by a police officer not being\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_156\">\f                                88</span>\n\n     below the rank of Inspector, in which case such police\n     officer shall have the powers referred to in sub-section\n     (3) of Section 155.\u201d\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_179\">101. Again, for offences in the nature of promoting enmity\n\nbetween different groups on grounds of religion etc. or offences\n\nrelatable to deliberate and malicious acts intending to outrage\n\nreligious feelings or statements that create or promote enmity,\n\nhatred or ill-will between classes can only be taken cognizance of\n\nby courts with the previous sanction of the Central Government or\n\nthe State Government. This procedural safeguard does not apply\n\neven when a similar offence may be committed over the internet\n\nwhere a person is booked under <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_300\">Section 66A</a> instead of the\n\naforesaid Sections.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_180\">     Having struck down <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_301\">Section 66A</a> on substantive grounds, we\n\nneed not decide the procedural unreasonableness aspect of the\n\nSection.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_181\">\n<a href=\"/doc/81889454/\" id=\"a_302\">Section 118</a> of the Kerala Police Act .\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"RespArg\" id=\"p_182\">102. Learned counsel for the Petitioner in Writ Petition No. 196 of\n\n2014 assailed sub-section (d) of Section 118 which is set out\n\nhereinbelow:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_160\">     \u201c118. Penalty for causing grave violation of public order\n     or danger.- Any person who,-\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_161\">     (d) Causes annoyance to any person in an indecent\n     manner by statements or  verbal or comments or\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_157\">\f                                   89</span>\n\n      telephone calls or calls of any type or by chasing or\n      sending messages or mails by any means;\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_183\">      shall, on conviction be punishable with imprisonment\n      for a term which may extend to three years or with fine\n      not exceeding ten thousand rupees or with both.\u201d\n\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_184\">103.
