Learned counsel first assailed the Section on the ground of\n\nlegislative competence stating that this being a <a href=\"/doc/162543282/\" id=\"a_303\">Kerala Act</a>, it would\n\nfall outside Entries1 and 2 of List II and fall within Entry 31 of List I.\n\nIn order to appreciate the argument we set out the relevant entries:\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_162\">      \u201cList - I\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_163\">      31. Posts and telegraphs; telephones, wireless,\n      broadcasting and other like forms of communication.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_185\">      List - II\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_186\">      1. Public order (but not including the use of any naval,\n      military or air force or any other armed force of the\n      Union or of any other force subject to the control of the\n      Union or of any contingent or unit thereof in aid of the\n      civil power).\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_187\">      2. Police (including railway and village police) subject\n      to the provisions of entry 2A of List I.\u201d\n\n      <a href=\"/doc/93131343/\" id=\"a_304\">The Kerala Police Act</a> as a whole would necessarily fall\n\nunder Entry 2 of List II. In addition, Section 118 would also fall\n\nwithin Entry 1 of List II in that as its marginal note tells us it deals\n\nwith penalties for causing grave violation of public order or danger.\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_188\">104. It is well settled that a statute cannot be dissected and then\n\nexamined as to under what field of legislation each part would\n\nseparately fall. <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1057797\" id=\"span_158\">In <a href=\"/doc/1057797/\" id=\"a_305\">A.S. Krishna v. State of Madras</a>, [1957] S.C.R.\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_159\">\f                                 90</span>\n\n399, the law is stated thus:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_164\">      \u201cThe position, then, might thus be summed up : When\n      a law is impugned on the ground that it is ultra vires\n      the powers of the legislature which enacted it, what\n      has to be ascertained is the true character of the\n      legislation. To do that, one must have regard to the\n      enactment as a whole, to its objects and to the scope\n      and effect of its provisions. If on such examination it is\n      found that the legislation is in substance one on a\n      matter assigned to the legislature, then it must be held\n      to be valid in its entirety, even though it might\n      incidentally trench on matters which are beyond its\n      competence. It would be quite an erroneous approach\n      to the question to view such a statute not as an organic\n      whole, but as a mere collection of sections, then\n      disintegrate it into parts, examine under what heads of\n      legislation those parts would severally fall, and by that\n      process determine what portions thereof are intra\n      vires, and what are not.\u201d (at page 410)\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_189\">105. It is, therefore, clear that the <a href=\"/doc/93131343/\" id=\"a_306\">Kerala Police Act</a> as a whole\n\nand Section 118 as part thereof falls in pith and substance within\n\nEntry 2 List II, notwithstanding any incidental encroachment that it\n\nmay have made on any other Entry in List I. Even otherwise, the\n\npenalty created for causing annoyance in an indecent manner in\n\npith and substance would fall within Entry 1 List III which speaks of\n\ncriminal law and would thus be within the competence of the State\n\nLegislature in any case.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_190\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_191\">106. However, what has been said about <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_307\">Section 66A</a> would apply\n\ndirectly to <a href=\"/doc/89023557/\" id=\"a_308\">Section 118(d)</a> of the Kerala Police Act, as causing\n\nannoyance in an indecent manner suffers from the same type of\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_160\">\f                                   91</span>\n\nvagueness and over breadth, that led to the invalidity of <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_309\">Section\n\n66A</a>, and for the reasons given for striking down <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_310\">Section 66A</a>,\n\nSection 118(d) also violates <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_311\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> and not being a\n\nreasonable restriction on the said right and not being saved under\n\nany of the subject matters contained in <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_312\">Article 19(2)</a> is hereby\n\ndeclared to be unconstitutional.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_192\">\n\n\n<a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_313\">Section 69A</a> and the Information Technology (Procedure and\nSafeguards for Blocking for Access of Information by Public)\nRules, 2009.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_193\">107. <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_314\">Section 69A</a> of the Information Technology Act has already\n\nbeen set out in paragraph 2 of the judgment. Under sub-section\n\n(2) thereof, the 2009 Rules have been framed. Under Rule 3, the\n\nCentral Government shall designate by notification in the official\n\ngazette an officer of the Central Government not below the rank of\n\na Joint Secretary as the Designated Officer for the purpose of\n\nissuing direction for blocking for access by the public any\n\ninformation referable to <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_315\">Section 69A</a> of the Act. Under Rule 4,\n\nevery organization as defined under Rule 2(g), (which refers to the\n\nGovernment of India, State Governments, Union Territories and\n\nagencies of the Central Government as may be notified in the\n\nOfficial Gazette by the Central Government)\u2013 is to designate one\n\nof its officers as the \u201cNodal Officer\u201d. Under Rule 6, any person\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_161\">\f                                 92</span>\n\nmay send their complaint to the \u201cNodal Officer\u201d of the concerned\n\nOrganization for blocking, which complaint will then have to be\n\nexamined by the concerned Organization regard being had to the\n\nparameters <a href=\"/doc/1057797/\" id=\"a_316\">laid down in</a> <a href=\"/doc/166979650/\" id=\"a_317\">Section 69A(1)</a> and after being so\n\nsatisfied, shall transmit such complaint through its Nodal Officer to\n\nthe Designated Officer in a format specified by the Rules. The\n\nDesignated Officer is not to entertain any complaint or request for\n\nblocking directly from any person. Under Rule 5, the Designated\n\nOfficer may on receiving any such request or complaint from the\n\nNodal Officer of an Organization or from a competent court, by\n\norder direct any intermediary or agency of the Government to\n\nblock any information or part thereof for the reasons specified in\n\n69A(1). Under Rule 7 thereof, the request/complaint shall then be\n\nexamined by a Committee of Government Personnel who under\n\nRule 8 are first to make all reasonable efforts to identify the\n\noriginator or intermediary who has hosted the information. If so\n\nidentified, a notice shall issue to appear and submit their reply at a\n\nspecified date and time which shall not be less than 48 hours from\n\nthe date and time of receipt of notice by such person or\n\nintermediary. The Committee then examines the request and is to\n\nconsider whether the request is covered by 69A(1) and is then to\n\ngive a specific recommendation in writing to the Nodal Officer of\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_162\">\f                                 93</span>\n\nthe concerned Organization. It is only thereafter that the\n\nDesignated Officer is to submit the Committee\u2019s recommendation\n\nto the Secretary, Department of Information Technology who is to\n\napprove such requests or complaints. Upon such approval, the\n\nDesignated Officer shall then direct any agency of Government or\n\nintermediary to block the offending information. Rule 9 provides for\n\nblocking of information in cases of emergency where delay caused\n\nwould be fatal in which case the blocking may take place without\n\nany opportunity of hearing. The Designated Officer shall then, not\n\nlater than 48 hours of the issue of the interim direction, bring the\n\nrequest before the Committee referred to earlier, and only on the\n\nrecommendation of the Committee, is the Secretary Department of\n\nInformation Technology to pass the final order. Under Rule 10, in\n\nthe case of an order of a competent court in India, the Designated\n\nOfficer shall, on receipt of a certified copy of a court order, submit\n\nit to the Secretary, Department of Information Technology and then\n\ninitiate action as directed by the Court. In addition to the above\n\nsafeguards, under Rule 14 a Review Committee shall meet at\n\nleast once in two months and record its findings as to whether\n\ndirections issued are in accordance with <a href=\"/doc/166979650/\" id=\"a_318\">Section 69A(1)</a> and if it is\n\nof the contrary opinion, the Review Committee may set aside such\n\ndirections and issue orders to unblock the said information. Under\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_163\">\f                                  94</span>\n\nRule 16, strict confidentiality shall be maintained regarding all the\n\nrequests and complaints received and actions taken thereof.\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_194\">108. Learned      counsel   for   the   petitioners   assailed   the\n\nconstitutional validity of <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_319\">Section 69A</a>, and assailed the validity of\n\nthe 2009 Rules.       According to learned counsel, there is no\n\npre-decisional hearing afforded by the Rules particularly to the\n\n\u201coriginator\u201d of information, which is defined under <a href=\"/doc/629401/\" id=\"a_320\">Section 2(za)</a> of\n\nthe Act to mean a person who sends, generates, stores or\n\ntransmits any electronic message; or causes any electronic\n\nmessage to be sent, generated, stored or transmitted to any other\n\nperson. Further, procedural safeguards such as which are\n\nprovided under <a href=\"/doc/875455/\" id=\"a_321\">Section 95</a> and <a href=\"/doc/1164912/\" id=\"a_322\">96</a> of the Code of Criminal\n\nProcedure are not available here.         Also, the confidentiality\n\nprovision was assailed stating that it affects the fundamental rights\n\nof the petitioners.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_195\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_196\">109. It will be noticed that <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_323\">Section 69A</a> unlike <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_324\">Section 66A</a> is a\n\nnarrowly drawn provision with several safeguards.          First and\n\nforemost, blocking can only be resorted to where the Central\n\nGovernment is satisfied that it is necessary so to do. Secondly,\n\nsuch necessity is relatable only to some of the subjects set out in\n\n<a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_325\">Article 19(2).</a> Thirdly, reasons have to be recorded in writing in\n\nsuch blocking order so that they may be assailed in a writ petition\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_164\">\f                                  95</span>\n\nunder <a href=\"/doc/1712542/\" id=\"a_326\">Article 226</a> of the Constitution.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_197\">110. The Rules further provide for a hearing before the Committee\n\nset up - which Committee then looks into whether or not it is\n\nnecessary to block such information.             It is only when the\n\nCommittee finds that there is such a necessity that a blocking\n\norder is made. It is also clear from an examination of Rule 8 that it\n\nis not merely the intermediary who may be heard. If the \u201cperson\u201d\n\ni.e. the originator is identified he is also to be heard before a\n\nblocking order is passed. Above all, it is only after these procedural\n\nsafeguards are met that blocking orders are made and in case\n\nthere is a certified copy of a court order, only then can such\n\nblocking order also be made. It is only an intermediary who finally\n\nfails to comply with the directions issued who is punishable under\n\nsub-section (3) of <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_327\">Section 69A</a>.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_198\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_199\">111. Merely because certain additional safeguards such as those\n\nfound in <a href=\"/doc/875455/\" id=\"a_328\">Section 95</a> and <a href=\"/doc/1164912/\" id=\"a_329\">96</a> CrPC are not available does not make\n\nthe Rules constitutionally infirm. We are of the view that the Rules\n\nare not constitutionally infirm in any manner.\n\n\n\n<a href=\"/doc/844026/\" id=\"a_330\">Section 79</a> and the Information Technology (Intermediary\nGuidelines) Rules, 2011.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_200\">112. <a href=\"/doc/844026/\" id=\"a_331\">Section 79</a> belongs to <a href=\"/doc/1965344/\" id=\"a_332\">Chapter XII of the Act</a> in which\n\nintermediaries are exempt from liability if they fulfill the conditions\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_165\">\f                                 96</span>\n\nof the Section. <a href=\"/doc/844026/\" id=\"a_333\">Section 79</a> states:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_165\">     \u201c79. Exemption from liability of intermediary in certain\n     cases.\u2014(1) Notwithstanding anything contained in any\n     law for the time being in force but subject to the\n     provisions of sub-sections (2) and (3), an intermediary\n     shall not be liable for any third party information, data,\n     or communication link made available or hosted by\n     him.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_166\">     (2) The provisions of sub-section (1) shall apply if\u2014\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_167\">     (a) the function of the intermediary is limited to\n     providing access to a communication system over\n     which information made available by third parties is\n     transmitted or temporarily stored or hosted; or\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_168\">     (b) the intermediary does not\u2014\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_169\">     (i) initiate the transmission,\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_170\">     (ii) select the receiver of the transmission, and\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_171\">     (iii) select or modify the information contained in the\n     transmission;\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_172\">     (c) the intermediary observes due diligence while\n     discharging his duties under this Act and also observes\n     such other guidelines as the Central Government may\n     prescribe in this behalf.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_173\">     (3) The provisions of sub-section (1) shall not apply if\u2014\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_174\">     (a) the intermediary has conspired or abetted or aided\n     or induced, whether by threats or promise or otherwise\n     in the commission of the unlawful act;\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_175\">     (b) upon receiving actual knowledge, or on being\n     notified by the appropriate Government or its agency\n     that any information, data or communication link\n     residing in or connected to a computer resource\n     controlled by the intermediary is being used to commit\n     the unlawful act, the intermediary fails to expeditiously\n     remove or disable access to that material on that\n     resource without vitiating the evidence in any manner.\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_201\">     Explanation.\u2014For the purposes of this section, the\n     expression \u201cthird party information\u201d means any\n     information dealt with by an intermediary in his\n     capacity as an intermediary.]\u201d\n\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_202\">113. Under the 2011 Rules, by Rule 3 an intermediary has not\n\nonly to publish the rules and regulations, privacy policy and user\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_166\">\f                                 97</span>\n\nagreement for access or usage of the intermediary\u2019s computer\n\nresource but he has also to inform all users of the various matters\n\nset out in Rule 3(2). Since Rule 3(2) and 3(4) are important, they\n\nare set out hereinbelow:-\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_176\">     \u201c3. Due diligence to be observed by intermediary.\n     \u2014The intermediary shall observe following due\n     diligence while discharging his duties, namely:\u2014\n\n     (2) Such rules and regulations, terms and conditions or\n     user agreement shall inform the users of computer\n     resource not to host, display, upload, modify, publish,\n     transmit, update or share any information that\u2014\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_177\">     (a) belongs to another person and to which the user\n     does not have any right to;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_178\">     (b) is grossly harmful, harassing, blasphemous\n     defamatory, obscene, pornographic, paedophilic,\n     libellous, invasive of another&#x27;s privacy, hateful, or\n     racially, ethnically objectionable, disparaging, relating\n     or encouraging money laundering or gambling, or\n     otherwise unlawful in any manner whatever;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_179\">     (c) harm minors in any way;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_180\">     (d) infringes any patent, trademark, copyright or other\n     proprietary rights;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_181\">     (e) violates any law for the time being in force;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_182\">     (f) deceives or misleads the addressee about the origin\n     of such messages or communicates any information\n     which is grossly offensive or menacing in nature;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_183\">     (g) impersonate another person;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_184\">     (h) contains software viruses or any other computer\n     code, files or programs designed to interrupt, destroy\n     or limit the functionality of any computer resource;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_185\">     (i) threatens the unity, integrity, defence, security or\n     sovereignty of India, friendly relations with foreign\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_167\">\f                                  98</span>\n\n      states, or public order or causes incitement to the\n      commission of any cognisable offence or prevents\n      investigation of any offence or is insulting any other\n      nation.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_186\">      (4) The intermediary, on whose computer system the\n      information is stored or hosted or published, upon\n      obtaining knowledge by itself or been brought to actual\n      knowledge by an affected person in writing or through\n      e-mail signed with electronic signature about any such\n      information as mentioned in sub-rule (2) above, shall\n      act within thirty-six hours and where applicable, work\n      with user or owner of such information to disable such\n      information that is in contravention of sub-rule (2).\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_203\">      Further the intermediary shall preserve such\n      information and associated records for at least ninety\n      days for investigation purposes.\u201d\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_204\">114. Learned counsel for the petitioners assailed Rules 3(2) and\n\n3(4) on two basic grounds. Firstly, the intermediary is called upon\n\nto exercise its own judgment under sub-rule (4) and then disable\n\ninformation that is in contravention of sub-rule (2), when\n\nintermediaries by their very definition are only persons who offer a\n\nneutral platform through which persons may interact with each\n\nother over the internet. Further, no safeguards are provided as in\n\nthe 2009 Rules made under <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_334\">Section 69A</a>.            Also, for the very\n\nreasons that <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_335\">Section 66A</a> is bad, the petitioners assailed sub-rule\n\n(2) of Rule 3 saying that it is vague and over broad and has no\n\nrelation with the subjects specified under <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_336\">Article 19(2).</a>\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_205\">115. One of the petitioners\u2019 counsel also assailed <a href=\"/doc/161852741/\" id=\"a_337\">Section 79(3)(b)</a>\n\nto the extent that it makes the intermediary exercise its own\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_168\">\f                                99</span>\n\njudgment upon receiving actual knowledge that any information is\n\nbeing used to commit unlawful acts.       Further, the expression\n\n\u201cunlawful acts\u201d also goes way beyond the specified subjects\n\ndelineated in <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_338\">Article 19(2).</a>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_206\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_207\">116. It must first be appreciated that <a href=\"/doc/844026/\" id=\"a_339\">Section 79</a> is an exemption\n\nprovision. Being an exemption provision, it is closely related to\n\nprovisions which provide for offences including <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_340\">Section 69A</a>. We\n\nhave seen how under <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_341\">Section 69A</a> blocking can take place only by\n\na reasoned order after complying with several procedural\n\nsafeguards including a hearing to the originator and intermediary.\n\nWe have also seen how there are only two ways in which a\n\nblocking order can be passed \u2013 one by the Designated Officer\n\nafter complying with the 2009 Rules and the other by the\n\nDesignated Officer when he has to follow an order passed by a\n\ncompetent court. The intermediary applying its own mind to\n\nwhether information should or should not be blocked is noticeably\n\nabsent in <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_342\">Section 69A</a> read with 2009 Rules.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_208\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_209\">117. <a href=\"/doc/161852741/\" id=\"a_343\">Section 79(3)(b)</a> has to be read down to mean that the\n\nintermediary upon receiving actual knowledge that a court order\n\nhas been passed asking it to expeditiously remove or disable\n\naccess to certain material must then fail to expeditiously remove or\n\ndisable access to that material.     This is for the reason that\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_169\">\f                                  100</span>\n\notherwise it would be very difficult for intermediaries like Google,\n\nFacebook etc. to act when millions of requests are made and the\n\nintermediary is then to judge as to which of such requests are\n\nlegitimate and which are not. We have been informed that in other\n\ncountries worldwide this view has gained acceptance, Argentina\n\nbeing in the forefront. Also, the Court order and/or the notification\n\nby the appropriate Government or its agency must strictly conform\n\nto the subject matters <a href=\"/doc/1057797/\" id=\"a_344\">laid down in</a> <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_345\">Article 19(2).</a> Unlawful acts\n\nbeyond what is <a href=\"/doc/1057797/\" id=\"a_346\">laid down in</a> <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_347\">Article 19(2)</a> obviously cannot form\n\nany part of <a href=\"/doc/844026/\" id=\"a_348\">Section 79</a>. With these two caveats, we refrain from\n\nstriking down <a href=\"/doc/161852741/\" id=\"a_349\">Section 79(3)(b)</a>.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_210\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_211\">118. The learned Additional Solicitor General informed us that it is\n\na common practice worldwide for intermediaries to have user\n\nagreements containing what is stated in Rule 3(2). However, Rule\n\n3(4) needs to be read down in the same manner as <a href=\"/doc/117237824/\" id=\"a_350\">Section 79(3)</a>\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_212\">(b). The knowledge spoken of in the said sub-rule must only be\n\nthrough the medium of a court order.          Subject to this, the\n\nInformation Technology (Intermediaries Guidelines) Rules, 2011\n\nare valid.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_213\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_214\">119. In conclusion, we may summarise what has been held by us\n\nabove:\n</p><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_170\">\f                                101</span><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_215\">\n\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_187\">(a)<a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_351\">Section 66A</a> of the Information Technology Act, 2000 is struck\n\n  down in its entirety being violative of <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_352\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> and not\n\n  saved under <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_353\">Article 19(2).</a>\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_188\">(b)<a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_354\">Section 69A</a> and the Information Technology (Procedure &amp;\n\n  Safeguards for Blocking for Access of Information by Public)\n\n    Rules 2009 are constitutionally valid.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_189\">(c) <a href=\"/doc/844026/\" id=\"a_355\">Section 79</a> is valid subject to <a href=\"/doc/161852741/\" id=\"a_356\">Section 79(3)(b)</a> being read down\n\n  to mean that an intermediary upon receiving actual knowledge\n\n  from a court order or on being notified by the appropriate\n\n  government or its agency that unlawful acts relatable to <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_357\">Article\n\n  19(2)</a> are going to be committed then fails to expeditiously\n\n  remove or disable access to such material.           Similarly, the\n\n  Information Technology \u201cIntermediary Guidelines\u201d Rules, 2011\n\n  are valid subject to Rule 3 sub-rule (4) being read down in the\n\n  same manner as indicated in the judgment.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_190\">(d)<a href=\"/doc/89023557/\" id=\"a_358\">Section 118(d)</a> of the Kerala Police Act is struck down being\n\n  violative of <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_359\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> and not saved by <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_360\">Article 19(2).</a>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_216\">     All the writ petitions are disposed in the above terms.\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_217\">                             \u2026.\u2026..\u2026..\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026...J.\n                             (J. Chelameswar)\n\n\n                              \u2026.\u2026..\u2026..\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026\u2026...J.\n                             (R.F. Nariman)\nNew Delhi,\nMarch 24, 2015.\n</p><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_171\">\f                                    102</span><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_218\">\n\n</p><pre id=\"pre_3\">ITEM NO.1A                   COURT NO.3                  SECTION PIL\n(For judgment)\n\n                 S U P R E M E C O U R T O F         I N D I A\n                         RECORD OF PROCEEDINGS\n\nWrit Petition(s)(Criminal)      No(s).    167/2012\n\nSHREYA SINGHAL                                            Petitioner(s)\n\n                                    VERSUS\n\nU.O.I. Mahesh Babu,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_261\">                    Mr. R. V. Kameshwaran,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_262\">                    Mr. Vijay Kumar,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_263\">                    Mr. Ravi Prakash Mehrotra,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_264\">                    Mr. Gaurav Bhatia,AAG, U.P.\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_182\">\f                                   104</span>\n\n                      Mr. Gaurav Srivastava,Adv.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_265\">                      Mr. Utkarsh Jaiswal,Adv.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_266\">                      Mr. Abhishek Chaudhary,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_267\">                      Mr. Mohit D. Ram,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_268\">                      Mr. P.S. Naarsimha,ASG\n                      Mr. V. Shyamohan,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_269\">                      Mr. P. Venkat Reddy,Adv.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_270\">                      Mr. Sumanth Nookala,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_271\">                      M/s. Venkat Palwai Law Associates\n\n                      Mr. Aftab Ali Khan,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_272\">                      Dr. Nafis A. Siddiqui,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_273\">                      Ms. Divya Roy,Adv.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_274\">\n\n           Hon&#x27;ble Mr. Justice Rohinton Fali Nariman pronounced the\njudgment   of   the   Bench   comprising   of   Hon&#x27;ble   Mr.   Justice   J.\nChelameswar and His Lordship.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_275\">\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_276\">           All the writ petitions are disposed of, in terms of the\nsigned reportable judgment.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_277\">\n\n</p><pre id=\"pre_4\">  [O.P. SHARMA]                             [SNEH BALA MEHRA]\n   COURT MASTER                            ASSISTANT REGISTRAR\n</pre><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_278\">     (Signed reportable judgment is placed on the file)\n\f</p>",

    "title": "Shreya Singhal vs U.O.I on 24 March, 2015",
    "doc": "<h2 class=\"doc_title\">Shreya Singhal vs U.O.I on 24 March, 2015</h2>\n\n<h3 class=\"doc_citations\">Equivalent citations: AIR 2015 SUPREME COURT 1523, 2015 AIR SCW 1989 AIR 2015 SC (CRIMINAL) 834, AIR 2015 SC (CRIMINAL) 834, 2015 AIR SCW 1989, (2015) 149 ALLINDCAS 224 (SC), AIR 2015 SC( CRI) 834, (2015) 3 MAD LJ 162, (2015) 1 UC 594, (2015) 2 BOMCR(CRI) 515, (2015) 2 CRILR(RAJ) 449, 2015 CALCRILR 3 748, 2015 CRILR(SC&MP) 449, (2015) 1 GUJ LH 741, (2015) 4 SCALE 1, (2015) 2 JLJR 161, (2015) 3 DLT(CRL) 192, (2015) 2 ALLCRILR 618, (2015) 218 DLT 370, (2015) 61 OCR 20, (2015) 2 ALD(CRL) 971, (2015) 2 CURCRIR 47, 2015 (5) SCC 1, (2015) 2 PAT LJR 138, (2015) 2 RECCRIR 403, 2015 CRILR(SC MAH GUJ) 449, 2015 (2) SCC (CRI) 449, 2015 (111) ALR SOC 2 (SC), 2015 (2) KLT SN 1 (SC)</h3>\n\n<h3 class=\"doc_author\">Author: <a href=\"/search/?formInput=authorid:r-f-nariman\">R.F. Nariman</a></h3>\n\n<h3 class=\"doc_bench\">Bench: <a href=\"/search/?formInput=benchid:r-f-nariman\">R.F. Nariman</a>, <a href=\"/search/?formInput=benchid:j-chelameswar\">J. Chelameswar</a></h3>\n\n<pre id=\"pre_1\">                                                                         [pic]REPORTABLE\n\n\n\n                        IN THE SUPREME COURT OF INDIA\n\n                    CRIMINAL/CIVIL ORIGINAL JURISDICTION\n\n                   WRIT PETITION (CRIMINAL) NO.167 OF 2012\n\n\n\nSHREYA SINGHAL                                ... PETITIONER\n\n                                   VERSUS\n\nUNION OF INDIA                               ... RESPONDENT\n\n                                    WITH\n\n\n\n                     WRIT PETITION (CIVIL) NO.21 OF 2013\n\n                     WRIT PETITION (CIVIL) NO.23 OF 2013\n\n                    WRIT PETITION (CIVIL) NO. 97 OF 2013\n\n                   WRIT PETITION (CRIMINAL) NO.199 OF 2013\n\n                    WRIT PETITION (CIVIL) NO. 217 OF 2013\n\n                   WRIT PETITION (CRIMINAL) NO.222 OF 2013\n\n                   WRIT PETITION (CRIMINAL) NO.225 OF 2013\n\n                    WRIT PETITION (CIVIL) NO.758 OF 2014\n\n                   WRIT PETITION (CRIMINAL) NO.196 OF 2014\n\n\n\n                               J U D G M E N T\n</pre>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_1\">R.F. NARIMAN, J.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_2\">1.         This batch of writ  petitions  filed  under  <a href=\"/doc/981147/\" id=\"a_1\">Article  32</a>  of  the\nConstitution of India  raises  very  important  and  far-reaching  questions\nrelatable primarily to the fundamental right of free speech  and  expression\nguaranteed by <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_2\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> of the Constitution of India.  The  immediate\ncause for concern in these petitions  is  <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_3\">Section  66A</a>  of  the  Information\nTechnology Act of 2000.  This Section was  not  in  the  Act  as  originally\nenacted, but came into force by virtue of an  Amendment  Act  of  2009  with\neffect from 27.10.2009.  Since all the arguments raised by  several  counsel\nfor the petitioners deal with the unconstitutionality of this Section it  is\nset out hereinbelow:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_1\">&quot;66-A. Punishment  for  sending  offensive  messages  through  communication\nservice, etc.-Any person who sends, by means of a  computer  resource  or  a\ncommunication device,-\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_2\">(a) any information that is grossly offensive or has menacing character; or\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_3\">(b) any information which he knows to be  false,  but  for  the  purpose  of\ncausing  annoyance,  inconvenience,  danger,  obstruction,  insult,  injury,\ncriminal intimidation, enmity, hatred or ill will,  persistently  by  making\nuse of such computer resource or a communication device; or\n\n\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_4\">(c) any electronic mail or  electronic  mail  message  for  the  purpose  of\ncausing  annoyance  or  inconvenience  or  to  deceive  or  to  mislead  the\naddressee or recipient about the origin of such messages,\n\n\nshall be punishable with imprisonment for a term which may extend  to  three\nyears and with fine.\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_3\">Explanation.- For the purposes of this section, terms &quot;electronic mail&quot;  and\n&quot;electronic  mail  message&quot;  means  a  message  or  information  created  or\ntransmitted or received on a computer, computer  system,  computer  resource\nor communication device including attachments in text, image,  audio,  video\nand  any  other  electronic  record,  which  may  be  transmitted  with  the\nmessage.&quot;[1]\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_4\">2.    A related challenge is also made to  <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_4\">Section  69A</a>  introduced  by  the\nsame amendment which reads as follows:-\n</p>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_5\">&quot;69-A. Power to issue directions for  blocking  for  public  access  of  any\ninformation through any computer resource.-(1) Where the Central  Government\nor any of its  officers  specially  authorised  by  it  in  this  behalf  is\nsatisfied that it is necessary or expedient so to do,  in  the  interest  of\nsovereignty and integrity of  India,  defence  of  India,  security  of  the\nState, friendly relations  with  foreign  States  or  public  order  or  for\npreventing incitement to the commission of any cognizable  offence  relating\nto above, it may subject to the provisions of sub-section (2),  for  reasons\nto be recorded in writing, by order, direct any agency of the Government  or\nintermediary to block for access by the public or cause to  be  blocked  for\naccess by the  public  any  information  generated,  transmitted,  received,\nstored or hosted in any computer resource.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_6\">(2) The procedure and safeguards subject to which such blocking  for  access\nby the public may be carried out, shall be such as may be prescribed.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_7\">(3) The intermediary who fails to comply with  the  direction  issued  under\nsub-section (1) shall be punished with an imprisonment for a term which  may\nextend to seven years and shall also be liable to fine.&quot;\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_5\">3.    The Statement of Objects  and  Reasons  appended  to  the  Bill  which\nintroduced the Amendment Act stated in paragraph 3 that:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_8\">&quot;3. A rapid increase in the use of computer and internet has given  rise  to\nnew  forms  of  crimes  like  publishing  sexually  explicit  materials   in\nelectronic form, video voyeurism and breach of confidentiality  and  leakage\nof data by intermediary, e-commerce frauds like personation  commonly  known\nas Phishing, identity theft and  offensive  messages  through  communication\nservices.   So,  penal  provisions  are  required  to  be  included  in  the\n<a href=\"/doc/1965344/\" id=\"a_5\">Information Technology Act</a>, the Indian Penal code, the <a href=\"/doc/1953529/\" id=\"a_6\">Indian  Evidence  Act</a>\nand the code of Criminal Procedure to prevent such crimes.&quot;\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_6\">4.    The petitioners contend that the very basis of <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_7\">Section 66A</a> -  that  it\nhas given rise to new forms of crimes - is incorrect, and that <a href=\"/doc/48199014/\" id=\"a_8\">Sections  66B</a>\nto <a href=\"/doc/91763765/\" id=\"a_9\">67C</a> and various  Sections  of  the  <a href=\"/doc/1569253/\" id=\"a_10\">Indian  Penal  Code</a>  (which  will  be\nreferred to hereinafter) are good enough to deal with all these crimes.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_7\">5.    The petitioners&#x27; various counsel raised a large number  of  points  as\nto the constitutionality of <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_11\">Section  66A</a>.   According  to  them,  first  and\nforemost <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_12\">Section 66A</a> infringes the fundamental  right  to  free  speech  and\nexpression and is not saved by any of the eight subjects covered in  <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_13\">Article\n19(2).</a>  According to them, the causing of annoyance, inconvenience,  danger,\nobstruction, insult, injury, criminal intimidation, enmity, hatred  or  ill-\nwill are all outside the purview of <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_14\">Article 19(2).</a>  Further, in creating  an\noffence, <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_15\">Section 66A</a> suffers from the vice of vagueness because  unlike  the\noffence created by <a href=\"/doc/326206/\" id=\"a_16\">Section 66</a> of the same Act, none of the  aforesaid  terms\nare even attempted to be defined and cannot be  defined,  the  result  being\nthat innocent persons are roped in as  well  as  those  who  are  not.  Such\npersons are not told clearly on which side of the line  they  fall;  and  it\nwould be open to the authorities to be as arbitrary and  whimsical  as  they\nlike in booking such persons under the  said  Section.   In  fact,  a  large\nnumber of innocent persons have been booked and  many  instances  have  been\ngiven in the form of a note to the  Court.   The  enforcement  of  the  said\nSection would really be an insidious form  of  censorship  which  impairs  a\ncore value contained in <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_17\">Article 19(1)(a).</a>  In  addition,  the  said  Section\nhas a chilling effect on the freedom of speech and  expression.   Also,  the\nright of viewers is infringed as such chilling effect would  not  give  them\nthe benefit of many shades of grey in terms of various points of  view  that\ncould be viewed over the internet.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_8\">The petitioners also contend that their rights under <a href=\"/doc/367586/\" id=\"a_18\">Articles 14</a> and <a href=\"/doc/1199182/\" id=\"a_19\">21</a>  are\nbreached inasmuch there is no intelligible  differentia  between  those  who\nuse the internet and those who by words spoken or written use other  mediums\nof communication. To punish somebody because he uses a particular medium  of\ncommunication is itself a discriminatory  object  and  would  fall  foul  of\n<a href=\"/doc/367586/\" id=\"a_20\">Article 14</a> in any case.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_9\">6.    In reply, Mr.  Tushar  Mehta,  learned  Additional  Solicitor  General\ndefended  the  constitutionality  of  <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_21\">Section  66A</a>.  He  argued   that   the\nlegislature is in the best position to understand and appreciate  the  needs\nof the people.  The Court will, therefore, interfere  with  the  legislative\nprocess only when a statute is clearly violative of the rights conferred  on\nthe citizen under Part-III of the Constitution.   There is a presumption  in\nfavour of the constitutionality of an enactment.  Further, the  Court  would\nso construe a statute to make it workable and in doing so can read  into  it\nor read down the provisions that are impugned.  The  Constitution  does  not\nimpose impossible standards of determining validity.   Mere  possibility  of\nabuse of a provision cannot be a ground  to  declare  a  provision  invalid.\nLoose language may have been used in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_22\">Section 66A</a> to deal with novel  methods\nof disturbing other people&#x27;s rights by using the internet as a  tool  to  do\nso.   Further,  vagueness  is  not   a   ground   to   declare   a   statute\nunconstitutional if the statute is  otherwise  legislatively  competent  and\nnon-arbitrary.  He cited a large number of judgments  before  us  both  from\nthis Court and from overseas to buttress his submissions.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_10\">Freedom of Speech and Expression\n\n<a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_23\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> of the Constitution of India states as follows:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_9\">&quot;<a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_24\">Article 19.</a> Protection of certain rights regarding freedom of speech, etc.-\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_10\">(1) All citizens shall have the right-\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_11\">(a) to freedom of speech and expression;&quot;\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_11\">7.    <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_25\">Article 19(2)</a> states:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_12\">&quot;<a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_26\">Article 19.</a> Protection of certain rights regarding freedom of speech, etc.-\n(2) Nothing in sub-clause (a) of clause (1) shall affect  the  operation  of\nany existing law, or prevent the State from making any law,  in  so  far  as\nsuch law imposes reasonable  restrictions  on  the  exercise  of  the  right\nconferred by the said sub-clause in the interests  of  the  sovereignty  and\nintegrity of India, the security  of  the  State,  friendly  relations  with\nforeign States,  public  order,  decency  or  morality  or  in  relation  to\ncontempt of court, defamation or incitement to an offence.&quot;\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_12\">8.    The Preamble of  the  Constitution  of  India  inter  alia  speaks  of\nliberty of thought, expression, belief, faith and  worship.   It  also  says\nthat India is a sovereign democratic republic. It cannot be over  emphasized\nthat when it comes to democracy, liberty of  thought  and  expression  is  a\ncardinal value that is of paramount significance  under  our  constitutional\nscheme.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_13\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"456839\" id=\"span_1\">9.    Various judgments of this Court have referred  to  the  importance  of\nfreedom of speech and expression both from the point of view of the  liberty\nof the individual and from the point of  view  of  our  democratic  form  of\ngovernment.  For example, in the early case of <a href=\"/doc/456839/\" id=\"a_27\">Romesh Thappar  v.  State  of\nMadras</a>, [1950] S.C.R. 594 at 602, this Court stated that freedom  of  speech\nlay at the foundation of all democratic organizations.</span>  <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"243002\" id=\"span_2\">In <a href=\"/doc/243002/\" id=\"a_28\">Sakal Papers  (P)\nLtd. &amp; Ors. v. Union of India</a>, [1962] 3 S.C.R. 842 at  866,  a  Constitution\nBench of this Court said freedom of speech and expression of opinion  is  of\nparamount  importance  under  a  democratic  constitution  which   envisages\nchanges in the composition of  legislatures  and  governments  and  must  be\npreserved.</span>  <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"125596\" id=\"span_3\">In a  separate  concurring  judgment  Beg,J.  said,  in  <a href=\"/doc/125596/\" id=\"a_29\">Bennett\nColeman &amp; Co. &amp; Ors. v. Union of India &amp; Ors.</a>, [1973] 2 S.C.R. 757  at  829,\nthat the freedom of speech and of the press is the Ark of  the  Covenant  of\nDemocracy because public criticism  is  essential  to  the  working  of  its\ninstitutions.[2]</span>\n\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_14\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1327342\" id=\"span_4\">10.   Equally, in <a href=\"/doc/1327342/\" id=\"a_30\">S. Khushboo v. Kanniamal &amp; Anr.</a>, (2010)  5  SCC  600  this\nCourt stated, in paragraph 45 that the importance of freedom of  speech  and\nexpression though  not  absolute  was  necessary  as  we  need  to  tolerate\nunpopular views. This right requires the free flow  of  opinions  and  ideas\nessential to  sustain  the  collective  life  of  the  citizenry.  While  an\ninformed  citizenry  is  a  pre-condition  for  meaningful  governance,  the\nculture of open dialogue is generally of great societal importance.</span>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_15\">11.   This last judgment is important in  that  it  refers  to  the  &quot;market\nplace of ideas&quot; concept that has permeated American Law.  This  was  put  in\nthe felicitous words of Justice Holmes in his famous dissent  in  Abrams  v.\nUnited States, 250 US 616 (1919), thus:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_13\">&quot;But when men have realized that time has upset many fighting  faiths,  they\nmay come to believe even more than they  believe  the  very  foundations  of\ntheir own conduct that the ultimate good desired is better reached  by  free\ntrade in ideas-that the best test of truth is the power of  thought  to  get\nitself accepted in the competition of the market,  and  that  truth  is  the\nonly ground upon which their wishes safely can be carried out. That  at  any\nrate is the theory of our Constitution.&quot;\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_16\">12.   Justice Brandeis in his  famous  concurring  judgment  in  Whitney  v.\nCalifornia, 71 L. Ed. 1095 said:\n</p>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_14\">&quot;Those who won our independence believed that the final  end  of  the  state\nwas to make men free to develop their faculties, and that in its  government\nthe deliberative forces should  prevail  over  the  arbitrary.  They  valued\nliberty both as an end and as a means.  They  believed  liberty  to  be  the\nsecret of happiness and courage to be the secret of liberty.  They  believed\nthat freedom to think as you will and  to  speak  as  you  think  are  means\nindispensable to the discovery and spread of political truth;  that  without\nfree speech and  assembly  discussion  would  be  futile;  that  with  them,\ndiscussion affords ordinarily adequate protection against the  dissemination\nof noxious doctrine; that  the  greatest  menace  to  freedom  is  an  inert\npeople; that public discussion is a political duty; and that this should  be\na fundamental principle of the  American  government.  They  recognized  the\nrisks to which all human institutions are subject. But they knew that  order\ncannot be secured merely through fear  of  punishment  for  its  infraction;\nthat it is hazardous to discourage thought, hope and imagination; that  fear\nbreeds repression; that repression breeds hate;  that  hate  menaces  stable\ngovernment; that the path of safety  lies  in  the  opportunity  to  discuss\nfreely supposed grievances and  proposed  remedies;  and  that  the  fitting\nremedy for evil counsels is good ones. Believing in the power of  reason  as\napplied through public discussion, they eschewed silence coerced by  law-the\nargument of force in its worst form. Recognizing  the  occasional  tyrannies\nof governing majorities, they amended the Constitution so that  free  speech\nand assembly should be guaranteed.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_15\">Fear of serious injury cannot alone justify suppression of free  speech  and\nassembly. Men feared witches and burnt women. It is the function  of  speech\nto free men from the bondage of irrational fears. To justify suppression  of\nfree speech there must be reasonable ground to fear that serious  evil  will\nresult if free speech is practiced.  There  must  be  reasonable  ground  to\nbelieve that the danger apprehended is imminent. There  must  be  reasonable\nground to believe that the evil to be prevented  is  a  serious  one.  Every\ndenunciation  of  existing  law  tends  in  some  measure  to  increase  the\nprobability that there will be violation  of  it. Condonation  of  a  breach\nenhances the probability. Expressions of approval add  to  the  probability.\nPropagation of the criminal state of mind by teaching syndicalism  increases\nit. Advocacy of lawbreaking heightens it still further.  But  even  advocacy\nof violation, however reprehensible morally,  is  not  a  justification  for\ndenying free speech where the advocacy falls short of incitement  and  there\nis nothing to indicate that the advocacy would be immediately acted on.  The\nwide difference between advocacy and  incitement,  between  preparation  and\nattempt, between assembling and conspiracy, must be borne in mind. In  order\nto support a finding of clear and present danger it  must  be  shown  either\nthat immediate serious violence was to be  expected  or  was  advocated,  or\nthat the past conduct furnished reason to believe  that  such  advocacy  was\nthen contemplated.&quot; (at page 1105, 1106)\n\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_17\">13.   This leads  us  to  a  discussion  of  what  is  the  content  of  the\nexpression &quot;freedom of speech and expression&quot;.   There  are  three  concepts\nwhich are fundamental in understanding the  reach  of  this  most  basic  of\nhuman rights.  The first is discussion, the  second  is  advocacy,  and  the\nthird is incitement.  Mere discussion  or  even  advocacy  of  a  particular\ncause howsoever unpopular is at the heart of <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_31\">Article 19(1)(a).</a>  It  is  only\nwhen such discussion or  advocacy  reaches  the  level  of  incitement  that\n<a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_32\">Article 19(2)</a> kicks in.[3]  It is at this stage  that  a  law  may  be  made\ncurtailing the speech or expression that leads inexorably  to  or  tends  to\ncause public disorder or tends to cause or tends to affect  the  sovereignty\n&amp; integrity of India, the security of the  State,  friendly  relations  with\nforeign States, etc. Why it is important to have  these  three  concepts  in\nmind is because most of the arguments of both  petitioners  and  respondents\ntended to veer around the expression &quot;public order&quot;.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_18\">14.   It is at this point that a word needs to be  said  about  the  use  of\nAmerican judgments in the context of <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_33\">Article 19(1)(a).</a>  In  virtually  every\nsignificant judgment of this Court, reference has  been  made  to  judgments\nfrom across the Atlantic.  Is it safe to do so?\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_19\">15.   It is significant to notice  first  the  differences  between  the  US\nFirst Amendment and <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_34\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> read with  <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_35\">Article  19(2).</a>   The  first\nimportant difference is the absoluteness  of  the  U.S.  first  Amendment  -\nCongress shall make no law which abridges the freedom  of  speech.   Second,\nwhereas the U.S. First Amendment speaks of freedom  of  speech  and  of  the\npress, without any reference to &quot;expression&quot;,  <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_36\">Article  19(1)(a)</a>  speaks  of\nfreedom of speech and expression  without  any  reference  to  &quot;the  press&quot;.\nThird, under the US Constitution, speech may be abridged, whereas under  our\nConstitution, reasonable restrictions may  be  imposed.  Fourth,  under  our\nConstitution  such  restrictions  have  to  be  in  the  interest  of  eight\ndesignated  subject  matters  -  that  is  any  law  seeking  to  impose   a\nrestriction on the  freedom  of  speech  can  only  pass  muster  if  it  is\nproximately related to any of the eight subject matters set out  in  <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_37\">Article\n19(2).</a>\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_20\">16.   Insofar as the  first  apparent  difference  is  concerned,  the  U.S.\nSupreme Court has  never  given  literal  effect  to  the  declaration  that\nCongress shall make no law abridging the freedom of  speech.   The  approach\nof the Court which is succinctly stated in one of  the  early  U.S.  Supreme\nCourt Judgments, continues even today.  In Chaplinsky v. New  Hampshire,  86\nL. Ed. 1031, Justice Murphy who delivered the opinion of the  Court  put  it\nthus:-\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_16\">&quot;Allowing the broadest scope to the language and purpose of  the  Fourteenth\nAmendment, it is well understood that  the  right  of  free  speech  is  not\nabsolute at all times and under all circumstances. There are  certain  well-\ndefined  and  narrowly  limited  classes  of  speech,  the  prevention   and\npunishment of which has never  been  thought  to  raise  any  Constitutional\nproblem. These include the lewd and obscene, the profane, the libelous,  and\nthe insulting or  &#x27;fighting&#x27;  words-those  which  by  their  very  utterance\ninflict injury or tend to incite an immediate breach of  the  peace. It  has\nbeen well observed that  such  utterances  are  no  essential  part  of  any\nexposition of ideas, and are of such slight social value as a step to  truth\nthat any benefit that may be derived from them is clearly outweighed by  the\nsocial interest in order  and  morality. &#x27;Resort  to  epithets  or  personal\nabuse is not in any proper sense communication  of  information  or  opinion\nsafeguarded by the Constitution, and its punishment as a criminal act  would\nraise no question under that instrument.&#x27; Cantwell v. Connecticut, 310  U.S.\n296, 309, 310, <a href=\"/doc/1408526/\" id=\"a_38\">60</a> S.Ct. 900, 906, 84  L.Ed.1213,  128  A.L.R.  1352.&quot;    (at\npage 1035)\n\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_21\">17.   So far as the second apparent difference is  concerned,  the  American\nSupreme Court has included &quot;expression&quot; as part of  freedom  of  speech  and\nthis  Court  has  included  &quot;the  press&quot;  as  being  covered  under  <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_39\">Article\n19(1)(a)</a>, so that, as a matter of judicial interpretation, both the  US  and\nIndia protect the  freedom  of  speech  and  expression  as  well  as  press\nfreedom.  Insofar as abridgement and reasonable restrictions are  concerned,\nboth the U.S. Supreme Court and this Court have held that a  restriction  in\norder to be reasonable must be narrowly tailored or narrowly interpreted  so\nas to abridge or restrict only what is  absolutely  necessary.  It  is  only\nwhen it comes to the eight subject matters that there is a vast  difference.\n In the U.S., if there is a compelling necessity  to  achieve  an  important\ngovernmental or societal goal, a law abridging freedom of  speech  may  pass\nmuster.  But in India, such law cannot pass muster if it is in the  interest\nof the general public.  Such law has to be  covered  by  one  of  the  eight\nsubject matters set out under  <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_40\">Article  19(2).</a>   If  it  does  not,  and  is\noutside the pale of 19(2), Indian courts will strike down such law.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_22\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"687159\" id=\"span_5\">18.   Viewed from the  above  perspective,  American  judgments  have  great\npersuasive value on the content of freedom of speech and expression and  the\ntests laid down for its infringement.  It is only  when  it  comes  to  sub-\nserving  the  general  public  interest  that  there  is  the  world  of   a\ndifference. This is perhaps why in <a href=\"/doc/687159/\" id=\"a_41\">Kameshwar Prasad &amp; Ors. v. The  State  of\nBihar &amp; Anr.</a>, 1962 Supp. (3) S.C.R. 369, this Court held:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_17\">&quot;As regards these decisions of the American Courts, it should  be  borne  in\nmind that though the First Amendment  to  the  Constitution  of  the  United\nState reading &quot;Congress shall make  no  law....  abridging  the  freedom  of\nspeech...&quot; appears to  confer  no  power  on  the  Congress  to  impose  any\nrestriction on the exercise of the guaranteed right,  still  it  has  always\nbeen understood that the freedom guaranteed is subject to the  police  power\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_18\">- the scope of  which  however  has  not  been  defined  with  precision  or\nuniformly. It is on the basis of the police power to  abridge  that  freedom\nthat the constitutional  validity  of  laws  penalising  libels,  and  those\nrelating to sedition, or to obscene publications etc., has  been  sustained.\nThe resultant flexibility of the restrictions that could be validly  imposed\nrenders the American decisions inapplicable to  and  without  much  use  for\nresolving  the  questions  arising  under  <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_42\">Art. 19(1) (a)</a>  or  (b)  of   our\nConstitution wherein the grounds on which limitations  might  be  placed  on\nthe guaranteed right are set out with  definiteness  and  precision.&quot;  (  At\npage 378)\n\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_23\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"223504\" id=\"span_6\">19.   But when it comes to understanding the impact and content  of  freedom\nof speech, in <a href=\"/doc/223504/\" id=\"a_43\">Indian Express Newspapers (Bombay) Private Limited &amp;  Ors.  v.\nUnion of India &amp; Ors.</a>, (1985) 2 SCR 287, Venkataramiah,J. stated:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_19\"> &quot;While examining the  constitutionality  of  a  law  which  is  alleged  to\ncontravene <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_44\">Article 19 (1) (a)</a> of the Constitution, we cannot, no  doubt,  be\nsolely guided by the decisions of the Supreme Court of the United States  of\nAmerica. But in order to understand  the  basic  principles  of  freedom  of\nspeech and expression  and  the  need  for  that  freedom  in  a  democratic\ncountry, we may take them into consideration. The pattern of <a href=\"/doc/1142233/\" id=\"a_45\">Article 19  (1)</a>\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_20\">(a) and of <a href=\"/doc/935769/\" id=\"a_46\">Article 19 (1) (g)</a> of our  constitution  is  different  from  the\npattern of the First Amendment to the American Constitution which is  almost\nabsolute in its terms. The rights guaranteed under <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_47\">Article 19  (1)  (a)</a>  and\n<a href=\"/doc/935769/\" id=\"a_48\">Article 19 (1) (g)</a> of the Constitution are to be  read  along  with  clauses\n(2) and (6) of <a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_49\">Article 19</a> which carve out areas in respect  of  which  valid\nlegislation can be made.&quot; (at page 324)\n\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Issue\" id=\"p_24\">20.   With these prefatory remarks, we will now go to the other  aspects  of\nthe challenge made in these writ petitions and argued before us.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_25\">A. <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_50\">Article 19(1)(a) -</a>\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_26\"><a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_51\">Section 66A</a> has been challenged on the ground that it  casts  the  net  very\nwide - &quot;all information&quot; that is disseminated over the internet is  included\nwithin its  reach.   It  will  be  useful  to  note  that  <a href=\"/doc/629401/\" id=\"a_52\">Section  2(v)</a>  of\nInformation Technology Act, 2000 defines information as follows:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_21\">&quot;2. Definitions.-(1) In this Act, unless the context otherwise requires,-\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_22\">(v)  &quot;Information&quot;  includes data,  message,  text,  images,  sound,  voice,\ncodes,  computer  programmes,  software  and  databases  or  micro  film  or\ncomputer generated micro fiche.&quot;\n</blockquote>\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_27\">Two things will  be  noticed.  The  first  is  that  the  definition  is  an\ninclusive one.  Second, the definition does not refer to  what  the  content\nof information can be.  In fact, it refers only to the medium through  which\nsuch  information  is  disseminated.   It  is  clear,  therefore,  that  the\npetitioners are correct in  saying  that  the  public&#x27;s  right  to  know  is\ndirectly affected by <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_53\">Section 66A</a>.  Information of all kinds is  roped  in  -\nsuch information may have scientific, literary or  artistic  value,  it  may\nrefer to  current  events,  it  may  be  obscene  or  seditious.  That  such\ninformation may cause annoyance or inconvenience to some is how the  offence\nis made out.  It is clear that the right of the people to know - the  market\nplace of ideas - which the internet provides to  persons  of  all  kinds  is\nwhat attracts <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_54\">Section 66A</a>.  That the information sent has  to  be  annoying,\ninconvenient, grossly offensive etc., also  shows  that  no  distinction  is\nmade between mere discussion or advocacy  of  a  particular  point  of  view\nwhich may be annoying or inconvenient  or  grossly  offensive  to  some  and\nincitement by which such words lead to an imminent  causal  connection  with\npublic disorder, security of  State  etc.   The  petitioners  are  right  in\nsaying that <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_55\">Section 66A</a> in creating an offence against persons who  use  the\ninternet and annoy or cause inconvenience to  others  very  clearly  affects\nthe freedom of speech and expression of the citizenry of India at  large  in\nthat such speech or expression is directly curbed by  the  creation  of  the\noffence contained in <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_56\">Section 66A</a>.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_28\">In  this  regard,  the  observations  of   Justice   Jackson   in   American\nCommunications Association v. Douds, 94 L. Ed. 925 are apposite:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_23\">&quot;Thought control is a copyright of totalitarianism, and we have no claim  to\nit.  It is not the function of our  Government  to  keep  the  citizen  from\nfalling into  error;  it  is  the  function  of  the  citizen  to  keep  the\nGovernment from falling into error.  We could justify  any  censorship  only\nwhen the censors are better shielded against error than the censored.&quot;\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_29\"><a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_57\">Article 19(2)</a>\n\nOne challenge to <a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_58\">Section 66A</a> made by the petitioners&#x27; counsel  is  that  the\noffence created by the said Section has no proximate relation  with  any  of\nthe eight subject matters contained in <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_59\">Article 19(2).</a>  We  may  incidentally\nmention that the State has claimed that the said Section  can  be  supported\nunder the heads of public order, defamation, incitement to  an  offence  and\ndecency or morality.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_30\">21.   Under our constitutional scheme, as stated earlier, it is not open  to\nthe State to curtail  freedom  of  speech  to  promote  the  general  public\ninterest.  <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"243002\" id=\"span_7\">In <a href=\"/doc/243002/\" id=\"a_60\">Sakal Papers (P) Ltd. &amp; Ors.  v.  Union  of  India</a>,  [1962]  3\nS.C.R. 842, this Court said:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_24\">&quot;It may well be within the power of the State to place, in the  interest  of\nthe general public, restrictions upon the right of a  citizen  to  carry  on\nbusiness but it is not open to the State to achieve this object by  directly\nand immediately curtailing any other freedom of that citizen  guaranteed  by\nthe Constitution and which is not susceptible  of  abridgment  on  the  same\ngrounds as are set out in clause (6) of <a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_61\">Article 19.</a> Therefore, the right  of\nfreedom  of  speech  cannot  be  taken  away  with  the  object  of  placing\nrestrictions on the business activities of a citizen. Freedom of speech  can\nbe restricted only in the interests of the security of the  State,  friendly\nrelations with foreign State,  public  order,  decency  or  morality  or  in\nrelation to contempt of court, defamation or incitement to  an  offence.  It\ncannot, like the freedom to carry on business, be curtailed in the  interest\nof the general public. If a law directly affecting it is challenged,  it  is\nno answer that the restrictions enacted by it are justifiable under  clauses\n(3) to (6). For,  the  scheme  of  <a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_62\">Article  19</a>  is  to  enumerate  different\nfreedoms separately and then to specify the extent of restrictions to  which\nthey may be subjected and the objects  for  securing  which  this  could  be\ndone. A citizen is entitled to enjoy each and  every  one  of  the  freedoms\ntogether and clause (1) does not prefer one freedom to another. That is  the\nplain meaning of this clause. It follows from this  that  the  State  cannot\nmake a law which directly  restricts  one  freedom  even  for  securing  the\nbetter enjoyment of another freedom. All the greater reason,  therefore  for\nholding that the State cannot directly restrict one freedom  by  placing  an\notherwise permissible restriction on another freedom.&quot; (at page 863)\n\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_31\">22.   Before we come to each of these expressions, we must  understand  what\nis meant by the expression &quot;in the interests of&quot;.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1386353\" id=\"span_8\">In  <a href=\"/doc/1386353/\" id=\"a_63\">The  Superintendent,\nCentral Prison, Fatehgarh v. Ram Manohar Lohia</a>, [1960] 2  S.C.R.  821,  this\nCourt laid down:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_25\">&quot;We do not understand the observations of the Chief  Justice  to  mean  that\nany remote or fanciful connection between the impugned Act  and  the  public\norder would be  sufficient  to  sustain  its  validity.  The  learned  Chief\nJustice was only making a distinction between an  Act  which  expressly  and\ndirectly purported to maintain public order and one which did not  expressly\nstate the said purpose but left it to be implied there from; and between  an\nAct that directly maintained public order and that indirectly brought  about\nthe same result. The distinction does not ignore the necessity for  intimate\nconnection between the Act and the public order sought to be  maintained  by\nthe Act.&quot; (at pages 834, 835)\n\n&quot;The restriction made &quot;in the interests of  public  order&quot;  must  also  have\nreasonable relation to the object to be achieved, i.e.,  the  public  order.\nIf the restriction has no  proximate  relationship  to  the  achievement  of\npublic order, it cannot  be  said  that  the  restriction  is  a  reasonable\nrestriction within the meaning of the said clause.&quot; (at page 835)\n\n\n\n&quot;The decision, in our view, lays  down  the  correct  test.  The  limitation\nimposed in the interests of public order to  be  a  reasonable  restriction,\nshould be one which has a proximate connection or nexus with  public  order,\nbut not one far-fetched, hypothetical or problematical or too remote in  the\nchain of its relation with the public order..........There is  no  proximate\nor even foreseeable connection  between  such  instigation  and  the  public\norder sought to be protected under section. We cannot  accept  the  argument\nof the learned Advocate General that instigation of a single individual  not\nto pay tax or  dues  is  a  spark  which  may  in  the  long  run  ignite  a\nrevolutionary movement destroying public order&quot; (at page 836).\n</blockquote>\n\n\n\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_26\">Reasonable Restrictions:\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_32\">23.   This Court has laid  down  what  &quot;reasonable  restrictions&quot;  means  in\nseveral cases.  <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1256541\" id=\"span_9\">In <a href=\"/doc/1256541/\" id=\"a_64\">Chintaman Rao v. The  State  of  Madhya  Pradesh</a>,  [1950]\nS.C.R. 759, this Court said:</span>\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_27\">&quot;The phrase &quot;reasonable restriction&quot; connotes that  the  limitation  imposed\non a person in enjoyment of the right should  not  be  arbitrary  or  of  an\nexcessive nature, beyond what is required in the interests  of  the  public.\nThe word &quot;reasonable&quot; implies intelligent care and  deliberation,  that  is,\nthe choice of a course which reason dictates. Legislation which  arbitrarily\nor excessively invades the right cannot be said to contain  the  quality  of\nreasonableness and unless it strikes a proper balance  between  the  freedom\nguaranteed in <a href=\"/doc/935769/\" id=\"a_65\">article 19(1)(g)</a> and the social control  permitted  by  clause\n(6) of <a href=\"/doc/1218090/\" id=\"a_66\">article 19</a>, it must be held to be  wanting  in  that  quality.&quot;   (at\npage 763)\n\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_33\">24.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"554839\" id=\"span_10\">In <a href=\"/doc/554839/\" id=\"a_67\">State of Madras v. V.G. Row</a>, [1952] S.C.R. 597, this Court said:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_28\">&quot;This Court had occasion in Dr. Khare&#x27;s case (1950)  S.C.R.  519  to  define\nthe scope of the judicial review under clause  (5)  of  Article19 where  the\nphrase &quot;imposing reasonable restriction on the exercise of the  right&quot;  also\noccurs and four out  of  the  five  Judges  participating  in  the  decision\nexpressed the view (the other Judge leaving the  question  open)  that  both\nthe substantive and the procedural aspects of the impugned  restrictive  law\nshould be examined from the point of view  of  reasonableness;  that  is  to\nsay, the Court should consider not only factors such  as  the  duration  and\nthe extent of the restrictions, but also the circumstances under  which  and\nthe manner in which their imposition has been authorised.  It  is  important\nin this context to bear in mind that the test of reasonableness, where  ever\nprescribed, should be applied to each, individual statute  impugned  and  no\nabstract standard, or general pattern of reasonableness can be laid down  as\napplicable to all cases. The nature  of  the  right  alleged  to  have  been\ninfringed, the underlying purpose of the  restriction  imposed,  the  extent\nand urgency of the evil sought to be remedied thereby, the disproportion  of\nthe imposition, the prevailing conditions at  the  time,  should  all  enter\ninto the judicial verdict. In evaluating such elusive  factors  and  forming\ntheir own conception of what is reasonable, in all the  circumstances  of  a\ngiven case, it is inevitable that the social philosophy  and  the  scale  of\nvalues of the judges participating in the decision should play an  important\npart, and the limit to their interference with legislative judgment in  such\ncases can only be dictated  by  their  sense  of  responsibility  and  self-\nrestraint and the sobering reflection that the  Constitution  is  meant  not\nonly for people of their way of thinking but for all, and that the  majority\nof the elected representatives  of  the  people  have,  in  authorising  the\nimposition of the restrictions, considered them to be reasonable.&quot; (at  page\n606-607)\n\n\n\n\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_34\"><span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1849054\" id=\"span_11\">25.   Similarly, in <a href=\"/doc/1849054/\" id=\"a_68\">Mohd. Faruk v. State of Madhya Pradesh &amp; Ors.</a>, [1970]  1\nS.C.R. 156, this Court said:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_29\">&quot;The Court must in considering the validity of the impugned law  imposing  a\nprohibition on the carrying on of  a  business  or  profession,  attempt  an\nevaluation of its direct and immediate impact upon  the  fundamental  rights\nof the citizens affected thereby and the larger public  interest  sought  to\nbe ensured in the light of the object sought to be achieved,  the  necessity\nto restrict the citizen&#x27;s freedom, the inherent  pernicious  nature  of  the\nact prohibited or its capacity or tendency to  be  harmful  to  the  general\npublic, the possibility of achieving the object by imposing a  less  drastic\nrestraint, and  in  the  absence  of  exceptional  situations  such  as  the\nprevalence of a state of emergency-national or  local-or  the  necessity  to\nmaintain essential supplies, or the necessity to stop activities  inherently\ndangerous, the existence  of  a  machinery  to  satisfy  the  administrative\nauthority that no case for imposing the restriction is made out  or  that  a\nless drastic restriction may ensure the object  intended  to  be  achieved.&quot;\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_35\">(at page 161)\n\n\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_36\">26.   <span class=\"citetext\" data-sentiment=\"Pos\" data-docid=\"1321505\" id=\"span_12\">In  <a href=\"/doc/1321505/\" id=\"a_69\">Dr.  N.  B.  Khare  v.  State  of  Delhi</a>,  [1950]  S.C.R.  519,  a\nConstitution Bench also spoke of reasonable restrictions when  it  comes  to\nprocedure.  It said:</span>\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_30\">&quot;While the reasonableness of the restrictions  has  to  be  considered  with\nregard to the exercise of the right, it does not  necessarily  exclude  from\nthe consideration of  the  Court  the  question  of  reasonableness  of  the\nprocedural part of the law. It is obvious that if the  law  prescribes  five\nyears externment or ten years externment, the question whether  such  period\nof externment is reasonable, being the substantive part, is necessarily  for\nthe consideration of the court under  clause  (5).  Similarly,  if  the  law\nprovides the procedure  under  which  the  exercise  of  the  right  may  be\nrestricted, the same is also for the consideration of the Court, as  it  has\nto determine if the exercise of the right has been  reasonably  restricted.&quot;\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_37\">(at page 524)\n\n\n\n\n\n</p><p data-structure=\"RespArg\" id=\"p_38\">27.   It was argued by the  learned  Additional  Solicitor  General  that  a\nrelaxed standard of reasonableness of restriction should apply regard  being\nhad to the fact that the medium of speech being the  internet  differs  from\nother mediums on several grounds.  To appreciate the width and scope of  his\nsubmissions, we are setting out his written submission verbatim:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_31\">&quot;(i)  the reach of print media is restricted to one state  or  at  the  most\none country while internet has no boundaries and its reach is global;\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_32\">(ii)  the recipient of the free speech and expression used in a print  media\ncan only be literate persons while internet can be accessed by literate  and\nilliterate both since one click is needed to download an objectionable  post\nor a video;\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_33\">(iii) In case of televisions serials [except live shows] and  movies,  there\nis a permitted pre- censorship&#x27;  which  ensures  right  of  viewers  not  to\nreceive any information which is dangerous to or not in conformity with  the\nsocial interest. While in the case of an internet,  no  such  pre-censorship\nis possible and each individual is publisher,  printer,  producer,  director\nand broadcaster of the content without any statutory regulation;\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_39\">In case of print media or medium of television and films whatever  is  truly\nrecorded can only be published or broadcasted I televised  I  viewed.  While\nin case of an internet, morphing of images, change of voices and many  other\ntechnologically advance methods to create serious potential social  disorder\ncan be applied.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"PetArg\" id=\"p_40\"> By the medium of internet, rumors having a serious potential of creating  a\nserious  social disorder can be spread to trillions of  people  without  any\ncheck which is not possible in case of other mediums.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_41\"> In case of mediums like print media, television and films,  it  is  broadly\nnot possible to invade privacy of unwilling persons. While  in  case  of  an\ninternet, it is very easy to invade upon the privacy of any  individual  and\nthereby violating his right under <a href=\"/doc/1199182/\" id=\"a_70\">Article 21</a> of the Constitution of India.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_42\"> By its very nature, in the mediums like newspaper, magazine, television  or\na movie, it is not possible to sexually harass someone, outrage the  modesty\nof anyone, use unacceptable filthy language and evoke communal frenzy  which\nwould lead to serious social disorder. While in the case of an internet,  it\nis easily possible to do so  by  a  mere  click  of  a  button  without  any\ngeographical  limitations and almost in all cases while ensuring   anonymity\nof the offender.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_43\"> By the very nature of the medium,  the  width  and  reach  of  internet  is\nmanifold as against newspaper and  films.  The  said  mediums  have  inbuilt\nlimitations i.e. a person will have to buy / borrow a  newspaper  and  /  or\nwill have to go to a theater to watch  a  movie.  For  television  also  one\nneeds at least a room where a television is placed and can only watch  those\nchannels which he has subscribed and that too only at a  time  where  it  is\nbeing telecast.
