We  have  seen\nhow under <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_347\">Section 69A</a> blocking can take  place  only  by  a  reasoned  order\nafter complying with several procedural safeguards including  a  hearing  to\nthe originator and intermediary.  We have also seen how there are  only  two\nways in which a blocking order  can  be  passed  -  one  by  the  Designated\nOfficer after complying with the 2009 Rules and the other by the  Designated\nOfficer when he has to follow an order passed  by  a  competent  court.  The\nintermediary applying its own mind to whether information should  or  should\nnot be blocked is noticeably absent in <a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_348\">Section 69A</a> read with 2009 Rules.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_190\">117.  <a href=\"/doc/161852741/\" id=\"a_349\">Section 79(3)(b)</a> has to be read down to  mean  that  the  intermediary\nupon receiving actual knowledge that a court order has  been  passed  asking\nit to expeditiously remove or disable access to certain material  must  then\nfail to expeditiously remove or disable access to that  material.   This  is\nfor the reason that otherwise it would be very difficult for  intermediaries\nlike Google, Facebook etc. to act when millions of  requests  are  made  and\nthe intermediary is  then  to  judge  as  to  which  of  such  requests  are\nlegitimate and  which  are  not.   We  have  been  informed  that  in  other\ncountries worldwide this view has gained acceptance, Argentina being in  the\nforefront. Also, the Court order and/or the notification by the  appropriate\nGovernment or its agency must strictly conform to the subject  matters  <a href=\"/doc/1057797/\" id=\"a_350\">laid\ndown in</a> <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_351\">Article 19(2).</a>  Unlawful acts beyond what is <a href=\"/doc/1057797/\" id=\"a_352\">laid  down  in</a>  <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_353\">Article\n19(2)</a> obviously cannot  form  any  part  of  <a href=\"/doc/844026/\" id=\"a_354\">Section  79</a>.   With  these  two\ncaveats, we refrain from striking down <a href=\"/doc/161852741/\" id=\"a_355\">Section 79(3)(b)</a>.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_191\">118.  The learned Additional Solicitor General informed  us  that  it  is  a\ncommon  practice  worldwide  for  intermediaries  to  have  user  agreements\ncontaining what is stated in Rule 3(2).   However, Rule  3(4)  needs  to  be\nread down in the same manner as <a href=\"/doc/161852741/\" id=\"a_356\">Section 79(3)(b)</a>.  The knowledge  spoken  of\nin the said sub-rule must only be through  the  medium  of  a  court  order.\nSubject to this,  the  Information  Technology  (Intermediaries  Guidelines)\nRules, 2011 are valid.\n</p>\n\n\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_192\">119.   In conclusion, we may summarise what has been held by us above:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_145\"><a href=\"/doc/170483278/\" id=\"a_357\">Section 66A</a> of the Information Technology Act, 2000 is struck  down  in  its\nentirety being violative of <a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_358\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> and  not  saved  under  <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_359\">Article\n19(2).</a>\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_146\"><a href=\"/doc/10190353/\" id=\"a_360\">Section 69A</a> and the  Information  Technology  (Procedure  &amp;  Safeguards  for\nBlocking  for  Access   of   Information   by   Public)   Rules   2009   are\nconstitutionally valid.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_147\"><a href=\"/doc/844026/\" id=\"a_361\">Section 79</a> is valid subject to <a href=\"/doc/161852741/\" id=\"a_362\">Section 79(3)(b)</a>  being  read  down  to  mean\nthat an intermediary upon receiving actual knowledge from a court  order  or\non being notified by the appropriate government or its agency that  unlawful\nacts relatable to <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_363\">Article 19(2)</a> are going to  be  committed  then  fails  to\nexpeditiously remove or disable access to  such  material.   Similarly,  the\nInformation Technology  &quot;Intermediary  Guidelines&quot;  Rules,  2011  are  valid\nsubject to Rule 3 sub-rule (4)  being  read  down  in  the  same  manner  as\nindicated in the judgment.\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_148\"><a href=\"/doc/89023557/\" id=\"a_364\">Section 118(d)</a> of the Kerala Police Act is struck down  being  violative  of\n<a href=\"/doc/1378441/\" id=\"a_365\">Article 19(1)(a)</a> and not saved by <a href=\"/doc/493243/\" id=\"a_366\">Article 19(2).</a>\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_149\">All the writ petitions are disposed in the above terms.\n</blockquote>\n\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_150\">............................................J.\n                                  (J. Chelameswar)\n\n\n\n............................................J.\n                                  (R.F. Nariman)\nNew Delhi,\nMarch 24, 2015.\n</blockquote>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_151\">-----------------------\n</blockquote><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_63\">[1]</span>\n\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_152\">      [2]The genealogy of  this  Section  may  be  traced  back  to  Section\n10(2)(a) of the U.K. Post Office (Amendment) Act, 1935,  which  made  it  an\noffence to send any message by telephone which is grossly  offensive  or  of\nan  indecent,  obscene,   or   menacing   character.    This   Section   was\nsubstantially reproduced by Section 66 of the UK Post Office  Act,  1953  as\nfollows:\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_153\">      66. Prohibition of sending offensive or false  telephone  messages  or\nfalse telegrams, etc.\n      If any person-\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_193\">      (a)sends any message by telephone which is grossly offensive or of  an\nindecent, obscene or menacing character ;\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_194\">      (b)sends any message by telephone, or any telegram, which he knows  to\nbe false, for the purpose of causing annoyance,  inconvenience  or  needless\nanxiety to any other person ; or\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_195\">      (c)persistently makes telephone calls  without  reasonable  cause  and\nfor any such purpose as aforesaid,\n      he shall be liable on summary conviction to a fine not  exceeding  ten\npounds, or to imprisonment for a term not exceeding one month, or to both.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_196\">      This Section in turn  was  replaced  by  Section  49  of  the  British\nTelecommunication Act, 1981 and Section 43 of the British  Telecommunication\nAct, 1984.  In its present form  in  the  UK,  it  is  Section  127  of  the\nTelecommunication Act, 2003 which is relevant and which is as follows:-\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_154\">      127.  Improper use of public electronic communications network\n      A person is guilty of an offence if he -\n</blockquote><p data-structure=\"Section\" id=\"p_197\">      sends by  means  of  a  public  electronic  communications  network  a\nmessage or other matter  that  is  grossly  offensive  or  of  an  indecent,\nobscene or menacing character; or\n      cause any such message or matter to be so sent.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_198\">      A person is guilty of an  offence  if,  for  the  purpose  of  causing\nannoyance, inconvenience or needless anxiety to another, he-\n</p><p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_199\">      sends by means  of  a  public  electronic  communications  network,  a\nmessage that he knows to be false,\n      causes such a message to be sent; or\n      persistently makes use of a public electronic communications  network.\n</p>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_200\">      A person guilty of an offence under this section shall be  liable,  on\nsummary conviction, to imprisonment for a term not exceeding six  months  or\nto a fine not exceeding level 5 on the standard scale, or to both.\n</p><p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_201\">      Subsections (1) and (2) do not apply to anything done  in  the  course\nof providing a programme service (within the  meaning  of  the  Broadcasting\nAct 1990 (c.42)).\n</p>\n\n\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_64\">[3]</span>\n<p data-structure=\"CDiscource\" id=\"p_202\">      [4] Incidentally, the Ark of the Covenant is perhaps the single most\nimportant focal point in Judaism.  The original ten commandments which the\nLord himself gave to Moses was housed in a wooden chest which was gold\nplated and called the Ark of the Covenant and carried by the Jews from\nplace to place until it found its final repose in the first temple - that\nis the temple built by Solomon.\n</p>\n<span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_65\">[5]</span>\n<p data-structure=\"Conclusion\" id=\"p_203\">      [6] A good example of the difference between advocacy  and  incitement\nis Mark Antony&#x27;s speech in Shakespeare&#x27;s  immortal  classic  Julius  Caesar.\nMark Antony begins  cautiously.  Brutus  is  chastised  for  calling  Julius\nCaesar ambitious and is repeatedly said to be an &quot;honourable man&quot;.  He  then\nshows the crowd Caesar&#x27;s mantle and describes who struck  Caesar  where.  It\nis at this point, after the interjection of two  citizens  from  the  crowd,\nthat Antony says-\n</p><blockquote id=\"blockquote_155\">      &quot;ANTONY- Good friends, sweet friends, let me not stir you up\n      To such a sudden flood of mutiny.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_156\">      They that have done this deed are honourable:\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_157\">      What private griefs they have, alas, I know not,\n      That made them do it: they are wise and honourable,\n      And will, no doubt, with reasons answer you.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_158\">      I come not, friends, to steal away your hearts:\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_159\">      I am no orator, as Brutus is;\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_160\">      But, as you know me all, a plain blunt man,\n      That love my friend; and that they know full well\n      That gave me public leave to speak of him:\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_161\">      For I have neither wit, nor words, nor worth,\n      Action, nor utterance, nor the power of speech,\n      To stir men&#x27;s blood: I only speak right on;\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_162\">      I tell you that which you yourselves do know;\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_163\">      Show you sweet Caesar&#x27;s wounds, poor poor dumb mouths,\n      And bid them speak for me: but were I Brutus,\n      And Brutus Antony, there were an Antony\n      Would ruffle up your spirits and put a tongue\n      In every wound of Caesar that should move\n      The stones of Rome to rise and mutiny.\n</blockquote><blockquote id=\"blockquote_164\">      ALL- We&#x27;ll mutiny.&quot;\n</blockquote><span class=\"hidden_text\" id=\"span_66\">[7]</span>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_204\">      [8] In its present form the clear and present  danger  test  has  been\nreformulated to say that:\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_165\">      &quot;The constitutional guarantees of free speech and free  press  do  not\npermit a State to forbid or proscribe advocacy of the use  of  force  or  of\nlaw violation  except  where  such  advocacy  is  directed  to  inciting  or\nproducing imminent lawless action and is likely to incite  or  produce  such\naction.&quot;\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_205\">       Interestingly,  the  US  Courts  have  gone  on  to  make  a  further\nrefinement. The State may ban what is called  a &quot;true threat&quot;.\n</p>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_166\">      &quot;&#x27;True threats&#x27; encompass those statements where the speaker means  to\ncommunicate a serious expression of an intent to commit an act  of  unlawful\nviolence to a particular individual or group of individuals.&quot;\n</blockquote>\n<blockquote id=\"blockquote_167\">      &quot;The speaker need  not  actually  intend  to  carry  out  the  threat.\nRather, a prohibition on true threats protects individuals from the fear  of\nviolence and from  the  disruption  that  fear  engenders,  in  addition  to\nprotecting people from the possibility that  the  threatened  violence  will\noccur.  Intimidation in the constitutionally proscribable sense of the  word\nis a type of true threat, where a speaker directs a threat to  a  person  or\ngroup of persons with the intent of placing the victim  in  fear  of  bodily\nharm or death.&quot;\n</blockquote>\n<p data-structure=\"Precedent\" id=\"p_206\">      See Virginia v. Black (Supra) and Watts v. United  States  22  L.  Ed.\n2d. 664 at 667\n\n\n\n\n</p>",
